So if you have multiple ALBs located in multiple regions, you can simply create one endpoint to access all of your load balancers. Amazon CloudFront is a content delivery network service that quickly delivers static content and video streams to your clients. A content delivery network or CDN is a globally distributed network of service spread around the globe that stores or caches your files. It shortens the time to deliver your data, which improves the response time of your application. So instead of fetching the data from one origin server that is hosted in a single location, your clients can retrieve your content from multiple edge locations located near them. You can set up a CloudFront distribution to cache your images, videos, media files, and software downloads of your websites. AWS PrivateLink is a service that allows private connectivity to your AWS services without passing through the public internet. It provides a private endpoint that you can use for your VPCs, EC2 instances, S3 buckets, and other resources. This private endpoint is also called a VPC endpoint. If you send your files over an unsecured network, your data is susceptible to various security threats like packet sniffing. Hackers can easily intercept your data as it travels through the routers and networks of the worldwide web. With AWS PrivateLink, your data stays within the Amazon network and does not traverse the unsecured lines of the internet. AWS Virtual Private Network or AWS VPN is a service that enables you to securely connect your on-premises network to AWS. This is basically just a regular VPN, which is an encrypted connection that passes through the public internet. It uses the IPsec protocol to authenticate and encrypt your data in transit. It's comprised of two services, AWS site-to-site VPN and AWS client VPN. The site-to-site VPN creates encrypted tunnels between your network and your Amazon VPCs or AWS transit gateways. On the other hand, AWS client VPN is simply a software that allows your users to connect to AWS or to your on-premises resources. Both of these types have a corresponding endpoint to your VPC. You can create a site-to-site VPN endpoint or a client VPN endpoint. AWS Direct Connect is a cloud service solution that allows you to establish a dedicated network connection from your on-premises network to AWS. Direct Connect provides a more consistent network experience over internet-based connections such as a VPN. You can create a private virtual interface to enable your on-premises servers to connect to the virtual private gateway of your Amazon VPC. You can even group your virtual private gateways and private virtual interfaces using a direct connect gateway. You can also use a public virtual interface to connect to your Amazon S3 buckets and other public resources in AWS. Unlike a VPN, the data being sent over a direct connect connection does not pass through the public internet. AWS Transit Gateway is a service that connects your Amazon VPCs, VPNs, direct connect gateways, and on-premises networks to a single gateway. This is recommended for large organizations with hundreds of Amazon VPCs, site-to-site VPNs, and external networks. It reduces the complexity of your infrastructure and empowers you to scale easily. Amazon API Gateway is a fully managed service that allows you to publish, maintain, monitor, and secure your RESTful APIs. It also supports WebSockets if you need real-time message communication to your clients. It acts as a front door for your back-end services that are running on Lambda, Amazon EC2, Amazon ECS, AWS Elastic Beanstalk, or any web application. You can think of Amazon API Gateway as a web proxy service like APIGEE, Mulesoft, and other proxies. AWS App Mesh is a service mesh that provides application-level networking for containerized applications. It allows your services to easily communicate with each other across multiple types of computing infrastructure. Under the hood, App Mesh is using an open-source service mesh proxy called Envoy. This proxy is deployed alongside your Microsoft containers. App Mesh standardize how your services communicate and provide you with end-to-end network visibility for your application. This can be used with microservices containers managed by Amazon ECS, Amazon EKS, AWS Fargate, Kubernetes running on AWS, and services running on Amazon EC2. AWS Cloud Map is a cloud resource discovery service. It is commonly used in microservices and containerized applications that have dynamically changing resources. Cloud Map enables you to name your application resources with custom names. By doing so, your application can always discover the most up-to-date locations of your resources, and consequently, improve the availability of your system.

AWS management and governance services
- [Instructor] Every company has its own standard operating procedures and policies that need to be enforced to its infrastructure to ensure efficient operations and comply with industry regulations. For example, healthcare organizations in the US must abide by the privacy and security standards of the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act of 1996 or commonly known as HIPAA. Other companies also follow the data protection and privacy regulations covered in the General Data Protection Regulation or GDPR which addresses the transfer of personal data outside the European Union and other European economic area. Failure to conform to these policies entails millions of dollars in fines and potential loss of revenue. Implementing strict security standards and procedures within your organization could also safeguard your business from unnecessary system failures and data leaks. AWS provides various management and governance services to provision, manage, govern, and operate your cloud environments more effectively. The AWS Management Console, AWS Command Line Interface, AWS Console Mobile Application, AWS Resource Access Manager, and AWS Systems Manager can be used to manage your cloud resources. To govern your entire cloud IT infrastructure, you can use AWS Config, AWS Organizations, AWS Service Catalog, AWS Control Tower, and other services to enforce operational standards across all your AWS resources and accounts to help you comply with your corporate IT policies. In this lecture, we'll discuss the management and governance services in AWS. Let's start off with the tools that you can use to manage and control your AWS resources. These tools can be in a form of a web-based console, a command-line interface via your terminal or command prompt, or a mobile application. Let's start with the AWS Management Console. This is basically a web interface for AWS that you can access on your web browser like Google Chrome, Mozilla Firefox, Safari, and others. You can securely log in to this web console using your IAM username and password. For added security, you can enable the Multi-Factor Authentication feature or MFA where you'll be prompted to enter an authentication code that is generated by your MFA device before you can log in. You can access the AWS Management Console by going to the console.aws.amazon.com link. AWS Command Line Interface, or AWS CLI for short, is simply a command-line interface that enables you to interact with your AWS services. If you're using a MacBook or a Linux OS, you can use your Terminal program to run the AWS CLI. If you're using Windows, you can use your command prompt or the Windows PowerShell. You can also develop shell scripts that invokes different AWS CLI commands to manage your resources. The AWS Console Mobile Application is the official mobile app provided by Amazon Web Services that allows you to monitor your resources through a dedicated dashboard. You can also view the configuration details, metrics, and alarms of select AWS services. A dashboard provides an overview of the account status, real-time CloudWatch metrics, your Personal Health Dashboard, and AWS Billing. Since this is just a mobile app, there are certain limitations on what you can do with it compared to the Amazon Management Console or the AWS CLI. AWS Systems Manager or SSM is a suite of services that provides you visibility and control of your cloud and on-premises infrastructure. SSM has several features that you can leverage on, such as Session Manager, State Manager, Patch Manager, Automation, Maintenance Window, Run Command, Parameter Store, and many other submodules. AWS System Manager also has an SSM agent just like a CloudWatch agent or a DataSync agent. Through this SSM agent, the System Manager service can manage both Amazon EC2 instances and your servers running on premises. The Patch Manager automates the process of patching the operating system of your EC2 instances and on-premises servers using a predefined or custom patch baseline. You can set a scheduled maintenance window to execute the patching activities to reduce any operational impact. With the State Manager, you can control the configuration details or the state of your resources, such as server configurations, virtualized hardware, and firewall settings. You can even associate Ansible playbooks, Chef recipes, PowerShell modules, and other SSM Documents to your resources. The Systems Manager Parameter Store provides centralized storage and management of your parameters, such as passwords, database strings, Amazon Machine Image IDs, license codes, environment variables, and other sensitive parameters. You can store the parameter as a secure string data type to instruct SSM to automatically encrypt it using a customer master key or CMK in AWS KMS. AWS Resource Access Manager is a service that enables you to easily and securely share your AWS resources with any AWS account or within your AWS organization. You can share AWS Transit Gateways, Subnets, AWS License Manager configurations, Route 53 Resolver rules, and other resources. AWS RAM eliminates the need to create duplicate resources in multiple accounts, reducing the operational overhead of managing those resources in every single account you own. Let's now move onto the governance services in AWS, namely AWS Config, AWS Organizations, AWS Service Catalog, and AWS Control Tower. AWS Config is a service that enables you to assess, audit, and evaluate the configurations of your AWS resources. It automates the compliance assessment of your internal policies and regulatory standards by giving you the visibility on the existing configurations of your various AWS services and third-party resources. AWS Config enables you to identify the changes made to a specific resource over time. It continuously assess changes in your resource configuration and compares them against your specified criteria. You can create rules that detect EC2 instance running on an unapproved AMI, publicly accessible S3 buckets, non-compliant services, and many more. The evaluation could either be triggered periodically or by an actual configuration change of your AWS resource. You can integrate AWS Config with CloudWatch Events and AWS Lambda to keep you updated for any resource changes in near real time and to execute custom actions. Remediating non-compliant AWS resources can be automated by associating an AWS Config rule with an AWS Systems Manager automation document. The AWS Organizations service enables you to consolidate and centrally manage multiple AWS accounts. It provides consolidated billing, access control, compliance and security, as well as the ability to share resources across your AWS accounts. Multiple AWS accounts with separate billing can be combined using a feature called Consolidated Billing. This allows a customer to take advantage of volume discounts with minimal impact to AWS resources. You can use Service Control Policies or SCPs to ensure that only the authorized users can execute actions that meet your policy requirements. Central Logging can also be implemented to monitor all activities performed across your organization using AWS CloudTrail. You can also aggregate data from all your AWS Config rules to quickly audit your environment for compliance. AWS Service Catalog empowers you to set up and centrally manage catalogs of approved IT services that you specify on AWS. You can manage various IT services, referred to as products in Service Catalog, then group them in a portfolio. In AWS Service Catalog, a product could be a machine image, application server, program, tool, database, or other services that you use for your cloud architecture. AWS Service Catalog assists you in meeting your compliance requirements and enforce granular access control that allows the deployment of only approved IT services through AWS cloud. AWS Control Tower is a service that helps you set up and govern a secure multi-account AWS environment. It automates a setup of your multi-account architecture with just a few clicks. The setup uses blueprints that follows AWS best practices for security and management. Control Tower provides mandatory high-level rules called guardrails that helps enforce your policies using service control policies or SCPs, or detect policy violations using AWS Config rules.

AWS machine learning services
- [Instructor] Machine learning is a branch of artificial intelligence. It's all about imitating how humans learn. Just like humans, a machine can learn by watching, hearing, reading, and analyzing the things in its environment. This machine can be in the form of a simple computer program, an API, or an entire enterprise system. Once a machine consumes enough information, it can provide a range of helpful information and useful results that can save you time, effort, and even money. Some people may find this topic to be quite difficult to understand, especially with all the complex math and algorithms it entails. To make it simple, let's start with a couple of real world machine learning applications that you might be using already. Most of us have a subscription to online streaming services like Netflix, Amazon Prime Video, Disney Plus, or HBO. In these streaming services, you might notice that they can recommend a particular movie or TV series based on shows that you've watched in the past. The recommendation engine will analyze your viewing history and predict the genre or type of shows you like. So if you're watching a lot of horror movies, it'll be presented with different horror shows since this genre that you're always watching. Let's look at another use case. Do you know that if you take a photo on your iPhone, it'll automatically detect any text in the image? In this way, you can easily copy text and not manually type them on your phone. You can even try it right now. Take a picture of something that has a label, a word, or a number on it. Open that picture, then double tap any text present in the image, and you'll be able to copy them right away. This iPhone feature is called Live Text, which is a form of optical character recognition that uses machine learning to transcribe text from images in your photo library. Here's another example. If you're living in the US, you might have shop at Amazon Go, which is a convenience store with no checkout. Amazon Go uses computer vision and machine learning to automatically track all the products you grab from the store and build you online without having to wait in line to check out your goods. This computer vision technology is also what powers self-driving cars, visual recognition systems, and many others. There's no doubt that we're already using machine learning in almost every moment of our lives. In this lesson, we'll learn about the various machine learning services that are available in Amazon Web Services. The primary machine learning platform in AWS is called Amazon SageMaker, which is followed by a lot of other ML services. Some services in AWS have machine learning features as well, like Amazon Aurora Machine Learning, Redshift ML, Deep Learning AMIs and so much more. Take note that we won't cover them here, as you'll only focus on a dedicated ML services that are relevant to your upcoming AWS exam. It's easy to get overwhelmed by the sheer number of services in AWS, so we'll divide the lecture into several sections. The AWS Machine learning services can be classified into these use cases: computer vision, language AI, automated data extraction and analysis, customer experience improvement, business metrics, and DevOps. For computer vision, we have Amazon Rekognition, Amazon Lookout for Vision, and AWS Panorama. For automated data extraction and analysis, AWS has Amazon Textract, Amazon Augmented AI, Amazon Comprehend. For language AI, we have Amazon Lex, Amazon Transcribe, Amazon Polly. For customer experience improvement, you can use the following services, Amazon Kendra, Amazon Personalize, Amazon Translate. For business metrics, you can use Amazon Forecast, Amazon Fraud Detector, Amazon Lookout for Metrics. And lastly, for DevOps to have Amazon DevOps Guru, Amazon CodeGuru Reviewer, Amazon Guru Profiler, and Amazon CodeWhisperer. Let's discuss each of these services one by one. First off, let's talk about Amazon SageMaker. Think of this as a full touch machine learning platform in AWS with tons of services, features and components. Amazon SageMaker is not just a simple ML service, but a fully managed cloud platform with lots of modules that you can use. With this, you can build, train, and deploy ML models for any use case with fully managed infrastructure, tools and workflows. SageMaker removes the manual task from each step of the ML process to make it easier for you to develop high quality models. This platform has so many modules that you can choose from, namely Amazon SageMaker Canvas, SageMaker Studio Lab, SageMaker Data Wrangler, SageMaker Autopilot, SageMaker Jumpstart, SageMaker Clarify, and so much more. Let's now discuss the different ML services related to computer vision. Amazon Rekognition provides pre-trained and customizable computer vision capabilities to extract information and insights from your images and videos. Just as its name implies, it can recognize certain objects, faces, texts, scenes, labels, and other attributes from your media files or streaming videos. This service is perfect for facial recognition where it can detect the face of a particular person or a well-known celebrity. It can also determine if someone is wearing a piece of personal protective equipment like a mask, a helmet, or gloves. If you upload an image of you holding a guitar while sitting on your sofa, Amazon Rekognition can detect your face, your guitar, and the sofa where you sit. It also has a feature called Amazon Rekognition Custom Labels. With this, you can easily build a machine learning model to classify custom components or products from your dataset. Amazon Lookout is a suite of services that is comprised of Amazon Lookout for Equipment, Amazon Lookout for Metrics, and Amazon Lookout for Vision. The last one uses computer vision to detect defects on industrial products at scale. Amazon Lookout for Vision is primarily used in factories and manufacturing lines to quickly and accurately identify defects in each product. The dataset can be in the form of product images that are stored in an Amazon S3 bucket. You can provide a couple of baseline images to Amazon Lookup for Vision containing defect-free products. And this service, you'll be able to automatically build a model for you within a few hours. From there, it can automatically detect anomalies in your product like dents, cracks and scratches. Next, let's talk about the services that you can use to automate text-based extraction and analysis. Let's start with the Amazon Textract service. The name of the service is a combination of the words text and extract. This will give you a hint that it's used to extract text from scanned documents, notes, and images. Amazon Textract is a service that uses optical character recognition to automatically extract texts from scanned files like PDFs or documents, handwritten notes, receipts, passport, IDs, and many others. What this service great is its capability to generate the results into a table form or a CSV file. It also has a query feature that allows you to extract a particular field using natural language questions, so if you upload your driver's license to Amazon Textract, you can submit a query like what's the first name or what's the driver's ID, and you'll get the value you ask for. You can also bash upload your documents to S3 and automated text analysis process. Amazon Augmented AI or A2I is a service that provides human review workflows for common machine learning use cases. A human review literally means that a human being will review a certain output that your machine learning model generated before it can proceed to the next step of the workflow. The service augments your AI to ensure the accuracy of prediction results and helps provide continuous improvements to your machine learning model. You can directly integrate your workflows from Amazon Rekognition or Amazon Textract to Amazon Augmented AI. For example, you can do a human review of the key-value pairs that are extracted by Amazon Textract, or implement image moderation by doing a human review of unsafe content such as explicit adult or violate content from Amazon Rekognition. 

Serverless computing overview
- [Lecturer] In this lecture, we'll talk about the concept of serverless computing and its various forms and benefits. We'll also discuss its history, advantages, disadvantages, and other factors that you have to consider if you're planning to build a serverless architecture for your workloads. Let's start off by defining what this technology is all about. We have the first know the answer to the question, what is serverless computing? Basically, serverless computing means an on-demand service with less server management. Take note of the two key phrases of this statement, on-demand service and less server management. The first one means that the compute capacity is provisioned and run once a request has been made and where you only pay for the actual computing time that you have consumed. It only runs on-demand and stops running after the processing has been completed to save on costs. Therefore, it provides some massive cost benefit since you only pay for the actual service usage, excluding the idle time where your resources are not used at all. Serverless computing is also highly scalable and can quickly increase its compute capacity in a matter of milliseconds. It can process hundreds or even thousands of requests concurrently without any manual intervention or scaling configuration in place. A serverless solution does not mean just literally running out of thin air with absolutely no physical server at all. That is a wrong concept behind serverless computing since you must have a CPU, RAM, a network interface card, and other physical server devices to process data. Again, serverless simply means that a particular service requires less server management, meaning a cloud service provider like AWS handles all of the manual server management tasks for you. This allows you to focus on implementing your core business logic instead of just wasting your time doing time-consuming server deployment and maintenance tasks that don't add much value to your business. You don't need to launch your own virtual machine like Amazon EC2, apply OS patches on a regular basis, upgrade the server capacity if the incoming load increases, or manage the available disk space of your server. Serverless computing has different forms that you can use for your various workloads. Its most popular form is FaaS or function as a service, which runs our code or function only when needed and scales its compute capacity automatically. An example of FaaS is AWS Lambda. There's also a serverless compute engine for containers such as AWS Fargate. You can use Amazon Aurora Serverless, Amazon DynamoDB, and Amazon S3 as serverless data store. Essentially, a serverless service is powered by lightweight micro-virtual machines or microVMs. These microVMs combine the capabilities of a regular virtual machine with the efficiency of containers. Since it's a smaller version of a VM, it can be instantiated way faster than a full fledged virtual machine, but with certain limitations. A serverless service can be in a form of a function, which is just a regular code that does a specific task. This is a concept that favors the use of a function to run independently without relying on a larger application. For example, you can develop a function that fetches data from a public API, and then store the results in an Amazon S3 bucket. The act of fetching data from a public API and storing data to an S3 bucket are the capabilities of that function. This is applicable in edge computing too. You can deploy a function on edge applications that are in near proximity to your users to further speed up the processing time of your data. Remember that the function is lightweight, so it can be deployed easier than the virtual machine. You can deploy your custom functions using Lambda@Edge, CloudFront Functions, and others. To better understand this concept, let's look at the differences between a common cloud architecture with virtual machines in a serverless architecture that is composed of serverless computes and database resources. We will start on how these two architectures launch and manage their acquired computing resources that power their applications and other components. In a traditional system, you have to buy a physical rack server first and set it up on-premises before you can start installing and running your applications. You are responsible for every single server maintenance task on your on-premises network, including the physical facilities of your servers and IT assets. For server-based cloud architectures, the virtual servers can be launched on-demand with just a click of a button, and you don't have to physically buy or set up the rack servers yourself. The physical host service setup is done by the cloud service provider like Amazon Web Services, and this physical server instantiates or creates hundreds or thousands of virtual servers which can run your applications. These virtual servers have their own guest operating system storage capacity, and other features that you can manually access and configure. The responsibility of managing the server is split by you and your cloud service provider. For example, AWS is responsible for patching the host operating system of your underlying physical server, whereas the patching of the guest operating system of your virtual server falls on you. If you're using an Amazon EC2 instance, it is your task to choose the right instance size for your workloads, to monitor the CPU, RAM, and the available disk space of your instances, setting up an auto-scaling group with the correct scaling policies, and troubleshoot any other server issues. This is also called server full architecture since the system is full of server maintenance tasks, which is the total opposite of serverless computing. This underlying cloud infrastructure is also the same thing that the serverless service is using. The cloud service provider is still the one that manages the physical servers. However, you don't have the responsibility of launching, managing, monitoring, or scaling the underlying virtual servers that are used by your serverless resources. You don't launch, manage, or troubleshoot any Amazon EC2 here, and there's less server management involved. It is also important to note that the serverless service is not running all the time like an Amazon EC2 instance or an RDS database. This is actually the key difference between a serverless service and managed service in AWS. Once a new request is received by a serverless service, then that's the only time to launch the compute capacity for workloads to execute. Therefore, you'll only be charged for the duration of the request and not for idle time. For example, if invoked an AWS Lambda function, it'll internally set up the execution environment for the function and do the required code initialization. This entire process takes about 100 milliseconds to over one second, depending on your code. A Lambda function can run for about 15 minutes only and not continuously like a regular server. So if your function executes your code for a duration of 15 minutes just once for the entire month, then you'll only have to pay for that 15 minutes of execution time. You'll not be billed for the time that the function is idle. This is in contrast with an Amazon EC2 instance where you have to pay for the entire time that the instance is running, whether you use it or not. Because of its cost efficiency, using serverless services can significantly reduce your total operating costs. You can also launch a serverless database like Amazon DynamoDB or Aurora Serverless. These serverless data stores are scalable, cost-effective, and have no administrative overhead for their underlying servers. Using serverless databases can yield better results than a server-based Amazon RDS database where you have to pay the entire duration whenever the resource is running. And there you go. We have completed our discussion about serverless computing. Once again, this concept simply means that it's indeed an on-demand service with less server management, just as I mentioned earlier. It's a lightweight service that is highly scalable and extremely cost effective for your workloads. We will further explore serverless computing in AWS in our succeeding lectures.

Serverless architectures
- [Instructor] There are a lot of benefits of going serverless. The burden of managing a virtual machine is gone, including the regular task of patching its operating system. A serverless architecture means less server management and troubleshooting activities for you and your team. This technology enables you to focus on building your IT solutions and applications for your customers instead of wasting time doing server provisioning, patching, migration, and troubleshooting that don't add value to your organization. In this lecture, we'll talk about the different serverless services available in AWS and the vast types of cloud architectures that exclusively use serverless technology for your workloads. We will also look at how a traditional architecture works versus a full-fledged serverless solution in AWS. This will give you valuable insights as to why a lot of companies right now are re-architecting their entire infrastructure to adopt a more cost-effective serverless architecture. All right, let's start. AWS provides different serverless services that you can use for your various workloads. You can use AWS Lambda to run your code as functions or execute it on a regular schedule using Amazon EventBridge or CloudWatch events. You can orchestrate the flow of data to multiple Lambda functions using AWS Step Functions. This is also helpful for edge computing where you can process data in close proximity to your end users. For example, you can use Lambda@Edge or an Amazon CloudFront function to run Java, Python, JavaScript, Node.js or any other code in the Edge location where users are located instead of your actual origin server. Edge computing can significantly reduce the processing time of your applications since the data doesn't travel thousands of kilometers or miles just to process and fetch your results. Right off the bat, the users can immediately receive a response to the request. It also cuts down your data transfer costs since the number of requests that go in and out of your origin server has increased dramatically. For containers, you can use AWS Fargate as a serverless compute engine for your docker container or kubernetes spots instead of using EC2 virtual machines, which you still have to manage. You also don't have to spin up another virtual server just for your application integration services or other components. AWS has a collection of ready-to-use services at your disposal, such as Amazon EventBridge, AWS Step Functions, Amazon SQS, Amazon SNS, Amazon API Gateway, AWS AppSync, and many more. You can launch a serverless data store in AWS, so you don't have to manually manage your storage capacity. Your static data can be stored in Amazon S3, and the dynamic data can be processed in Amazon DynamoDB or Amazon Aurora serverless databases. AWS also allows you to launch a serverless data warehouse using Redshift Spectrum, which allows you to perform SQL queries on data stored in Amazon S3 buckets. Since there's no database server to manage, you can lessen your operating expenses and administrative costs. Another serverless service that you can use is AWS Glue, which is primarily used for extract, transform, and load processing or ETL. For analytical workloads, you can leverage the power of Amazon Athena, Amazon QuickSight, Amazon Kinesis Data Analytics, and other serverless analytic services in AWS. In the past, companies have to buy expensive physical servers, set them up on premises and apply custom configurations in order to run their applications. They also have to hire a team of systems and network administrators to maintain these bare metal servers and troubleshoot any issues that may occur along the way. The entire process of deploying a rack server, a blade server or other devices can take days or even weeks to complete. This entails a high administrative overhead and an exorbitant cost to your organization. Using this model requires you to properly estimate and allocate the server capacity in advance. Since you cannot dynamically add up or remove CPUs from these IT assets, it's just simply inconvenient, labor-intensive and downright expensive to manage your own servers. Cloud computing ushered a new era of on-demand virtual machines that you can use to launch your online solutions in a matter of minutes instead of days. The bare metal server runs the host operating system in the virtualization layer, which produces tens or hundreds of virtual machines. This pool of virtual machines is sharing the CPU cores, RAM, network bandwidth and local storage of a single physical server. So if a rack server running on your on-premises network has 40 CPU cores, then you can roughly launch 40 virtual machines with one CPU core each or so. Each of these virtual machines is running a guest operating system that you also have to patch regularly. Since a VM has its own OS, it also has its own kernel, which provides a secure boundary over the other VMs running in the host computer. There is a considerable amount of time necessary to launch these instances due to the different components that its hypervisor must virtualize and allocate. Moreover, you'll have to pay the cost of running your VMs, including its idle time where a resource isn't used at all. There is also a smaller virtualized entity than virtual machines called containers. A container shares the physical servers OSS system kernel and doesn't launch its own guest OS. Unlike a VM, it virtualizes the libraries dependencies of her applications instead of the whole operating system. Therefore, a container doesn't have its own kernel, unlike a VM, so it's not fully isolated to other containers running in the physical host. It's important to note that the container is primarily used for running applications only, which can also be moved to another host if necessary. This is the reason why it's faster to launch a Docker container or a Kubernetes pod compared with an Amazon EC2 Instance. Again, it is smaller than a virtual machine and doesn't run its own guest operating system. A container also works continuously, so you'll be billed for the total time that this container is running. Although a container provides a substantial performance improvement over VM, it's still has an operating cost that you have to cover. The burden of paying both it's active and idle time remains, even if no one is using your containerized application at all. This is especially true if you have workloads that only need to run once a month or infrequently. This is where serverless computing comes in. Serverless is essentially a combination of a VM and a container. It is powered by a micro virtual machine or a micro VM, which has its own kernel. Just like a container, it deploys faster than the virtual machine, and it can be used to run your applications. In contrast with VMs and containers, a serverless service doesn't run continuously at all. A serverless solution will only run once you invoked it and then release its computing capacity afterward. This is the reason why serverless architecture is cost effective since you won't have to pay for its idle time. Let's now discuss the different serverless architectures that you can implement in AWS. Just as I mentioned before, you can use different AWS serverless services for your computing, storage, database, orchestration, ETL, and many other workloads. These services can be used to re-architect your existing solutions at a much lower cost. If you want to design a static single page application with a custom domain name and can provide low latency access to your users globally, then you can use a combination of Amazon S3 and Amazon CloudFront. The static files can be stored in an Amazon S3 bucket, while Amazon CloudFront will distribute the content to your users around the world. You can also re-architect your legacy web application and turn it into serverless architecture. If you're using Amazon EC2 Instances in running your SOAP or Restful APIs, you can develop a serverless solution using AWS Lambda and API gateway that can process the API requests for a fraction of the cost. You can modernize your APIs by turning them into WebSockets API or GraphQL API. If you have a containerized application running in Amazon EC2, Amazon ECS or Amazon EKS, then it'll be better to use a serverless compute engine like AWS Fargate. This will lower down your costs in running your ECS task or EKS spots. AWS Lambda, API Gateway, AWS Fargate and other serverless competing services are recommended if your company does not have an administration team that can manage your EC2 Instances or clusters. Since these competing services are serverless, you wont to be burdened by routine server management tasks. You can also incorporate a serverless database in your architecture. If your web application has sporadic or infrequent database usage patterns, then you can use DynamoDB or Amazon Aurora Serverless. These serverless database services can be launched without choosing a particular instance type unlike an Amazon RDS database or a standard Amazon Aurora instance. A serverless architecture also eliminates the responsibility of manually managing the replication and scaling of your database resources as demand increases or decreases. It's like auto-scaling in Amazon RDS or Aurora, where it can automatically increase your storage capacity. However, RDS auto-scaling doesn't usually include the process of adding or removing the CP resources of your database. It commonly augments your database storage and not its compute capacity. You will still have to manually scale your database instance to a larger instance type to increase its CPU, and don't forget that it's difficult to downsize a large RDS instance to a smaller one. There's a way to directly upgrade a type of your RDS database, but not the other way around. If you want to change a large DB instance type to a small DB type, then you have to migrate your data manually. You have to export your data from the source database using a tool like MySQL Dump or any other backup utility, and then import it to a brand new database with a smaller instance type. Apparently, this entails a lot of database management tasks and it is prone to human error. Amazon DynamoDB, Amazon Aurora Serverless and other serverless databases simplify the process of adding or removing the compute capacity to or from the database tier as required. It improves the performance, scaling and durability of your DB with minimal effort from your IT operations team. Because of this massive performance and cost benefit, more companies are now adopting a serverless architecture each and every day. Organizations are starting to re-architect their cost-intensive legacy systems and move them to the cloud to cut down their expenses. Serverless solutions can provide scalability and cost efficiency in just a few clicks of a button without the hassle of managing any servers that power their entire infrastructure. Moreover, companies can also save money from redundant systems administrators, database administrators, and IT staff since their systems are completely serverless.

AWS Lambda overview
- [Instructor] Okay, so let's talk about AWS Lambda. AWS Lambda is a serverless computing service that allows you to run codes in the cloud without thinking of servers. Back in the day, if you want to host an application or run a scheduled task, like taking data backups, for example, you run those jobs on a managed server that you or your company own. You're in charge of monitoring the server, setting up the environment for your application, or making sure that you have the latest security patches installed in your server. This is not the case with AWS Lambda. Instead of worrying about provisioning servers, you just have to create a Lambda function and upload the code that you intend to run. AWS Lambda will do the heavy lifting of managing, securing, and scaling servers for you. As a developer, this is actually a good thing because now you'll have more time to spend on writing codes for your application. So less operational management means quicker development time. AWS Lambda is also a lot cheaper compared to other computing services like EC2 instance because you only pay for the compute time that you actually consume. You can write Lambda functions in any programming language that you prefer. However, there are programming languages that AWS Lambda natively supports. These are Java, Go, PowerShell, Node.js, C#, Python, and Ruby. If you're using a language that is not on this list, like PHP, for example, then you have to create a custom runtime to support PHP. A runtime is basically a language, a specific environment, which is responsible for interpreting and running your code. Unlike an EC2 instance, you don't get to choose the number of CPUs, disk I/O, and the network bandwidth that will power your Lambda function. Rather, Lambda gives you the option to choose the available memory that your function can use during runtime. Lambda will automatically allocate the CPU power, disk I/O, and the network bandwidth in proportion to the memory that you set. So the more memory you put, the more CPU you'll get. Of course, it goes without saying that the performance of your Lambda function increases as you allocate more memory. Okay, so this is pretty much what AWS Lambda is at the basic level. I hope that you get the foundational idea of why it is used and the advantages that it offers. In the next Lambda sections, we'll have a more deep dive discussions about the concepts that you're most likely to encounter in the CDA exam. Okay, so see you there.

AWS Lambda synchronous vs. asynchronous invocation
- [Instructor] A Lambda function runs when it is invoked via CLI, API, or whenever it detects events emitted by an EWS service. And events can be as simple as a file upload to Amazon S3, a request to API Gateway, or something like a periodic invocation through CloudWatch Events. There are two methods by which you can invoke lambda functions. The first one is the default method called synchronous invocation. Synchronous invocation is pretty straightforward. When a function is invoked synchronously, the Lambda function will process the event, wait until it's completed, then returns a response. To understand how it works, let's take a look at the scenario where you typically see synchronous invocation in action. Say you have a food ordering application that allows customers to retrieve information about their orders from a database. We can implement the application in a serverless way using API Gateway, AWS Lambda, and Amazon DynamoDB. The role of API Gateway here is to expose the API that your customer's devices can use to make a request to your backend service. Your backend service in this case is a Lambda function, and it is the one that actually talks to the DynamoDB table and fetches order information. Client requests are routed via API Gateway. And as a result of that request, API Gateway emits an event that invokes the Lambda function. Once the function is invoked, it'll start processing the requests. The Lambda function will make a query against this DynamoDB table and wait for it to finish. Once the order information has been retrieved, the Lambda function will return that information to API Gateway back to the clients. So the main idea here is when Lambda function is invoked synchronously, AWS Lambda runs the function and waits for a response. When the function is finished, it'll return a response with a 200 status code and the payload containing the order information. In most cases, synchronous invocation is used in operations that don't take too long to process. So that's synchronous invocation. Now let's go to a synchronous invocations. Asynchronous invocations are normally used for operations that don't need to wait and return a response from the function. Some examples of these are long latency operations, such as batch operations, video encoding, and order processing. Unlike synchronous invocation, asynchronous invocation uses an event queue to control which request will trigger Lambda function. This event queue is fully managed behind the scenes by AWS Lambda, and you don't have to do anything to make it work. It's already there once you invoke function asynchronously. Let's understand asynchronous invocation. We have here a food ordering application like the one in our example earlier. So when a user sends a post request to the putOrder API like the previous example, the request is routed first through API Gateway, which produces an event. This time, instead of directly responding to the event, Lambda will hand off the event into an internal event queue. In this case, it is up to AWS Lambda which request will trigger the Lambda function. If the event is queued successfully, AWS Lambda will immediately return a 202 status code as a response to the client. The response does not contain any payload. The 202 status code indicates that the request has been accepted for processing. The user won't have to wait for the Lambda function to finish. So on the client side, what you can do is create a loader or any way for a user to track the completion of his order. This way, you can provide a much better user experience.

AWS Lambda execution environment lifecycle
- [Instructor] When you invoke Lambda function, it undergoes three phases, the Init phase, the Invoke phase, and the Shutdown phase. The first stage is the Init phase. This phase occurs when a Lambda function is invoked for the first time or after a long period of inactivity. So what happens during this phase? During the Init phase, AWS Lambda creates an execution environment for your Lambda function inside a micro virtual machine. The amount of CPU and memory that will be allocated to the execution environment is determined by the memory settings that you've configured for your Lambda function. Then after that, AWS Lambda will download your Lambda function code from an Amazon S3 bucket. Just so you know, AWS Lambda stores code encrypted in an S3 bucket that AWS manages. What happens next is Lambda will run your initialization code. The initialization code is any code that is written outside of the handler function. So these may refer to the dependencies that you import, your global variables and objects and so on. These are all done to bootstrap or prepare the execution environments for running the handler function. The time it takes for the Init phase to complete adds latency to the total execution time of your function. And this latency is what we call a cold start. Take note that in most cases, you don't pay for the cold start, you just pay for the time that your handler function is actually running. However, there's an exception to this. The Init phase has a time limit. If it does not finish within the time limit, you will be charged for the initialization time, plus the time it took your handler function to process an event. So how will you reduce cold start? One way that AWS recommends is to minimize the dependencies that you import into your code. Make sure that you only import dependencies that are really required by your Lambda function in order to run. The idea is, the less code Lambda initializes, the shorter the time of your cold start. After the Init phase, Lambda enters the Invoke phase. This is the part where your handler function is executed. When the Lambda function has completed processing an event, AWS Lambda freezes the execution environment for a period of time in anticipation of serving subsequent invocations. This enables your Lambda function to accept new requests without the need to establish new execution environments. As a result, the execution time of your function will be much faster because Lambda won't need to go through everything that was done in the Init phase. So basically, you get to skip all of the bootstrap operations. In AWS Lambda, there's a concept of execution environment reuse. Some people may refer to it as context reuse, but they're essentially the same thing. Throughout this course, I might use them interchangeably. Hopefully, you don't get confused. Execution environment reuse is all about reusing the config settings, dependencies, global variables, or even database connections that were already initialized during the Init phase. Also, keep in mind that an execution environment has a 512 megabyte temporary storage that your function can use to cache a static data, and it is located at the tmp directory. This temporary storage is quite useful for storing images, JSON data, CSV files or text files that your function needs to process at every invocation. For example, imagine you have an application that invokes a Lambda function. The Lambda function retrieves and processes files from S3 based on the user's inputs. If you don't use the temporary storage, your Lambda function would have to download files every time it is invoked. If two users request the same file, your function will download it twice. This is inefficient and will add latency to the total function execution time. So to solve that, what we can do is to cache the files to the tmp directory. This way, if multiple users request the same file, instead of fetching it from an S3 bucket, through Lambda function, will be able to access it from its local storage. As a best practice, always take advantage of these initialized resources to reduce your function's execution time. Remember that you are billed for the time it takes for your function to run. So a shorter execution duration means a lower cost. So how can you take advantage of the execution environment reuse? Simple. Just place all the global variables, database connections, SDK clients and codes that caches data outside the handler function. For example, instead of writing the database connection inside the handler function, move it outside so that you won't have to initialize it every time the function is invoked. Just a word of caution, while reusing the execution environment improves Lambda function performance, you should not forget that Lambda functions are meant to be stateless. The execution context is for short term storage only, so you should avoid storing any state data in it. Instead, you can persist state data to other services like Amazon DynamoDB or Amazon ElastiCache. The final stage is the Shutdown phase. If the function's execution environment stops receiving invocations after some time, then Lambda will terminate the execution environment. AWS does not state the specific inactivity period before Lambda shuts down an environment. Based on my experience, environments are kept warm for around five to 10 minutes, but this can vary from time to time. Sometimes, functions are terminated earlier to free up resources for use by other Lambda functions. After this idle time, AWS Lambda will terminate your function's execution environment and all things that were initialized in your execution context are good as gone. Take note that redeploying your Lambda function will also terminate all existing environments of your function. If the function starts to receive invocations after some time, the function will start at the Init phase causing some latency to the execution duration again.

AWS Lambda event vs. context object
- [Instructor] Now let's talk about the event object in the context object in AWS Lambda. Whenever you run a Lambda function, it passes two objects to the handler function. An object in the concept of programming is simply a data type that can contain properties and methods that you can access. AWS Lambda provides prebuilt objects that you can use to create powerful and dynamic applications. The first one is the event object. The event object is a JSON-formatted data that contains information about the event that invoke your Lambda function. The event object is purely data and does not contain any methods. An event may come from an AWS service, like an SNS notification, for example, or data coming from an IOT sensor. It can be anything. Here is what an SNS notification event looks like. So, you can see here the full details on a particular SNS event, like its timestamp, signature, message ID, and more importantly, the message, which is what we're mostly concerned about. The structure of an event object depends on the event source. So, for example, how an SNS event was structured isn't the same as an Amazon S3 event. The structure of AWS service events is prebuilt. You can't modify them, so you're always going to have to refer to the documentation if you need to work on a certain service to see what pieces of information are available for you. Depending on what you need in the requirement of your application, you can play around with these data and use them as you see a fit. The second object is the context object. This object provides methods and properties that describe the current execution environments of your Lambda function. For example, it can give the name of your Lambda function, the version of the function, the identifier of the invocation request, the amount of memory that's allocated for the function, and many more. It also has a method that you can use, which is the get remaining time in Millis, and what it does is return the time left in milliseconds before the execution of your function times out.

AWS Lambda layers
- [Instructor] In the last section, we've seen how custom dependencies can be included in a function handler. And we did this by bundling our application code together with all of its dependencies in a single deployment package. This method gets the job done, but it has some drawbacks. First, deploying multiple Lambda functions that rely on the same dependencies is inefficient and would just make the total code size of your functions unnecessarily large. Second, updating those dependencies on each function would take time and can slow down the development process. Imagine having five Lambda functions that contain the same external libraries, but work on separate tasks. Sooner or later, you'd eventually have to update those libraries so you could provide your function with better security control, makes your code more performance, or so you can enjoy its latest features. But in order to do that, you need to create a package for each function and deploy them again to AWS Lambda. In this case, five Lambda functions are equivalent to five deployment packages. As you can already imagine, this involves a lot of work and can really be burdensome. Imagine having to deploy 50 or even hundreds of deployment packages. To solve this problem, we have to use Lambda Layers. Lambda Layers is a component in AWS Lambda that you can use to store additional codes like external dependencies, utility functions, or custom runtime separately from your deployment package. Like a deployment package, you need to zip up any external dependencies before uploading them to Lambda Layers. Instead of wrapping your function along with its dependencies in a deployment package, as an alternative, you can set apart those dependencies in Lambda Layers. Using Lambda Layers gives you the following advantages. Codes inside Lambda layers can be shared with all Lambda functions in a region, which in effect, reduces the overall size of your deployment packages. And since deployment packages are smaller, deployments also become faster. You get to keep your Lambda function code smaller, which allows you to focus more on the main logic of your function. And lastly, Lambda Layers give you a separation of concern. You can now deal with managing dependencies and building your application logic independently.

AWS Lambda versions and aliases
- [Instructor] In this video, we'll talk about Lambda versions and aliases. So right now, I'm on the AWS Lambda console, and I have this newly created Lambda function with the default Python code. Here, you'll notice two sections after the Configuration tab, which are the Aliases and Versions. First, let's talk about versions. If you're developing an application, it is unavoidable to have code revisions, whether you're adding new features to your application or just fixing bugs. Instead of directly making changes in the production version of your Lambda function, you can isolate a test version of it where you can play around and test whatever you want with the code without affecting the prod. Another useful case of publishing a new version is for beta testing. That is, if you're planning to release a new feature and you want to see and test how the new version will perform under real conditions when users start interacting with it. This is particularly helpful in canary testing where you gradually introduce the new version to your users. So let me show you real quick how to publish a new version. Click on the Versions tab and hit Publish new version. Okay, so it says here, "Publish new version from LATEST". So what does this mean? If you haven't yet published a version of your function, by default, AWS Lambda will refer to whatever saved function you're currently working with as the latest version. So that's what it is. We're free to describe the purpose of this version, but I will leave it blank for now. Let's click on Publish. So as you notice, a :1 suffix has been appended to the function name indicating that this is version 1. And this number will just increment as you publish new versions. Even if I delete this version, and publish another one, the numbering of versions will not reset. It will just keep on incrementing as you add new versions. Take note that a published version is immutable. This means that if you go to the Code tab and try to add a line to this function, you'll see that nothing shows up. You can't even delete. Editing this Lambda function code is not possible. Let's try to publish another version but this time, let's add a little modification to our function. Let's go to the unpublished Lambda function version. And let's change the body message to, "this is a new version". Let's deploy the change. Okay, click on Versions, then publish a new version. Let's check if the new message has been added. So here it is. All right, what I want to do next is to invoke these function versions via CLI. Let me bring up my terminal. Okay, so when you invoke a function, you can either refer to it by its name or ARN. It's just a matter of preference. Let's type the aws lambda invoke command. I prefer using the function name because it's shorter. First, let's invoke version two. I'm going to type test, which is the function name, followed by a colon, then the version number. If you're using ARN, the syntax is the same. Just append a column followed by the version number. Then let's hit Enter. So it returns the hello from Lambda message, which is the default one that comes when you create a function. This time, let's invoke version 3. So I'm just going to repeat the command and change the version from 2 to 3. Hit Enter. So as expected, it returns a different message, and this is the one that we changed a while ago. Great. Now, the problem with versions is you don't get to control the number that gets appended to the ARN or function name. Whenever you publish a new version, the version number just keeps on increasing. Now, it can be a little annoying if your application references a particular version, or if your function is integrated with an AWS service like API Gateway, because every time you deploy a new version, you also have to update them in your code. Would it be nice if we can just have a pointer that we can switch from one version to another? So this is where aliases come into play. You can think of an alias as a nickname that you can give to a certain version. Let's go ahead and assign an alias to the two versions that we have. Click on Aliases and hit Create alias. First, let's choose a version. Let's select version 2, and give it the name of old. Then let's do the same for version 3. This time, let's name it new. Now, if you go to the Aliases section, you see the list of aliases and the versions that they represent. What's nice with aliases is that you can actually change the versions that they're pointing at. For example, I can edit the new alias and switch the version it's pointing to from 3 to 2. To reference a function name or ARN with an alias, just append a colon next to the name or ARN followed by the alias name. Again, the advantage of using an alias instead of the version number when referencing a Lambda function is that you no longer have to update your application code. Every time you publish a new version, you can just change the version that the alias is pointing to in the Lambda console or via the update alias API command.

AWS Lambda and Amazon VPC integration
- [Instructor] If you have resources like a database or EC2 inside a private subnet that you want to access and process data from it using Lambda functions, by default, you won't be able to. The reason is that under the hood, Lambda functions are run in a separate secure VPC with access to the internet and AWS services. This VPC is entirely managed by AWS Lambda, which means you don't have access to it. Take note that this VPC is different from the default VPC that is created in your accounts. So how will you enable connectivity between a Lambda function and the resources hosted in a private subnet? First, you must create an execution role for the function with the AWS Lambda VPC access execution role policy attached to it. This manage IAM policy contains a list of permissions required for the Lambda function to create an elastic network interface. This is necessary because when you connect a Lambda function to A VPC, Lambda needs to manage the creation and deletion of elastic network interfaces in the subnet of the resource you are accessing. And if you're wondering how AWS does it, they use an internal service called AWS Hyperplane, which basically acts as a net service responsible for linking the Lambda function and the ENIs in your VPC. Next, you must configure the network settings of your Lambda function and connect it to the VPC where the private resources are located. When you create a VPC configuration for the function, you get to choose the subnets where the ENI will be deployed, and a security group that will control the traffic between your Lambda function and the VPC. Take note that your Lambda function will lose access to the internet once it's connected to the VPC. This is because AWS Lambda only assigns private IP addresses to the ENIs it creates. So even if your function is connected to a public subnet, your VPCs internet gateway will still not be able to route traffic between the internet and your function. To give internet access to your Lambda function, you have to create a net gateway in the public subnet of your function's VPC and configure the private subnets route table to direct request to all IP addresses to the net gateway.

AWS CodeCommit overview
- [Instructor] AWS CodeCommit is a fully managed source control service that hosts private Git-based repositories. CodeCommit work similarly to a lot of code repo out there, like GitHub. So if you have experience using any of these services before in your project, then you now have an idea of CodeCommit. You can use the same Git commands that you're familiar with to push or pull codes into your own CodeCommit Repository. CodeCommit is natively secure as it only uses HTTPS or SSH for authenticating file transfers. There are no limits on the number of repositories that you can create, and you can store any file type of any size, including application assets, such as images and libraries, alongside your code. This is possible because under the hood, CodeCommit uses Amazon S3 and DynamoDB for storing repository data. So besides its unlimited storage, high availability and durability are also guaranteed. So why would you want to choose CodeCommit over other repositories available out there? Well, the key benefit of creating a code repo in CodeCommit is that integrations with other AWS services will be a lot easier and more convenient for you as a developer. This is especially the case if most of your development workloads are done within AWS. For example, you can use Elastic Beanstalk to directly deploy incremental code updates made to a CodeCommit repository. Another example is when you want to monitor repository events. Suppose your team wants to be alerted every time a new branch is created, or when a commit is pushed to a branch. You can do so by creating a CodeCommit trigger that points out to an Amazon SNS topic.

AWS CodeBuild overview
- [Instructor] AWS CodeBuild is a fully managed continuous integration service that compiles source code, runs tests, and produces a build artifact of that code that is ready to deploy. Let's briefly discuss what continuous integration is so you can have a practical insight into the benefits of CodeBuild. Continuous integration is a practice in software development that allows multiple developers to integrate frequent code changes into a shared repository without running into what is known as the merge hell. A merge hell is a scenario where two incompatible codes are merged together, resulting in a build fail. This could escalate to a bigger mess, especially when too many code changes have already been committed. A lot of time would be wasted on debugging and undoing the damage, which could hamper the development progress, or positively delay the application's deployment schedule. Continuous integration allows you to detect those errors early before they are merged with other codes. Here is a simple workflow of how continuous integration works. First, you commit a change to a code repository, which could be in GitHub or AWS CodeCommit. This change triggers a CI server that will automatically perform a suite of tests on the code to ensure that the change won't cause the application to break. Once all tests are completed, the CI server will notify you if the code passes the test or not. If the code is good, then it is accepted. Go back to the first step and feel free to keep submitting changes until you're satisfied with your work. But if the test fails, then you have to fix the code first before you can continue developing. 

AWS CodeBuild workflow
- [Instructor] Now let's find out how CodeBuild works when you run a build. First off, you must create a build project. A build project defines how CodeBuild will run a build. It includes information such as, where to get the source code, which build environment to use, the build commands to run, and where to store the build artifact, if there's any. You can create a build project using the CodeBuild console, AWS CLI, AWS SDK, or at the creation of a CodePipeline. Take note, you can only associate one build project per branch. If you need to test multiple branches, then you have to create a separate build project for each of them. Once you start a build, CodeBuild will launch a build environment based on the configurations that you defined in your build project. You can manually start the build from the CodeBuild console by simply clicking the Start Build button. If you're looking to write a script to automate this process, you can do so by using the Start build API. Once the build environment is up and running, CodeBuild will download the source code from the source provider that you picked. A source provider can be Amazon S3, AWS CodeCommit, GitHub, Bitbucket, and GitHub Enterprise. Next, CodeBuild will follow and run the Build Commands it reads in inside the buildspec.yaml file. The build spec, or build specification, is a yaml file that lets you instruct CodeBuild on what commands to run at each phase of the build. The buildspec.yaml file can be defined when you're creating a build project or it can also be included as a part of your source code. If you have the build spec as a part of your source code, by default, the build spec file must be named buildspec.yaml, which are all in lowercase and should be placed at the root level of your project folder. After the build environment is done with code testing, it will upload the build artifact to an S3 bucket, if there's any. An artifact refers to the compiled and compressed version of your source code. It can be a ZIP file, JAR file, an APK for mobile apps, a binary file, an executable, and many others. Producing an artifact is completely optional and it's up to you to configure. If you're just running tests and have no need for artifacts, you can specify the No artifacts option in your build project configuration. You can also create a build notifications rule about the status of your build using Amazon SNS. By doing this, you or your team can be alerted if a build has successfully completed or if something went wrong. If you enable logging to CloudWatch or Amazon S3, the build environment will send build logs to CloudWatch logs or an S3 bucket while running the build. Logging is optional, but I recommend you to enable it so you can see the events that took place during the build, which could be helpful later on in debugging. You can get the summarized build information from the CodeBuild console, but you can go to CloudWatch logs in case you want to check the entire build logs.

AWS Step Functions overview
- [Instructor] In this lecture we'll talk about AWS Step Functions. Step Functions lets you orchestrate the components of a microservices application or any processes into a serverless workflow. A workflow is a series of steps with the output of one step acting as input into the next step. Your workflow defines the process of how your application accomplishes its goal from start to end. In Step Functions, we refer to the workflow as a State Machines, and each step in a State Machine is called a state. What's nice about Step Functions is that you can review your State Machine visually with its graphical user interface. This is a truly great feature as it can already serve as your blueprint, so you would have a big picture of what you're trying to do when designing a workflow. Having a visual workflow will help you track where you are, your inputs and outputs for each state, as you add more steps in your workflow or as you update it. A State Machine is defined using the Amazon States language or ASL, which has a similar syntax to adjacent document. If you don't want to write in JSON Step Functions, also provide a drag-and-drop interface, which further simplifies how you build your State Machine. There are eight state types that you can choose from when building a State Machine. These are the Task, Choice, Parallel, Wait, Fail, Succeed, Pass, and the Map state. So just a quick rundown on what they are. The first one, which is the core of your State Machine, is the task state. The Task State represents a single unit of work in your State Machine, application code that needs to be executed by your Lambda function, is done by the Task state. A task state can only point to a single lambda function, an easiest task or what have you. When you start the execution of a State Machine, the Step Functions will invoke Lambda function directly from a task state. Next is the choice state. The choice state adds a branching logic to your workflow, and you can think of it as an if then else statement where there is more than one possible outcome that can occur when evaluating a parameter against a condition that you set. Then we have the parallel state. The parallel state allows you to perform two branches of execution at the same time, and a use case for this is when you want to run two independent activities that perform different processes to an input from the same node. Next is the wait state. The wait state allows you to add a delay to your State Machine before it continues on executing states. This is similar to how a sleep function works. Now let's talk about the fail state. This state stops the execution of the State Machine and marks it as a failure. One example where you could use this is when you have a registration process and you want to deny any invalid form that a user has submitted. You can work the registration as a failure and have a custom error message like Invalid Username. The Succeed state, on the other hand, will just stop the execution successfully. And next is the passive state. You can use the passive state to pass the input from the previous state to its output without performing work. This is useful when you're debugging a portion of your State Machine, and you want to have a visibility of the output of a particular state. Maybe the data is formatted incorrectly and it is sending that down to another state, which makes your State Machine fail. The last state is the map state, and this state takes in an array as an input. You can think of the map state as a four-loop condition where you are basically iterating over each item in the array, and you're executing the same function to different parameters. So why use Step Functions, one use case where you can easily understand why Step Functions could be useful is when you have a sequential job implemented in a Lambda function. Imagine there are a lot of utility functions here that are chained sequentially, so whatever the output of function A is taken as an input for function B. The output of function B acts as an input to function C and so on. The problem here is that if something unexpected happens while, say, function C is running, which causes the Lambda function's execution to fail, you would have to invoke the Lambda function again to initiate the workflow. Since AWS Lambda is stateless, whatever output that function B has already produced will be lost. So basically you're back to zero again. One thing you could do is break these functions into separate states in a State Machine using Step Functions, so we have a task state for each function A, function B, and function C. AWS Step Functions has built in retry and error condition handling capabilities that you can implement for each state. So when the time function C fails, you would only need to retry the execution of function C. The state from function B is retained. Step Functions can also help solve the problem of timeout errors for a Lambda function. Let's say that there are four functions in your Lambda function and each of those functions runs sequentially for five minutes, we will get a total of 20 minutes if we add older execution times. This is a problem since Lambda can only run for a maximum of 15 minutes. To solve this, we concern the functions inside the Lambda function, into individual states, this way, each function is contained in a separate Lambda function, which has a separate execution timeout.

AWS Amplify overview
- [Instructor] Let's now talk about AWS Amplify. What really is this service, and what can it do? AWS Amplify is one of the many development services in AWS that helps you build extensible full-stack web and mobile apps faster. It allows you to easily start your application development and automatically scale your resources with less management overhead. In other words, AWS Amplify deploys your application in a serverless architecture without any manual intervention on your part. Plus, it also gives you the option to integrate machine learning capabilities in your applications. Put simply, AWS Amplify is a set of purpose-built tools and features. It consists of the Amplify Studio, Amplify Libraries, Amplify CLI, Amplify Hosting, and many more. Let's quickly cover its different modules one by one. Amplify Studio is a visual development environment that simplifies the development of your web and mobile apps. You can easily build your frontend UI with a set of ready-to-use UI components, which you can directly connect your app backend. Since your UI components are already pre-built, the number of your boilerplate code will be reduced, allowing you more time to focus on implementing the application business logic. You can define your data models, implement user authentication, and add file storage using the Amplify Studio without any backend expertise. In addition, you can import Figma prototypes built by your application designers into the Amplify Studio for a more seamless collaboration. The AWS Amplify Libraries are open source JavaScript libraries in the Amplify framework. These are specifically designed to aid you in building AWS-powered mobile and web apps. The Amplify JavaScript libraries are supported in React, React Native, Angular Ionic View, and different web and mobile frameworks. The Amplify Command Line Interface is a CLI tool chain that you can use to configure and maintain your app backend straight from your local desktop with Amplify CLI as an interactive workflow and intuitive use cases that you can leverage such as authentication, storage, and API. It allows you to test your features locally and deploy your app to multiple environments. This tool provides infrastructure-as-code templates, which are automatically loaded to CloudFormation or AWS SAM. With the Amplify CLI, you can have a much effective team collaboration and easy integration with Amplify's CI/CD workflow. It also has a feature called Amplify Hosting, which allows you to host secure, reliable, fast web apps or websites via the AWS Content Delivery Network. Under the hood, it deploys your applications to Amazon CloudFront's Content delivery network, which comes with hundreds of points of presence globally. Amplify Hosting also allows you to set up your own custom domain for your applications and add custom alarms to send notifications if a certain metric exceeds a threshold that you specify. These are some of the tools and features available in AWS that you can leverage with. Aside from Amplify Studio, Amplify Libraries, Amplify CLI, and Amplify Hosting, there are many other features and tools in AWS Amplify that you can try out. And AWS is adding more features soon to fast-track your software development experience, AWS Amplify can indeed amplify your development capabilities.

Amazon VPC overview
