In this lecture, we'll discuss these types one by one, and discover the different use cases for each storage class. Let's first talk about the Amazon S3 Standard class. This class is primarily used to store your frequently accessed data. It offers high durability availability, and performance object storage for your virus workloads. It replicates your data to three or more availability zones, to achieve high availability of 99.9%. Among all other storage classes, the Amazon S3 Standard class, is the only one that doesn't have a minimum storage duration charge. This allows you to temporarily store your data for a day or so, and only pay for that period. There's no obligation to pay the 30 day, 90 day, or 180 day minimum storage duration fee. In addition, there's also no data retrieval fee when you use this storage class. There are many use cases for this particular type. You can use this if you want to have a highly available, durable static web hosting. You can also use this if your company needs a temporary storage service for storing the nightly log processing of your application. If the logs are meant to be stored for 24 hours only, then using the Amazon S3 Standard storage class, will be the right pick. In terms of cost, the S3 Standard class, is the most expensive among all other classes, so make sure that your properly your storage requirements before using this type. Let's now move on to the Amazon S3 Standard Infrequent-Access for S3 IA As its name implies, the storage class is primarily used to store infrequently accessed data, that's suitable for storing data that is accessed less frequently, but also requires rapid access when needed. This storage class also replicates your data, to three or more availability zones and has a data availability of 99.99%. However, it has a minimum storage duration charge of 30 days. This means that you have to pay for the entire 30 day storage, even if you have already deleted your objects after a few days or so. The same fee applies if you change your storage class to a different type before that 30 day period. Make sure that the objects you store here, should last for at least a month or so to avoid unnecessary charges. Unlike the standard class, this one has a data retrieval fee that is measured per gigabyte. This is why you have to only store objects that are infrequently accessed to avoid unnecessary retrieval fees. The S3 Standard Infrequent-Access class, is suitable for long-term storage, data backups, and as a data store for your disaster recovery files. It's also applicable for storing the primary backup copies of your on-premises dataset. The next type that we'll cover, is the counterpart of the Standard Infrequent-Access class, the S3 One Zone Infrequent-Access, or One Zone-IA, is meant for storing less frequently accessed, and easily reproducible data that requires immediate retrieval when needed. It also has a minimum storage duration charge of 30 days. In terms of the price, the One Zone-IA is cheaper, compared with the S3 IA storage class. Unlike other S3 storage classes, which store data in a minimum of three availability zones, this class stores data in a single availability zone only. This is why it only has an availability of 99.95%, which is the lowest among all other classes. Because of the fewer AZs, it costs less to store data here, than the usual S3 Standard-IA class. This S3 One Zone-IA type is ideal for clients who require a cheaper option for their infrequently accessed data, but to not require the availability and resilience of the S3 standard or S3 IA a class. This is suitable for storing secondary backup copies of your on-premises dataset, that are rarely accessed, or for storing easily recreatable data. When using this storage class, you have to consider that your data is replicated, in a single AZ only. Therefore, you have to be careful not to store the primary backup copies or any critical business data that is not easy to reproduce. Let's now discuss the Amazon S3 Intelligent-Tiering class. This is the only cloud storage type that delivers automatic cost savings by moving your objects between different access tiers when access patterns change. It has a minimum storage duration charge of 30 days. Just like the S3 Standard class, there's also no retrieval fee It is designed to optimize costs, by automatically moving data to the most cost-effective access tier, without any operational overhead on your part. The S3 Intelligent-Tiering class, stores the objects in four access tiers, which are the two low-latency access tiers that are optimized for frequent and infrequent access into optional archive access tiers, that are designed for asynchronous or rare access. You can use the S3 Intelligent-Tiering storage class, if your data has an unpredictable access pattern, meaning some files in your bucket are frequently accessed, while the rest are not. This storage class is also suitable for other use cases, like if the access patterns to your data vary all the time, if some of our files are accessed frequently, while other files are rarely accessed in an unpredictable pattern, if some of the data in your S3 bucket are accessed less frequently than others, if you are unsure of how frequently your data will be accessed, if you want to keep costs low by automatically moving your data to the appropriate S3 storage class, if your data will be accessed by users over variable periods of time. If you need storage with no management overhead, or if you want to avoid lifecycle policies that are not consistently implemented, or are partially implemented, which could cost an SR high cost storage. Let's now tackle the different archive storage classes in Amazon S3. The Amazon S3 Glacier class, is a secure, durable, and low cost storage type that is suitable for data archiving. This is perfect if you need a cost effective storage solution for data that is rarely accessed and doesn't require a fast retrieval time. It also replicates your data to three or more availability zones and has a data availability of 99.99%. However, it has a minimal storage duration charge of 90 days with a high data retrieval fee. It's one of the storage classes in Amazon S3, but it has its own management console, apart from the regular S3 console. So technically, you can store your data through S3 Glacier in two ways, by using the S3 console, or by its own leisure console. You can create an object using the S3 console, and set its storage glass to S3 Glacier, to directly archive your data. And S3 lifecycle policy, can also be configured to automatically move your data from S3 standard, or S3 infrequent access storage classes, to S3 Glacier. In the Amazon S3 Glacier console, you can create a resource called a "vault." A vault is basically a container for storing your data archives. An archiving glacier is essentially any object, such as a photo, video, document, or a zip file that you can store in the vault. This is the base unit of storage in S3 Glacier, which contains a unique ID, and an optional description. You can only create a vault in the S3 Glacier console, and not in the S3 console. When you create a Glacier vault, you have to specify a vault name, and the AWS region in which you want to place your vault. This resource comes with its own security feature as well. You can use a vault lock to your Glacier vault, to ensure data integrity and access control. A vault lock is simply an access policy that helps you enforce regulatory, and compliance requirements. You can specify a "write once, read many" control, to lock a vault policy from feature edits. Once the vault lock is enabled, the policy can no longer be changed. This feature is useful if your company needs to retain archives for a specific number of years, before the files can be deleted. For example, you can set up a vault lock policy that denies users from modifying or deleting an archive, until after one year, three years, seven years, or any number of figures that you define. Amazon Glacier offers three types of archive retrieval options. These are Expedited, Standard, and Bulk. Expedited retrievals enables you to quickly access your data. In the event that urgent requests for a subset of archives are required. It allows you to access your archive data within just one to five minutes, as long as it's file size doesn't exceed 250 MB. You can also set up provision capacity, to ensure that the retrieval capacity of your expedited retrievals is available as needed. The standard retrievals, on the other hand, allow you to access any of your Glacier archives within three to five hours. This is a default option for retrieval requests if you don't specify a retrieval option on your request. The less archive retrieval option is called Bulk retrievals. This is the lowest cost retrieval option in Amon S3 Glacier. You can use this to retrieve large amounts of data archive within half a day or so. A bulk retrieval option typically completes the process within five to 12 hours. The other type of archive storage class is called the Amazon S3 Glacier Deep Archive. This is the lowest cost storage class in Amazon S3. The Deep Archive class supports long-term retention, and digital preservation of your data. This is primarily used to retain your data sets for seven to 10 years or longer, to meet regulatory compliance requirements. Just like its S3 glacier counterpart, the Deep Archive class, also replicates your data to three or more availability zones, and has a data availability of 99.99%. However, this class cost way less than the regular Glacier class, and has a longer retrieval time. Its minimum storage duration charge is 180 days, which is almost equivalent to six months. That is the longest duration among all other storage classes. Therefore, should only store data that is actually meant for data archiving only. The data that you store here, should be rarely accessed, and does not have a strict retrieval time. The Amazon S3 Glacier Deep Archive class, only has two retrieval options, which are standard and bulk. If you retrieve your archive using the standard option, your data will be restored within 12 hours, and if you chose bulk, your data will be available within four to eight hours. It doesn't have an expedited option like S3 Glacier, so don't expect that you'll be able to fetch your archive data in a matter of minutes, if it is in the Deep Archive class

Amazon S3 event notification
- [Instructor] The Amazon Simple Storage System service, or Amazon S3, provides scalable and durable object storage for storing a variety of data. Companies can store static content, media files, data archives, and even confidential information under S3 buckets. Many AWS services also use it for storing CloudTrail logs, CloudFormation templates, EBS snapshots, ELB access logs, and many others. You can also utilize Amazon S3 as a data lake for Amazon Redshift Spectrum, AWS Glue, Amazon Athena, or Amazon EMR. With this vast amount of stored information, organizations must ensure that their Amazon S3 buckets are secured and monitored for unexpected changes. Amazon S3 is an important service in AWS. That's why you have to ensure that it is properly configured at all times, and you're aware of what's happening to the objects stored in your bucket. For mission-critical applications, you should set up a mechanism that automatically notifies you of any new objects that were added in the bucket and the ones that were deleted. This use case can be fulfilled by setting up the Amazon S3 Event Notification. The Amazon S3 Event Notification feature enables you and your team to be notified when specific events happen in your S3 buckets. You can choose the particular S3 events that you want to monitor, and Amazon S3 will publish a notification to your desired destinations. This feature enables you to have more visibility on your data and promptly remediate any potential data leaks. Amazon S3 can publish notifications for new object creation, object deletion, object restoration from S3 Glacier storage class, Reduced Redundancy Storage object lost events, and replication events. You can configure Amazon S3 to publish events to various destinations, such as an Amazon SNS topic, Amazon SQS, or an AWS Lambda function. The S3 Event Notifications are usually transmitted within seconds and are designed to be delivered at least once. You can enable object versioning on your S3 bucket to ensure that an event notification is always sent for every successful object upload. With S3 versioning, every successful write operation will produce a new version of your S3 object and send the corresponding event notification. Versioning avoids the event notification issue, where only one notification is sent when multiple operations are executed to a non-versioned object.

Amazon Simple Notification Service (Amazon SNS) overview
- [Instructor] Notification is a vital component of any enterprise system. Teams can get notified of any system problems that may occur, allowing them to quickly respond to the changes or outages of their applications. A notification can also give a heads up to your IT operations team that a certain component is currently experiencing an issue or service degradation, which could possibly escalate if not properly fixed soon. Just imagine this, your production website experienced an outage and thousands of your customers around the globe are already furious, yet you still don't know what's happening. This means that you do not have a notification service in your architecture. Your IT operations team could be completely unaware of your application's health if they don't have a proper monitoring or notification process in place. You haven't gotten any text, email, or any type of message at all to inform you about the current situation. Moreover, the unavailability issue could have been prevented if you were just notified of the current status of the resources much earlier. This is the main reason why every modern architecture needs to have a notification system configured. In AWS the primary service for adding notification capability to your architecture is by using Amazon SNS. We'll discuss the basics of Amazon SNS in this lecture and also cover its types, concepts, features, and other components. Firstly, the Amazon Simple Notification Service or Amazon SNS is a fully managed messaging and notification service in AWS. Its primary purpose is of course, for notification, as already stated in its name. Amazon SNS, has a selection of different features that you can use and configure to build your custom cloud architecture. This can also be integrated with a lot of AWS services, such as Amazon SQS, Amazon CloudWatch, AWS Config, and many others. In Amazon SNS, we create a resource called a Topic. An SNS topic is the AWS resource that can be subscribed to by your operations team, by an external application, by another AWS service, or even by you. It's like a subscription to a web newsletter that allows you to receive important emails for news, promotions, events, and other notifications. The messages that are being sent by the SNS topic to its subscribers are populated by a publisher. A publisher is simply an entity or a service that produces messages. For example, AWS Config can send data to an SNS topic if there are any changes in the settings of your AWS resources. Amazon S3 can also send data to Amazon SNS for any new objects in your bucket via the Amazon S3 Events Notifications feature. The same goes for Amazon CloudWatch, Amazon EC2 auto scaling, Amazon RDS, AWS Budgets, EC2 Image Builder, AWS Backup, Amazon Cloud Guru, and many more. In addition, you can also add a Lambda trigger to your DynamoDB streams to send items to our topic. An SNS topic can have one or more subscribers, which can be an actual person, an AWS service, or an external system. You can actually subscribe to a specific SNS topic, so we can receive an email, a text message, or even a push notification to your mobile phone. This is a powerful notification service since it can alert you to any critical errors in your production environment in many different ways, instead of just a usual email. Amazon SNS is capable of sending notification messages to both HGTP or HTTPs endpoints, such as a webhook URL for Slack or other third party messaging services. You can also integrate an Amazon SQS Queue, AWS Lambda function, Amazon can assist data firewall stream and other AWS services as a subscriber to your topic. For example, two or more SQS Queues can subscribe to a single SNS topic where each queue sends the messages to a totally separate system. Having two or more subscribers to a single topic allows you to fan out or spread a single message to multiple recipients. Essentially, there are two types of communication where the Amazon SNS service is used, which are for application to application messaging or for application to person messaging. The first type is obviously for sending notifications to multiple applications or AWS services. The latter is all about transmitting a message to an actual person, or an IT operation staff that can take action to specific system events. An application to person messaging is quite helpful if you have a roster of on-call team members who need to be notified of any application issues and to help them resolve system issues quickly. Amazon SNS offers two types of topics that you can launch, which are standard topic and first in first out, or a FIFO topic. The default type is the standard topic that provides maximum throughput and best effort ordering. A FIFO topic is commonly used if strict message ordering and message duplication are required. The core concepts for these types are similar in Amazon Simple Queue service, which also offers a standard and a FIFO type. For security, Amazon SNS provides data encryption and access control options that you can configure for your topics. It already provides in-transit encryption by default, but you can optionally enable service site encryption too. An access policy can also be added to define the individuals or the systems that can access your topic. By default only the topic owner can publish or subscribe to your SNS topic. Amazon SNS offers message filtering, message fan out, message durability, message encryption, message archiving, and many other features, to customize your SNS topics. The message filtering feature enables you to filter or just send a subset of messages to a particular subscriber. You can assign a filter policy to the topic subscription and configure the topic to publish specific messages to multiple Amazon SQS Queues based on its message type. This setup is also known as Fanout Event Notification, where messages are pushed to multiple subscribers. The individual SQS Queues can be designed to have different consumers to meet your requirements. This is an example of an event-based architecture that eliminates the need to periodically pull a queue for updates. Amazon SNS can also have its own dead letter queue, or DLQ as well. The messages that can be delivered through subscribers successfully can be captured by assigning a Redrive policy to your Amazon SNS subscriptions, and then selecting an Amazon SQS Queue. There's also a feature called Delivery Status, which checks the status of the messages sent to the topic subscribers. This feature generates log entries that are sent to CloudWatch logs for monitoring and troubleshooting purposes.

Amazon Simple Queue Service (Amazon SQS) overview
- [Instructor] In this lecture, we'll discuss the basics of Amazon Simple Queue Service and its various components and features. We will rediscover what a queue is and why it is considered an important component in the distributed architecture. We'll also talk about the different features of Amazon SQS and how it is integrated with other AWS services. Okay, let's begin. Amazon SQS is a fully managed message queuing service that allows you to do two primary things, decouple your tightly coupled architecture and process workloads Asynchronously. Decoupling means modularizing your monolithic application by bringing it up into multiple independent components. In this way, any failure or changes in one component will not directly affect the other components. It also improves the system reliability of our application and makes the scaling easier too. For the second one, asynchronous processing simply means the ability to process a request at a later time, rather than right away. It enables you to add hundreds or even thousands of messages to the queue without having to wait for a long period of time, until the entire processing has been completed. This asynchronous method of processing provides the necessary delay that lessens the incoming load to your web servers. It also provides a way to reprocess field requests in case of a system failure, which is not a common thing for synchronous workflows. Essentially, the Amazon Simple Queue service is simply a queue, or to be more specific, a message queue. Understanding the core concept of a queue is quite important to fully understand the capabilities of Amazon SQS. So what is a queue and how does a message queue works? Queues are actually everywhere. Whether you're lining up to order your food at McDonald's, standing in line to check in your luggage at the airport, waiting for your turn to pay your grocery items at the store counter, or just queuing up to get hot coffee from a barista in the morning. You have to stop and wait for your person in front of you while the staff at the counter process each and every transaction. You have to be patient and wait for your turn because the flow is FIFO, meaning, First-In, First-Out. The very first person who lined up in the queue will be the first one to be attended to by the operator, and it's also the first one to get out of the queue. If you're lucky, there will be two or more counters available that can do the processing in parallel cutting down that time it takes to serve you and other customers in the queue. In data structures, a queue is a linear structure that mimics the characteristic of a queue in the real world. The first item that goes in is also the first one that goes out, so by default, the order of processing in a queue is also FIFO. The items in the queue are stored sequentially, or in other words, arrange in order of arrival. You can enqueue or insert data at the end of this linear structure and then dequeue or remove data for processing. The component that process the items in the queue is called a consumer. Apparently, a consumer consumes the item from the queue one by one which is similar to a store counter which accepts your payment for groceries and issues a receipt whenever you line up in a supermarket. There's also a queue in distributed architectures that handles the incoming messages in your application and pass them on to the consumers for data processing. This is also called a message queue which is a form of asynchronous service-to-service communication that allows the modules of your system to pass data with each other and reprocess at a later time. Basically, a message can be in the form of an HTTP or an API request that is sent by website users or other systems. This strategy is commonly used if the total time of processing a single transaction takes a long time to complete, or if your system has an associated downstream call that is expected to last for a couple of minutes or so. The consumers in a distributed architecture fetch their messages from the queue and process them asynchronously. This method of consuming a message is called polling. Now that we understand what the queue is, we can now grasp the Amazon SQS concepts easily. Amazon SQS is a message queue service that is fully managed by AWS. Therefore, you don't have to launch or manage your physical servers that power your Amazon SQSQ. This is perfect for workloads with long-running requests, which may cause your application to be unresponsive to other users. It is also used to help scale your resources in response to the changes in your system load. In addition, Amazon SQS can be integrated with other AWS services to help you build your microservices, containers, and serverless architectures. There are two types of message queues, which are the Standard Queue and First-in, First Out Queue or FIFO Queue. The standard queue is a default type that offers an at least once delivery with best-effort ordering. On the other hand, the SQS FIFO queue is designed to provide an exactly once delivery and the messages are stored in the exact order they were added into the queue. The standard queue provides higher throughput than FIFO, since it does not check the order of messages. The downside of this is it may deliver duplicate messages to your consumers which can affect the integrity of your data. To solve this, you can change the visibility timeout of your messages in the queue using the change message visibility API, or through the AWS Management console. Take note that this not a guarantee against receiving a message twice all the time. On the other hand, the FIFO queue guarantees that all messages will also be delivered exactly once. The drawback for this type is just lower throughput than the standard queue. A FIFO queue is suitable for applications where messages must be processed exactly once without any duplicate records and in the order in which they're received in the queue. The method of eliminating the redundant messages in the SQS queue is called deduplication. In a FIFO queue, you can configure the the duplication ID and group ID of your messages to ensure that the items pulled by your consumers are all unique. Amazon SQS also offers different configurations that you can use to create a special type of queue to meet your requirements. You can set the visibility timeout, message retention period, delivery delay, receive message wait time, and other configurations of our queue. A dead-letter queue, a delay queue, and a temporary queue can be created as well. A dead-letter queue, which is also known as DLQ, is actually a separate SQSQ where you can send them messages that experience a failure or for messages that cannot be processed by your consumers. For the second one, a delay queue is just normal SQSQ with a custom delivery delay setting. Lastly, a temporary queue is basically a lightweight virtual queue that is deleted automatically after use. Temporary queues can be created using a temporary queue client which is a Java program that you can run and configure. Amazon SQS provides encryption in transit to your messages by default. You can optionally enable server-side encryption to your queue where SQS encrypts all customer data addressed on its underlying servers. You can define who can access your queue using an access policy. You can restrict who can send messages to or receive messages from your queue, which can be the queue owner only, or an AWS account, an IAB user, or an IAM role that you specify. For application integration, you can use Amazon SQS along with other AWS services to build a cost-effective, scalable, and highly available architecture. You can configure your SQSQ to trigger a Lambda function when new messages arrive in your queue. This feature is also called a Lambda trigger. Conversely, you can configure Amazon SQS as the event source for your Lambda function. This is applicable if you have long-running requests or for generating custom reports that take five minutes or more to complete. Amazon SQS and Amazon SNS can also be used together for monitoring and event notifications. And Amazon SQS queue can subscribe to an Amazon SNS topic. In this setup, the messages are coming from Amazon SNS itself and are forwarded to Amazon SQS. It's important to note that an Amazon SNS topic can have multiple subscribers which can be different SQS queues. With this, one message from SNS can be sent to multiple SQS queues that are subscribed to that SNS topic at once. This is called a fan-out event notification. Amazon SQS can be integrated with Amazon EC2 Auto Scaling to scale your EC2 instances based on the age, depth, or number of messages in your queue. It can trigger an auto-scaling group to scale out your Amazon EC2 instances based on the SQS queue depth. A target-tracking policy can also be configured to scale your auto-scaling group based on the age of the oldest message in your SQS queue. This is somewhat similar to a real-world queue where the people are lined up in one queue and are attended by three or more counters to process transactions in parallel. This cuts down that time it takes to serve you and other customers in the queue, since there are a lot of people behind the counter to process their transactions. This same concept is also applicable to your applications. Amazon SQS can also be integrated with Amazon S3, Amazon ECS, Amazon EKS, and other services to do various functions, such as Amazon S3 events notifications, and sending messages between two ECS or EKS microservices.

Amazon Route 53 overview
- [Instructor] Amazon Route 53 is a global domain name system service that allows you to route traffic across various AWS regions and external systems outside of AWS. It provides a variety of routing policies that you can implement to meet your required use cases and automatically monitor the state and performance of your applications, servers, and other resources using health checks. With Route 53, you can register your own domain name, transfer a domain from another domain registrar, create health checks, route traffic flows, configure DNS Resolvers and many more. Basically, a domain name system or DNS, is just a system that routes a domain name to a particular IP address. Say for example, your website is running on a server with an IP address of 49.143.173.201. You then bought a new domain name in Route 53 called tutorialsdojo.com and set up a new DNS record that points to the IP address of your web server. If a user visits the tutorialsdojo.com website, it'll route the request to the 49.143.173.201 IP address where your server is located. This is the basic request flow in mapping the domain name to its mapped resource. Route 53 also allows you to map domain names to your elastic IP addresses, EC2 instances, static S3 websites, elastic load balancers, CloudFront distributions, and other AWS resources. The first thing that you have to do when you have successfully registered your domain name in Route 53 is to set up a hosted zone for your domain. A hosted zone is essentially a container for your DNS records and subdomains. The name of your hosted zone is similar to its corresponding web domain, so if you have a domain name called to tutorialsdojo.com, then your hosted zone is also to tutorialsdojo.com. You can create different types of DNS records within your hosted zone, which are mostly subdomains. You can add a subdomain called www.tutorialsdojo.com, portal.tutorialsdojo.com, or many other subdomains. In this example, the zone apex is tutorialsdojo.com, which is also known as a root domain or naked domain. Remember that the www.tutorialsdojo.com record is just another subdomain and not it's root domain. Route 53 also supports the DNSSEC security feature, which is an acronym for Domain Name System Security Extensions. This protocol protects our web applications from DNS spoofing or from Man-In-The-Middle attacks. If DNSSEC is enabled in your domain, then a DNSSEC solver establishes a chain of trust for responses from intermediate DNSSEC solvers. The chain of trust starts from the top level domain registry for your domain and ends with the authoritative name service at your DNS service provider. You can select two types of hosted zones in Route 53, a public hosted zone or a private hosted zone. The public one contains DNS records that specify how you want to route traffic on the public internet. In contrast, the private hosted zone contains records that specify how you want to route traffic in an Amazon VPC. Amazon Route 53 automatically creates an NS, or a name server record, and an SOA record, which stands for Start of Authority record. In your hosted zone, you can create different types of DNS records for your workloads. You can also enable query logging to store the information about the queries that Route 53 receives. It tracks a domain or subdomain request, the date and time of the query, and even the DNS record type for the request. In general, there are two kinds of records in Amazon Route 53. You can have an alias record or a non-alias record. An alias record lets you route traffic to selected AWS resources, whereas a non-alias record just allows you to specify the IP addresses or the custom domain names of your service or resources. Technically, the alias resource records sets are virtual records that work like CNAME records. CNAME is an acronym for Canonical Name Record. The difference between these two is that an alias record is not visible to DNS resolvers. This means that the resolvers only see the underlying a record and the resulting IP address of a target record. For example, you can create an alias record that directly points to a CloudFront distribution or an application node balancer. You can also route to another record in your hosted zone, to an API gateway, to an Elastic Beans stock environment, to a network node balancer, to a global accelerator, to an S3 website endpoint, to a VPC endpoint, and to other select AWS resources. Just keep in mind that whenever you see an alias record, that means it's pointing to a specific AWS resource rather than a particular IP address that you have to supply. In Route 53, you have different DNS record types that you can choose. You can create an A Record, an AAAA Record, CNAME, MX, TXT, PTR, SRV, SPF, NAPTR, CAA, DS, and many other types. The AAAA Record is also known as the Quad A Record. These types can be an alias record or not. So an A record can be an Alias A Record, or a Non-Alias A Record. For the Quad A Record, it can be an Alias Quad A Record, or just a Non-Alias Quad A Record, and so on and so forth. An A Record routes traffic to an IP version 4 address, while the Quad A Record primarily routes to an IP version 6 address. A CNAME record can redirect DNS queries to any DNS record, however, you cannot create a CNAME record at the Zone Apex or the top node of your DNS namespace. There are on-premises networks that have their own domain name server. Most companies use BIND as their internal DNS service. BIND is actually an acronym for Berkeley Internet Name Domain server. With a BIND DNS forwarder, you can resolve the domain names in the private hosted zone in AWS from your on-premises network. You can also migrate your existing DNS service, such as a buying server to Amazon Route 53 by importing a zoned file. Your globally distributed resources can either be considered active or passive. It's active if accepts live production traffic, and passive if it is just on standby, which will only be activated during a failover event. You can set up an active active failover to improve your system's fault tolerance and performance. By having several active environments, you can ensure the high availability and resiliency of our global applications. To set up an active active failover, you can use a single or a combination of routing policies such as latency, geolocation, geoproximity, and other policies to configure Route 53. To respond to a DNS query using any healthy record, you can combine two or more routing policies to comply with your company's strict RTO and RPO requirements. It helps simplify the process of setting up an active passive or active active failover for disaster recovery plan by intelligently routing traffic from your primary resources to the secondary resources based on the rules you specify.

Amazon CloudFront overview
- [Instructor] In this lecture, we will talk about Amazon CloudFront and the core concepts behind content delivery networks and edge locations. We will also go over its different components and other features. Basically, Amazon CloudFront is a Content Delivery Network service. A Content Delivery Network is also known as CDN and it is technically a network of servers and data centers scattered around the world that temporarily store the pictures, videos, documents and other static files of your website. The process of temporarily storing these static files is called caching, which is improves the performance of your web applications that are accessed by users globally. CloudFront can significantly decrease the latency of delivering your web content to your end users. In other words, it reduces the lag time of floating your website. Before we dig deeper into Amazon CloudFront, let's discover why we need a CDN in the first place and how it works. This will provide us a strong foundation of the underlying technology used by CloudFront. So, what really is a Content Delivery Network? Simply put, CDN is a network of data centers that delivers content to the viewers or users of your website. Imagine it this way, suppose you have a server running somewhere on the west coast, specifically in Los Angeles, California. Your server in LA hosts a website that shares travel photos, videos and other high resolution static media files to millions of users in Asia. The photos and videos are reviewed and downloaded by your customers in the Philippines, India, Taiwan, Singapore, Indonesia, Malaysia and other Asian countries. The question is, how long can these high resolution travel photos be transmitted and viewed by users? We have to consider the size of these files and the distance between the source server and its viewers. For example, there's a user named Stacy who is located in Manila, Philippines. She visited your website by typing the domain name in the web browser. At this point, the journey of her HTTP request to your server begins. The request will start from her laptop, then to her home wire free router, and then go through the different counters of her local internet service provider, or ISP. Now, since her server is located in California, how will her request reach the United States if it originated from the Philippines? There are roughly two ways for her HTTP request to reach her server in LA. It's either by satellite or by sea. If Stacy has satellite-based internet access like Starlink, then the traffic will be beamed from her antenna dish to a SpaceX satellite that's orbiting the earth. The traffic will then be routed to the US network and it'll be forwarded continuously until it reaches her whole server in Los Angeles. If Stacy is using a traditional internet service provider, then traffic may traverse different routers and the working components across the Pacific Ocean via undersea internet cables, which are also known as submarine cables. There's a series of undersea cables that run through the Pacific Ocean called Trans-Pac, or Trans-Pacific Cables, linking Asia to the mainland United States. You have the Asia-America Gateway, or AAG, the Pacific Light Cable Network, or PLCN, the Tata TGN Intra Asia Cable System, or TGN-IA, Southern Cross Cable and many others. These submarine cables span tens of thousands of kilometers in length. So, from the Philippines, the request may travel to Guam, to Hawaii, to the United States' west coast until it reaches her server in LA. That distance is over 10,000 kilometers, or about 7,000 miles. This may cause several seconds of delay in fetching the content in your LA server back to your user in Manila. For photos, the lag time could be about a few seconds, but for high quality videos, it may take several minutes to load before Stacy can view the content. This poor level of performance is definitely not desirable and may cause you to lose clients or high value on intercessions. The same thing goes if you have a user in Europe who tries to access her website. The HTTP request will have to travel a long way to deliver the content to the user. There is a series of trans-Atlantic submarine cables, too, that goes through the Atlantic Ocean connecting the United States to Ireland, the UK, France, Germany and other European countries. The traffic may (indistinct) TAT, fiber optic cable routes, which are built by joint ventures between a number of telecommunication companies, or via private cable routes like MAREA which is owned by Facebook, Microsoft and Telefonica. Of course, Amazon and Google have their own undersea cables, too. From your server in LA, the response traffic will have to travel to the Midwest, to New York, and all the way from the Atlantic before the media content is distributed to your European users who just want to view your travel photos and videos. This long geographic distance being your origin server and your users is a common route cause as to why it takes a lot of time for your content to load and why your website is too slow. This is the primary reason why you need a Content Delivery Network. With a CDN in place, the user request will be traveling thousands of miles across the Pacific or the Atlantic Ocean, this affects your data from your origin server. Your data can be stored or cached in the vast network of data centers and co-location facilities that are located in nearby proximity to your end users. These data centers are also called Points-of-Presence, or POP, which consists of edge locations and original ex-caches. A POP is a physical location that access a demarcation point for which two or more networks share a connection. Going back to our example, let's suppose you set up your travel website as an origin to an Amazon CloudFront distribution. Your photos and videos will eventually be stored in different applications worldwide, and not just in Los Angeles, California. Your content could be cached in Indonesia, Malaysia, and even in the Philippines. In this way, your users like Stacy, will be able to view the media files in less than a second since the data is already available nearby. Essentially, an edge location is simply a site that Amazon CloudFront uses to cache or store the copies of her content for faster delivery. Remember that this involves different Internet Service Provider from various countries and territories. Each of these ISPs or private networks has an edge or a boundary where other networks can connect to. An edge location AWS is powered by a physical extra router located at another boundary that enables an internal network to connect to external networks. The connection to other networks is done in an Internet Exchange Point, or IXP. There are also edge servers that caches content and perform computations in close proximity to the users. An edge server is just a standard rack server, or a device that provides an entry point for a particular network to connect to a different network. These edge servers are used to cache static content, which effectively reduces the latency to your website. It can also cache dynamic content, too, such as the response data of EPA calls, SLS websocket connections. Once again, Amazon CloudFront is a Content Delivery Network service in AWS. You might get overwhelmed by advanced networking concepts that we mentioned here, but don't worry. Just keep in mind that a CDN is primarily used to deliver the static and dynamic content of her websites faster. CloudFront is a network of data centers, point of presence and edge locations that distribute content to the viewers of her website. Amazon CloudFront has three basic components which are the origin, the distribution and the viewer. Basically, an origin is where the content originates or comes from. The distribution is an actual AWS resource that you can launch and configure to control how to distribute content to your users. Lastly, a viewer is the actual website visitors or users who view your content. In your CloudFront distribution, you can set an Amazon S3 Bucket, an Elastic Load Balancer, an AWS Elemental Service and HTTP server running in an Amazon EC2 Instance, or in an another external host as the origin. You can overset your on-premises server as the custom origin offered distribution. Amazon CloudFront has a lot of caching and security features that you can use to distribute your content effectively and securely. For example, if your origin is an Amazon S3 Bucket, you can restrict access to S3 content by using an Origin Access Identity user, or OAI. You can restrict access to your content based on the country or geographic location of your viewers. This can be done with its geo-restriction feature which lets you control the distribution of your content at the country level. Amazon CloudFront has a feature called Lambda@Edge which lets you do certain computations in proximity to your users, and that just delivers static content. Lambda@Edge allows you to run custom code closer to the end users of your application to improve your application performance in the (indistinct). Since the traffic doesn't go to the underlying origin server, this saves you from high data transfer cost as well. You can also improve the availability of your web applications with CloudFront by adding two origins instead of one. You can set up an origin failover by creating an origin group that contains your primary and secondary resources. If your primary origin is unavailable, the traffic will fill over to the secondary origin. Serving private content to specific users is also possible with its signed URLs and signed cookies features. You can distribute your content security via HTTPS by adding an SNI Custom SSL or a Dedicated IP Custom SSL. AWS WAF can be integrated as well with your CloudFront distribution to save user application from common web vari abilities. CloudFront has a lot of other features that you can use for your workloads. We will discuss these in detail in our succeeding lectures.

TWO. Security

AWS security services
- [Instructor] There are a lot of available security services in AWS to help you secure your cloud infrastructure and encrypt your data in transit or at rest. Whether you need protection against common web vulnerabilities, managing your encryption keys, generating SSL certificates, setting up your custom key stores, and many more, you can easily manage your application or database passwords using these services and ensure that your sensitive data is always safe. AWS can also prevent distributed denial of service attacks, or DDOS, that can overwhelm or even shut down your mission-critical applications. A DDOS attack usually exploits the protocols that are within the OSI layer three and layer four, such as the IP, UDP, and TCP. So for example, a hacker can command a fleet of botnets to initiate half open TCP connections to a server by only sending synchronized packets, or SYN, without sending the acknowledgement packets, which is also known as ACK. This compromises the three-way handshake process of the TCP connection and floods your website with illegitimate requests. In this lecture, we'll discuss AWS WAF, AWS Firewall Manager, AWS Shield, Amazon GuardDuty, AWS CloudHSM, AWS Key Management Service, AWS Secrets Manager, AWS Certificate Manager, Amazon Macie, Amazon Inspector, and Amazon Detective. The AWS Web Application Firewall Service, or AWS WAF, is basically just a web application firewall in AWS, just as its name implies. It protects your web applications from common web exploits that could affect the application availability, compromise security, or consume excessive resources. You can use AWS WAF to create custom rules that blocks common attack patterns, such as SQL injection and cross site scripting attacks. You can integrate AWS WAF with Amazon CloudFront, Application Load Balancer and Amazon API Gateway. With its IP Match condition feature, you can block malicious requests from a recurring set of IP addresses. AWS WAF can also protect your application from illegitimate requests sent by illegitimate external systems through its rate limiting rule. When these attacks occur, a rate-based rule can reduce the impact on the legitimate users of your websites. This can be achieved by creating a rate-based web access control list, or web ACL, and attaching it to a CloudFront distribution to help minimize the effects of a DDOS attack. This service is also suitable if your application is only authorized to be accessed from one specific country only. AWS WAF provides a geo match condition that blocks specific countries from accessing your site or to only allow access from certain countries that you define. AWS Firewall Manager is a security management service, specifically designed for your AWS WAF rules. It makes it easy for you to centrally configure and manage your WAF rules across your accounts and applications. Using the Firewall Manager, you can easily roll out your custom rules for Application Load Balancers, API gateways, and Amazon CloudFront distributions across accounts in AWS organization. AWS Shield is a managed DDOS protection service that safeguards applications running on AWS. This provides detection and automatic mitigations that minimize application downtime and latency. It can mitigate different types of flood attacks, such as UDP reflection, SYN flood, DNS Query flood, and HTTP flood attacks. AWS Shield protects your applications that use Amazon EC2, Elastic Load Balancers, CloudFront distributions, AWS Cloud Accelerators, and Route 53 edge locations. There are two types in the service, standard and advanced. The AWS Shield Standard tier is already built into your AWS services by default, with no extra charge. While the Advanced Tier is a paid version, which provides access to real-time DDOS attack notification and DDOS Response Team. Amazon GuardDuty is a managed threat detection service that helps you identify malicious or unauthorized activities in your AWS accounts and workloads. It monitors for activities such as unusual API calls, cryptocurrency mining, or potentially unauthorized deployments that integrates a possible account compromise. GuardDuty also detects potentially compromised EC2 instances. This service produces security reports called Findings that represents a potential security issue detected within your network. GuardDuty can send notification using CloudWatch Events when any change takes place in these security findings. Once again, this service is just used for threat detection, and it's not capable of doing any resource changes by itself, like rate-limiting protection or DDOS attack mitigation. Let's now discuss the two AWS services that you can use for your key management infrastructure. They are AWS CloudHSM, and AWS Key Management Service. AWS CloudHSM is a fully managed, cloud-based hardware security module, or HSM. Obviously, the word HSM in CloudHSM means Hardware Security Module, which enables you to easily generate and use your own encryption keys. These encryption keys can be in 128 bit or 256 bit that are used to encrypt your custom data or other encryption keys. And HSM is just a physical hardware device that performs cryptographic operations and securely stores cryptographic key material. This key material is basically a random Base64 or hexadecimal string in a binary format that is used by your encryption key. In CloudHSM, the cluster can be accessed or managed using CloudHSM clients, which is installed and hosted in your Amazon EC2 instances. The CloudHSM cluster is deployed in your Amazon VPC. Your clients can communicate with your HSM cluster using the Elastic Network Interfaces, or ENIs, of your HSMs. Since all of these resources are in your Amazon VPC and under your exclusive control, the CloudHSM cluster only has one sole tenant, which is you. This is what single tenant access means in CloudHSM. This service can be used to offload the SSL processing of your web servers, enabling Transparent Data Encryption, or TDE, for your Oracle databases and protecting the private keys for an issuing Certificate Authority, or CA. You can also integrate CloudHSM and AWS KMS to create a custom key store. AWS Key Management Service, or KMS, is a managed service that works almost like CloudHSM. Under the hood, KMS also uses hardware security modules that makes it easier for you to create and control your encryption keys. But unlike CloudHSM, this service has multi-tenant access, which means that you share the HSM with other tenants or AWS customers. Moreover, you cannot launch an HSM to your Amazon VPC or EC2 instances that you own. The HSM is fully managed by the Amazon Web Services Team themselves. AWS KMS can be integrated with other AWS services to help you protect the data you store with these services. For example, encrypting volumes or snapshots in Amazon EBS is powered by AWS KMS, as well as server-side encryption in Amazon S3, and database encryption in Amazon RDS. AWS KMS uses envelope encryption, which is a practice of encrypting your plain text data with a data key, and then encrypting that data key using another key called the Master Key. The primary resources in KMS are called Customer Master Key, or CMK. A CMK is basically a representation of the master key that encrypts your data key. With AWS KMS, you can store your CMKs and automatically rotate them to meet your encryption requirements. You can also create a custom key store in AWS KMS with CloudHSM. This custom key store provides complete control over your encryption key lifecycle management and allows you to remove the key material of your encryption keys. This is the same key material that we discussed earlier. You can also audit key usage independently of AWS CloudTrail and KMS itself using this custom key store. AWS Secrets Manager helps to protect secrets needed to access your applications, services, and IT resources. The service enables you to easily rotate, manage, and retrieve your secrets throughout their lifecycle. Basically, a secret can be a database password, API key, authentication tokens, or other sensitive data. This eliminates the need to hardcode sensitive information in plain text for a Lambda function or custom applications. Secrets Manager offers secret rotation with built in integration for Amazon RDS, Amazon Redshift, Amazon DocumentDB, and other services. In addition, Secrets Manager enables you to control access to secrets using fine-grained permissions and centrally audit your secrets. Just keep in mind that this service does not use HSM, so you should not store your encryption keys or key materials here. Amazon Macie is a fully managed security service that automatically recognizes and classify sensitive data or intellectual property on AWS. It uses machine learning to automatically discover, classify, and protect sensitive data stored in your Amazon S3 bucket and other AWS services. Amazon Macie recognizes Personally Identifiable Information, or PII, such as names, social security numbers, driver's license numbers, bank account numbers, password numbers, and email addresses. It provides you with dashboards and alerts that gives you a visibility of how sensitive data is being accessed or moved. AWS Certificate Manager is a service that allows you to create and manage SSL certificates. It lets you easily provision, manage, and deploy public and private Secure Sockets Layer and Transport Layer Security certificates for use with AWS services and your internal connected resources. It also enables you to create private certificates for your internal resources and manage the certificate lifecycle centrally. Public and private certificates provisioned through the AWS Certificate Manager are free, which you can use with certain AWS services. Amazon Inspector is an automated security assessment service that allows you to improve the security and compliance of your applications deployed on AWS. Amazon Inspector automatically assesses applications for vulnerabilities or deviations from best practices. After performing an assessment, Amazon inspector produces a detailed list of security findings prioritized by level of security. It provides an automated security assessment report that will identify the unintended network access to your Amazon EC2 instances and vulnerabilities in those instances. These findings can be reviewed directly or as part of your detailed assessment reports, which are available via the Amazon Inspector console or via its API. Amazon Detective is a service primarily used for security investigations and analysis. It makes it easy for you to analyze, investigate, and quickly identify the root cause of potential security issues or suspicious activities. Amazon Detective automatically collects log data from your AWS resources. What it basically does is to collect logs from AWS CloudTrail, Amazon VPC Flow Logs, Amazon GuardDuty findings, and other AWS services, then uses machine learning to analyze and conduct security investigations.

AWS Identity Services
- AWS Identity Services can enable you to securely manage identities, resources, and permissions in your IT infrastructure. You can also use these services on your hybrid cloud, web identity federation, or other third party identity services. These AWS identity services allows you to establish a single sign-on access strategy across different AWS accounts and on-premises environments. Companies with customer-facing web and mobile apps can quickly add sign-up and sign in functionalities using the cloud directories provided by AWS. You can set up fine grade access controls to grant their employees applications and devices the specific permissions they need to access the AWS services and resources. In this lecture, we will discuss the different identity services available in AWS. They are AWS Identity and Access Management, AWS Single Sign-on, Amazon Cognito, and lastly, AWS Directory Service. The AWS Identity and Access Management or IAM is the primary identity service in AWS. It enables you to manage access to various AWS services and resources securely. With IAM, you can create and manage AWS users and groups, which are called IAM users and IAM groups respectively. An IAM user has long-term credentials, such as a password or a set of access keys. You can create a policy called an IAM policy that contains permissions to allow internet access to the AWS resources. Once you created your IAM policy, you can then attach it to your IAM users and IAM groups. You can also create a role-based entity called an IAM role. An IAM role also has a set of permissions that allows an IAM user, another AWS account or other AWS services to perform functions that you define. Amazon Cognito is an identity service that lets you add user sign-up, sign-in, and access control features to your web or mobile apps. It allows users to log in to your application with their Facebook, Google, Amazon, or other social media accounts. It also supports enterprise identity providers, such as Microsoft Active Directory via the Security Assertion Markup Language, or SAML for short. In Amazon Cognito, you can either launch a user pool or an identity pool. To put simply, user pools are for authentication, while identity pools are for authorization. With a user pool, your app users can sign in by authenticating through their social identity providers. With an identity pool, you can obtain temporary and limited privilege AWS credentials that authorize access to other AWS services. AWS Single Sign-On or SSO is basically just a single sign-on service in AWS and SSO is an authentication mechanism that allows a user to log in with a single ID and password to access multiple and independent software systems. So instead of memorizing and using a lot of username and password, you only need to remember and use one set of credentials to easily log in across systems. AWS SSO provides a user portal in AWS that allows users to access their roles that they can assume in their assigned AWS accounts or business applications in one single place. It offers pre-configured SAML integrations to many business applications such as Salesforce, Office 365, and other apps. AWS Directory Service is essentially just a managed Microsoft Active Directory or AD for short. This service is built on actual Microsoft AD and does not require you to synchronize or replicate data from your existing active directory to the cloud. With this service, you don't need to install and manage an active directory domain controller in your Amazon EC2 instance. This will improve the security of your architecture and minimize the administrative demand on your IT staff. It also allows you to assign IAM roles to your active directory users and groups in AWS and in your existing on-premises, Microsoft Active Directory using an AD connector.

AWS audit and compliance services
- There are available AWS services that you can use to audit your resources and see who created which resources and when. These services can help you track the API operations or web actions across your company's AWS Cloud infrastructure. We will talk about the following services in this lecture: AWS CloudTrail, AWS Artifact, and AWS Security Hub. AWS CloudTrail is a service that's primarily used for IT audits. It tracks the user activity in API usage in your AWS account. It stores the data in an Amazon S3 Bucket owned by your AWS account or by other people. CloudTrail enables risk auditing by continuously monitoring and logging account activities, such as user actions in the AWS Management Console, AWS SDKs, APIs or AWS CLI. There are two types of events that can be logged in AWS CloudTrail. We have management events and data events. These events are also called Control Plane and Data Plane operations respectively. Management events provide information about the management operations performed on resources in your AWS account, such as attaching an IAM Role, creating a VPC or creating a subnet. Data events on the other hand provide information about the resource operations performed on or in a resource, like Amazon S3 object-level API activities, or invoking an AWS Lambda function. You can also receive CloudTrail log files from multiple AWS accounts by granting cross-account permissions to CloudTrail. Each account can deliver the log files to an S3 Bucket in your central account. AWS Artifact is a service that provides on-demand AWS security and compliance reports. It acts as a self-service portal to find compliance related information and reports about AWS, such as ISO reports, Payment Card Industry or PCI reports, Service Organization Control or SOC reports and certifications from accreditation bodies across the globe. You can use this to download AWS security and compliance documents, such as SOC 1 reports, ISO certifications, and other reports. AWS Security Hub is a service that provides a centralized and comprehensive view of the security posture of your cloud infrastructure across multiple AWS accounts. This service helps you to comply with specific security standards and best practices that your organization requires. Basically, it works like a hub that collects security alerts and findings from multiple AWS services, such as Amazon GuardDuty, Amazon Inspector, Amazon Macie, IAM Access Analyzer, AWS Firewall Manager and other sources. You can use the AWS Security Hub to comply with the payment card industry data security standard, or PCI DSS Center for Internet Security or CIS benchmarks and many other security standards.

IAM overview
- [Lecturer] The AWS Identity and Access Management service, or IAM for short, is a web service that helps your security control and manage access to various AWS resources. The two primary functions of the service are quite obvious based on its name. It's for managing the identity of your users and for providing access management to the resources and services available in your account. In other words, it handles the authentication and authorization aspects of your cloud infrastructure. The identity aspect of IAM helps you identify who are the validated users or entities in your organization. This is also known as authentication. An IAM identity can be an IAM user, an I am user group, or an IAM role. If you create a new AWS account, the very first user that will automatically exist will be the root user. A root user is accessed by signing in with the actual email address and password that you use to create the account. This user is quite powerful as it can change your account settings, access your billing details, or even close down your AWS account. It also allows you to create individual IAM users within your AWS account that corresponds to the users or the actual employees of your company. You can create an IAM user for your administrator, for your developers, for your network engineers, and so on. These IAM users are not separate AWS accounts, but are just regular users that exist within your AWS account. Let's now talk about the authorization function of the IAM service. An IAM user can be authorized to access and control your AWS resources. You can create a policy that contains multiple permissions that authorize the IAM user to do a set of actions. This is the access management aspect of the IAM service. You can manage the type of access your users should have by attaching an AWS-managed policy, a customer-managed policy, or an inline policy to different IAM identities. An AWS managed-policy is a standalone policy that is managed by AWS, whereas a customer-managed policy is a standalone type that you administer in your own AWS account. An inline policy is a type that's just embedded in an IAM user. This inline policy can be embedded in an IAM group or an IAM role. You can even attach multiple policies to a single IAM user. The IAM service is where you apply the concept of granting the least privilege. This concept simply states that should only grant defined set of permissions necessary for the entity to perform its designated task. By doing so, it ensures that no resource can execute an operation that it is not meant to do, thereby reducing the security risk of unauthorized actions. For example, you have a new DevOps engineer in your company that only needs access to the AWS CloudFormation templates to create multiple AWS resources. After creating an IAM user, what permissions should you provide without compromising security? At the very least, you should add the IAM user to an IAM group with a policy that only allows AWS CloudFormation actions. If the user does not need this access anymore, you can then easily remove him or her from that IAM user group. You can also create an IAM role that explicitly defines the CloudFormation permissions necessary for the new hire to properly do his or her job. This is a perfect example of following the best practice of granting the least privilege to our users. If you add this user to an IAM group with power user access or an administrator access IAM policy, then that's a security risk. This means that a DevOps engineer can do a lot of administrative actions that are out of scope and may adversely affect your resources. What's worse is if you had it over the account route user credentials. The user can certainly perform the necessary CloudFormation stack operations, but you're putting the security of your entire cloud infrastructure in imminent danger. Again, you should only grant the required permissions to your resources using the various policy types available in IAM. Aside from granting access to an individual person, you can also provide access to different AWS resources, such as your EC2 instances, S3 buckets, lambda functions, and others. For Amazon EC2, you can use the instance profile to pass a specific IAM role to your EC2 instance for it to perform certain actions like uploading data to Amazon S3 or other operations. These IAM rules attached to your instance can also be viewed on your EC2 metadata. If you have a Linux virtual machine, just type, curl http://169.254.169.254/latest/meta-data/iam/info to view the list of attached IAM roles. For Amazon S3, you can set up a bucket policy to grant IAM users in other AWS accounts the access permissions for your bucket and its objects. Say, you have multiple AWS accounts in your AWS organizations that represent different departments. If one department wants to share an Amazon S3 bucket with all other departments, you can set up an S3 bucket policy that allows cross-account access to other departments. You can also use IAM in your databases. For DynamoDB, you can design an IAM policy that allows access to put, update, and delete items in one specific table. This policy can be attached to an IAM role that can be used by lambda function or an ECS task to manage a particular DynamoDB table in your account. There's also a security feature called IAM DB Authentication for Amazon RDS and Aurora. This feature allows you to use IAM to centrally manage access to your database resources, eliminating the hassle of managing user access individually on each DB instance. It also improves the security of your application hosted in Amazon EC2 by not storing the database password and just using its instance profile to connect to Amazon RDS. For Amazon SQS, you can set up an access policy to control external access to your SQS queue. This is helpful if you need to grant permissions to an external company to access your queue, and SQS access policy can be prepared to allow the other company to poll the queue without giving up the permissions of your own account. In IAM, there are two primary types of policies that you can use. These are identity-based policy and resource-based policy. Basically, identity-based policies are attached to an IAM user, group, or role, while resource-based policies are attached to a resource like EC2 instances, S3 buckets, and others. You can also use permissions boundaries for your IAM users and roles. A permissions boundary is a feature that allows you to set the maximum permissions that an identity-based policy can grant to an IAM entity. With this, an entity can only perform the actions that are allowed by both its identity-based policies and its permissions boundaries. In our succeeding lectures, we will learn more about the Identity and Access Management service or IAM. We will discuss the different IAM identities, IAM policies, and its other concepts. We will also cover the unique evaluation logic of the IAM policies that control access to your AWS resources.

IAM identities
- [Instructor] You can control access to your AWS resources by using the AWS Identity and Access Management Service, or IAM. You can set up a user with a set of permissions that allows it to do certain functions in your AWS account through the use of IAM policies. A policy contains the list of permissions that allows an entity to do a series of tasks. It also includes certain restrictions that deny a set of actions based on a condition that you define. You can organize these users into a group and associate a policy on it for easier management. In this way, you don't have to manually assign permissions to individual users one by one. You can also configure a role to your AWS resources, such as your EC2 instance, Lambda function, or any other services, to enable them to perform certain actions. In IAM, there are three types of identities that you can use, namely, an IAM user, an IAM group, and IAM role. These identities are pretty self-explanatory. The IAM user is usually for an actual user, an IAM group is simply a group of IAM users, and an IAM role is literally a role that your AWS resources can assume. Let's discuss each of these types further in this lecture. An IAM user is an entity that represents an actual person or a service. You can create an IAM user that can interact with your AWS resources using the AWS Command Line Interface, AWS API, or through the AWS Management Web Console. The primary function of an IAM user is to provide someone the ability to sign in to the AWS Management Console of your account. With that web access, a user can do a variety of interactive tasks and may even invoke programmatic requests to your AWS services via the API or CLI. Basically, an IAM user consists of a name, a password, and an access key pair. It's name is just like a regular username, while the password is used for signing in to the AWS Management Console. The access key pair is composed of two components, which are the access key ID and a secret access key. These keys are used for programmatic access on the AWS CLI, AWS APIs, software development kits, or SDKs, and cloud development kits, or CDKs. You can grant permissions to your brand-new IAM user in various ways. You can directly attach existing permissions to it using an AWS-managed policy or a customer-managed policy. Obviously, an AWS-managed policy is managed by Amazon itself, whereas a customer-managed policy is managed by you. The latter is a custom policy that you can fully customize based on your requirements. The AWS-managed policies provide readily available job functions which are designed to closely align to the common job functions in the IT industry. These job functions could be an administrator, support user, security auditor, network administrator, developer power user, billing, and many others. So if you want to create another administrator to your AWS account, you can simply attach the administrator job function to your IAM user, or grant the developer power user for your software developer. An IAM user can be added to an IAM group, which makes the user inherit the group's policies and permissions. You can add a user to one or more IAM Groups. Speaking of which, let's now talk about IAM Groups. An IAM group has the following characteristics. First, it can contain multiple IAM users, and a single user can belong to multiple IAM groups. Second, these user groups cannot be nested. This means that an IAM group can only contain IAM users and not other IAM groups. And lastly, there's no default user group that automatically includes all of the IAM users in your AWS account. If you need to implement the setup, you must create it yourself and assign each new user to that group. As mentioned earlier, IAM groups lets you specify permissions for multiple users. This makes it easier for you to manage permissions for those users instead of granting them individually. For example, you can create a new IAM group named Tutorials Dojo Developers and then grant it with the related permissions that your development team requires. Any IAM user in that group automatically inherits the permissions that are assigned to that group. If a new developer joins her company, you can easily grant the appropriate permissions by simply adding that new user to an IAM group. Conversely, if an employee decides to leave your company and tendered his or her resignation, then you can simply remove his or her associated IAM user from that IAM group. It just makes your access control management easier. It eliminates the time-consuming need to manually edit a user's permissions. The third type is called an IAM role. This is an IAM identity that has specific permissions which can be assumed by your AWS resources. An IAM role is just like an IAM user but with just a few differences. Both of these AWS identities have permissions and policies that determine what a particular identity can and cannot do in your AWS account. An IAM user is uniquely associated with one, single person only, which means that it can only be used by that same person to access the AWS Management Console. In contrast, an IAM role is intended to be assumed by one or more AWS resources. So an EC2 instance can use the same IAM role that an AWS Lambda function uses to upload objects to an Amazon S3 bucket. That IAM role can also be used by an Amazon ECS task, Elastic Beanstalk application, and other AWS services. Another difference is that a role does not have long-term credentials, such as a web console password or a pair of access keys. It only provides temporary security credentials for the duration of that session. An IAM role can be used by a user that is in the same or different AWS account. If the role is used by another AWS account, then it is called a cross-account IAM role. It can also be used by a web service offered by AWS, such as your Amazon EC2 instances. An external user that is authenticated by an external identity provider or IGP Service can also use an IAM role. This can also be used by a web identity, OpenID Connect, or SAML 2.0 provider, including the Amazon Cognito service. There are also different types of roles as well. You can set up a cross-account role, an AWS service role, and an AWS service-linked role. A cross-account role grants access to resources in one account to a trusted principle in a different AWS account. IAM roles are the primary way to grant cross-account access to your multi-account cloud architecture. An AWS service role is what a particular AWS service assumes to perform the actions in your account on your behalf. Service roles are only limited within your AWS account and cannot be used to grant access to AWS services in other accounts. There's also a special type of service role that an application or a system running on EC2 instance can assume to perform certain actions. Applications running on that EC2 instance can retrieve temporary security credentials to access other AWS services or securely execute operations. Finally, an AWS service-linked role is a unique type of service role that is directly linked to an AWS Service. These service-linked roles are predefined by the service and contain all the permissions that the service requires to invoke other AWS services on your behalf.

IAM policy types
- [Lecturer] In this lecture, we'll discuss what a policy is and its different types. We will also cover how to use an IAM policy with your Amazon EC2 instances, Amazon S3, AWS organizations, and other AWS services. Let's start. In IAM, a policy is an object that defines the permissions of either an IAM identity or an AWS resource. As you know, an IAM identity can be an IAM user, an IAM group, or an IAM role. An AWS resource basically refers to the different AWS services that you can use in your account, such as EC2, S3, RDS, et cetera. An IAM policy contains permissions that explicitly allow or deny access to certain AWS services. It provides fine-grained access control to specific API actions, as well as the AWS resources that the policy should be applied to. For example, you can create a policy that only allows object upload to an S3 bucket by using the S3 PutObject API. You can also define a request condition that allows access to the API actions that you define only when the request comes from a specified IP address range. Alternatively, you can also add a request condition that requires the use of multifactor authentication, or MFA, for added security. Most IAM policies are stored as JSON documents, but you can also use a visual editor in the AWS Management console to easily generate a policy. A policy can be standalone or an inline type. The first one remains unchanged. Even if you delete its associated IAM identity. It doesn't have a strict one-to-one relationship to its associated IAM identity. In contrast, an inline policy will automatically be deleted if you also delete its associated identity. There are many IAM policy types that you can choose from. They are identity-based policies, resource-based policies, permissions boundaries, organizations SCPs, S3 access control lists, session policies, and many more. Let's first tackle identity-based policies. Basically, an identity-based policy is a type of policy that you attach to an IAM identity.
