As you know, an IAM identity can be an IAM user, an IAM group, or an IAM role. Identity-based policies can be further categorized into managed and inline policies. A managed policy is a standalone, identity-based policy that you can attach to multiple IAM users, groups, and roles in your AWS account. There are also two types of managed policies, which are the AWS-managed policies and the customer-managed policies. An AWS-managed policy is a type of policy that is created and managed by AWS, whereas a customer-managed policy is the one that you, as an AWS customer, manages. The latter provides more precise control over your IAM policies than AWS-managed policies. The other type of identity-based policy is called an inline policy. This type maintains a strict one-to-one relationship between a policy and an IAM identity. This policy will be gone automatically if you delete its associated IAM identity. Let's now move on to the second IAM policy type, called resource-based policies. This type attaches an inline policy to a specific AWS resource. The most common example of a resource-based policy is an Amazon S3 bucket policy. Basically a bucket policy grants IAM users and other AWS accounts access permissions for a bucket and its objects. It allows cross-account access to other AWS accounts in your organization. Aside from Amazon S3, you can also add a resource-based policy to your Amazon SQS queue, through the use of SQS access policies. This allows other AWS accounts or an external company to poll the queue while still maintaining control of your queue. An IAM role trust policy is also a type of resource-based policy. This is also called a trust relationship policy document, which declares the trusted entities that can assume the role as well as the access conditions for the role/ Resource-based policies grant permissions to the principal or an IAM identity that is specified in the policy. Principals can either be in the same AWS account as the resource or in other accounts. Moving on, let's talk about permissions boundaries. A permissions boundary is a policy that defines the maximum permissions that an identity-based policy can grant to an IAM entity, like an IAM user or an IAM role. This policy type does not explicitly grant permissions. It just sets a clear boundary to ensure that a given IAM policy can over-provision permissions to resources. This next policy type is related to AWS organizations. A service control policy, or SCP, is used by the AWS organization service to define the maximum permissions for account members of an organization or organizational unit. It limits permissions that identity-based policies or resource-based policies grant to the IAM users or roles within the account. One main difference between a regular IAM policy and an SAP is that an SAP cannot explicitly grant permissions, unlike an IAM policy. The other type of policy that we will discuss is related to Amazon S3. An access control list, or an ACL, is a feature in S3 that controls which principals in other AWS accounts can access a particular bucket. An ACL works like a resource-based policy, since an AWS resource is using this policy, ACLs are cross-account permission policies that grant certain permissions to a specified principal that you define. This one doesn't use a regular JSON policy document structure, unlike the rest of the policy types. These ACLs cannot grant permissions to entities within the same account. Lastly, let's talk about session policies. A session policy limits the permissions that an identity-based policy grants to a particular session. Just like permissions boundaries, a session policy just sets a limit of what kind of permission a session has, without granting any permissions. Aside from an identity-based policy, the permissions of a session policy can also come from a resource-based policy. If there's an explicit deny in any of these policies, then it'll effectively override any allowed permissions.

IAM policy basics
- [Lecturer] In this lecture we'll discuss the anatomy of an IAM policy and the different examples that you can use for your own AWS resources. You already know that a policy is an entity that contains permissions, that allow an identity or a resource to execute certain API operations or tasks. An IAM policy is defined in a JavaScript object notation or JSON policy document, which basically includes these two components, a policy-wide information and one or more individual statements. The policy-wide information is an optional element that is placed on top of the document. There are two policy-wide elements. The ID and the version element. The ID uniquely identifies the IAM policy while the version just specified the policy access language version. Again, the version element pertains to the AWS policy language and not the version of your custom policy. This element usually has a static value of 2012-10-17, which may pertain to the release date of the current AWS policy access language. The second component of an IAM policy is called a statement. A single policy can have one or more individual statements. If a policy includes multiple statements, then AWS implements a logical OR across the statements when evaluating them. A statement has the following elements, SID, effect, principal, action, resource, and condition. SID stands for statement ID, which is an identifier to help AWS differentiate your statements. This element is optional, so you may or may not add this in your statement. The second one is effect. This element only has two possible values, allow or deny. This explicitly indicates whether the policy allows or denies access to a given resource. This is a mandatory field that you have to supply. The third element is the principal which refers to a particular IAM identity that you define. Before you use this, keep in mind that there is a constraint in using this element. This is only mandatory if you're creating an identity-based policy. If you create a resource-based policy, then you must indicate this specific AWS account, IAM user, IAM role, or the federated user, which you would like to allow or deny access. Take note that you cannot include this element if you're creating an IAM permissions policy to attach a specific user or role. In an IAM policy, the current IAM user or role is implied to be the principal element by default. The fourth one is called the action element. This includes a list of actions or APIs that the policy allows or denies. For example, you can allow a user to view the list of buckets in Amazon S3 by adding the S3 list bucket API. For DynamoDB, you can select the approved APIs that your resource can use, such as the PutItem, UpdateItem or DeleteItem actions. You can also add a wildcard using an asterisk. So for example, you can allow all DynamoDB eBay actions by defining a value of DynamoDB: asterisk in the action element. A wildcard gives access to all the actions or APIs of this specific AWS service. For example, the value of S3: asterisk means that it applies to all actions in Amazon S3. Let's now move on to the resource element. This one similar to the principal element, because it has certain constraints as well. If you create an IAM permissions policy, then you must specify a list of resources to which the eBay actions apply. This element is also optional if you create a resource-based policy. You can even use wildcards here with asterisk and question mark characters. Essentially, an asterisk represents a combination of multiple characters where as the question mark wildcard represents any single character. You can create a policy that refers to all the items that are within a specific Amazon S3 bucket. For example, you can use a wildcard that affects all the objects in your Amazon S3. To implement this, you have to use the Amazon resource name or the ARN of the S3 bucket and then append an asterisk after it. So if you have a bucket named to tutorialsdojo, you can set the value of arn:aws:s3::: tutorialsdojo/ asterisk in the resource element to fulfill this task. You can also specify an actual DynamoDB table as a resource element. Just like the previous one, just use the ARN of the AWS resource and then add the DynamoDB table name. So if you have a DynamoDB table named Books in the US-East-1 region, you can add its ARN and then append it with /Books. It can be written as arn:aws: dynamodb-us-east-1 ::/table/Books. The last element is called a condition. This one is an optional element that specifies the preliminary rules under which the policy grants permissions. You can craft permissions using condition operators to match certain condition keys or values. There are many categories of condition operators available in IAM. You can use a string, a numeric, a date, a Boolean, a binary, an ARN, an IfExists initial operator, and many more. Each of these categories has different conditional operators. For instance, a string operator can be StringEquals, StringNotEquals, StringLike, and many others. The same goes for the numeric, date, Boolean, binary and other conditional operators. You can also append an IfExists operator to the end of any condition operator name. So for example, you can use the StringEqualsIfExists for a string, NumericEqualsIfExists for numeric, or BoolIfExists for the Boolean side. This operator is like setting a condition that if a policy key is present in the context of the request, it then proceeds in processing the key as specified in the policy. In the event that the key is not present, then IAM will evaluate and consider the condition element as true. These conditional operators are quite useful in creating fine-grained access to AWS resources. For example, you can share an Amazon S3 bucket with an external vendor while ensuring that the bucket owner must still be able to access all the objects. This can be done by adding a condition element with a StringEquals conditional operator. You can use the S3:x-amz-acl conditional key in S3, which refers to a canned ACL or also known as a predefined grant. You can match this with a canned ACL type of bucket owner full control to ensure that the bucket owner is able to access all of the S3 objects. You can also use this for checking the multi-factor authentication or MFA in your account. Say for example, you want to allow all actions in your S3 bucket except for the Put operation, if MFA is not enabled. To meet this requirement, you can use a Boolean conditional operator called BoolIfExists to check if your MFA feature is enabled or not. This can be done by using a special type of key called the AWS global condition context key, such as the AWS:MultiFactorAuthPresent key. The MFA feature is enabled if the value of this context key is true. Conversely, the feature is disabled if the value of the key is false. So for this one, you must add a condition element with a BoolIfExists conditional operator. Then add the AWS:MultiFactorAuthPresent global condition context key with the value of false.

IAM policy evaluation logic
- [Instructor] A single IAM policy can have one or more individual statements. This different statements contain permissions that may allow or deny operations for given identity or resource. This is a powerful access management tool that you can use to provide fine grain control over your AWS resources and internal systems. AWS implements a logical OR across the multiple statements when evaluating them. You can also explicitly deny your permissions so it can override the explicit allow statements. However, there are times that one statement might contradict another statement in your IAM policy. In this lecture, we'll briefly talk about the policy evaluation logic in the AWS Identity and Asset Management Service and how it evaluates a policy if there are certain conflicts in one of its statements. Let's start. AWS completes a series of steps to determine whether to allow or deny a certain request. Usually, it'll first authenticate the principle that makes the request. This step may not be required if the resource allows anonymous request like an Amazon S3 bucket. It'll then process the request context or information gathered in the request to determine the specific policies that apply to the request. Finally, it evaluates all the policy types in the account that may affect the exact order in which the policies are evaluated. If the IAM policies are within a single account, the following high level summary of the AWS evaluation logic applies. First, all requests will be implicitly denied. The exception to this rule is the AWS account root user, which has full access. This is followed by an explicit allow in the identity-based or research-based policy. The IAM service might override the explicit allow with an implicit deny, if there's a permissions boundary, a service control policy, or a sessions policy present in your account. Ultimately, an explicit deny in any policy overrides any type of allow actions, even if it is explicitly declared. AWS provides a detailed flow chart that shows details of how a policy is evaluated. The flow is just like what I've mentioned earlier. All decisions starts with an implicit deny. Then it goes to check the service control policies or SAPs of your AWS organization, the research-based policies, the IAM permissions boundaries, the session policies, and lastly, the identity-based policies. This is a handy diagram that you can use to understand the evaluation logic in IAM. Let's discuss some of the examples of an IAM policy with contradicting statements. Look at this IAM policy that has two statements. The first statement allows the EC2 terminate instances API action with a condition element that checks the source IP address of the requester. This statement means that if a user has an IP address that is within the CIDR block of 49.147.194.0 slash 24, then it can terminate the EC2 instances in any AWS region of your account. This seems to be straightforward policy, but if you look at the second statement, you'll see that there's a conflict between the two. The second one is explicitly denying all EC2 actions plus a string not equals condition element that checks if the region of the Amazon EC2 instance is not located in the US West, Northern California region. In this case, what will be the outcome of your request? Can you directly terminate an instance on any region as long as you have an IP address that's within the prefix 24 CIDR block? Will you be able to terminate an instance using any IP address as long as it's not in the US West-1 region? Or will you be able to terminate an EC2 instance as long as it is in the US West-1 region and your IP is within the given CIDR block? To better understand this, think of these statements as two overlapping circles of a Venn diagram. The final permissions that will be in effect will be the combination of these two statements. Keep in mind that an explicit deny always overrides any type of allow action. Armed with this logic, let's evaluate this policy one more time. The first statement just states that you can terminate any EC2 instance as long as you have an IP address based on the 49.147.194.0 prefix 24 CIDR block. Notably, there's no restriction on the AWS region of the instance. So whether the EC2 instance is in US West-1, US West-2, US East-1, US East-2 or any other region, the incoming request will still be fulfilled as long as it has the correct source IP address. Now for the second statement, you will see that there's an explicit deny that prohibits any EC2 action based on a certain condition. This condition is important since it'll determine if it'll process the second statement or not. Here, the condition key is string not equals, which means that if the EC2 region is not equal to US West-1, then the request will be denied. In other words, the EC2 termination request will be allowed if the EC2 instance is located in the US West-1 region. Other regions such as US East-1, US East-2, et cetera, will be blocked. Therefore, this policy will allow you to terminate an EC2 instance in the US West-1 region as long as your source IP is within the 49.147.194.0 prefix 24 CIDR block. Let's look at another example here. This time we have two different policies. The first one is an allow statement that has an action element containing EC2 colon asterisk and ds colon asterisk prefixes. ds stands for the AWS directory service. This means that it allows all API actions of the AWS Directory Service and Amazon EC2 services. Attaching this policy will enable an IAM identity to create, read, update, and delete EC2 instances and ds directories. The second policy has an explicit deny that prohibits any delete operations on all of your AWS directly service resources. In this example, there will be no changes in the EC2 permissions of the first policy since it has no conflict with the second policy. You can still delete any EC2 instance in your AWS account. The issue here is the conflict between the two directory service permissions. The second policy negates the allow permission provided by the first policy for deleting instances. This is because an explicit deny carries more weight than an explicit allow statement. Hence, you can only create, read and update AWS directories, but not delete them. Let's try out one more policy to further understand the IAM policy evaluation logic. Suppose you were given this IAM policy with two different statements. The first one allows all AWS lambda API actions, while the second explicitly denies the create function and delete function APIs. In addition, it also has a conditional element that checks the source IP address of the request. This statement simply means that if the request comes from a CIDR range of 220.200.16.0 prefix 24, then the IAM identity won't be able to create or delete any lambda function. In this example, what do you think will happen? Does the first policy override the second policy, or is it the other way around? Don't forget the important rule in IAM that says an explicit deny in any policy overrides any type of allow actions, even if it is explicitly declared. Thus, the deny action of the second policy will override the explicit allow of the second policy. This configuration will allow an AWS lambda function to be deleted as long as the IP address of the request doesn't fall under the 220.200.16.0 prefix 24 IP range. If you use a different computer with an IP address of 10.100.0.20, then you'll be able to create and delete your lambda functions.

Amazon Cognito user pool
- [Narrator] There are two components that make up Amazon Cognito, Cognito user pools, and identity pools. In this lecture, we're going to be looking at Cognito user pools. An Amazon Cognito user pool is a managed user directory service that provides signup and sign-in functionalities to your web and mobile applications. With user pool, you can handle the authentication of users via the Cognito user pool APIs. So this truly takes a lot of development overhead on your part as you no longer have to code a backend service for a login system yourself. If you don't want to build a sign-in UI for your application, perhaps you're working on a pet project and just want to get it done. Cognitive user pool also has a built-in user interface for signing in users. This hosted UI is customizable. You can change the way it looks. You can modify the size and color buttons in input fields, and you can even upload your own brand logo if you want. Aside from that, Cognitive user pool helps you easily implement best practices in securing your application, like enabling multi-factor authentication, checking for compromised credentials, account takeover protection, and phone and email verification. You can also enforce the creation of strong passwords for your users by adjusting the password requirements. Users can also be authenticated using external identity providers such as Amazon, Facebook, and Google, as well as a supported SAML authentication provider such as Microsoft Active Directory. For example, if a new user visits your page and he already has a Google account, he may sign into your application using that account rather than creating a new one in your user pool. Cognito will manage the interactions with these providers, leaving it to only focus on handling the authentication tokens on your app. Here is a sample workflow of how Cognito user pools work. First, a user sends an authentication request using his username and password to Amazon Cognito. Then Cognito verifies the identity of the user by looking up its profile from a user pool. If the user log in via a third party identity provider, Cognito will route the request to the corresponding identity provider. Once the user has been verified, the user receives a JSON web token. Finally, the user can use this token to gain access to your backend resources. For example, it can be used as an authorizer for a REST API hosted in API gateway, or it can be exchanged for temporary credentials to gain access to your AWS resources, such as an S3 bucket via an identity pool.

Amazon Cognito identity pool
- [Instructor] Now let's look at identity pools. Amazon Cognito identity pools provide a different functionality compared to user pools. If user pools are used for authentication, then identity pools are used for user authorization or access control. Identity pools are used for obtaining temporary AWS credentials to access other AWS services, such as Amazon S3 or DynamoDB. So how do we get temporary AWS credentials? Here's the user pool authentication flow from the last video. So after a user has been successfully verified, Cognito aids adjacent web token and returns it to the client. Now, the role of user pool stops at this point and identity pool takes over. So from the client, you will use the AWS SDK to send a request to Cognito Identity Pool. Then Identity Pool, which uses AWS STS under the hood exchanges the JSON web token for temporary AWS credentials that can be used to grant your users permissions to access AWS resources in your accounts. When you create an identity pool, you get to choose an authentication provider, which could be Cognito user pool, Amazon, Google, Twitter, Open Id Connect Provider, a SAML IDP, or you could also authenticate users via your own authentication process using the developer authenticated identities feature. Take note that an identity pool can work without a user pool. If you want to get the token from an identity provider like Google, it's fine. You don't have to use Cognito user pool. However, in the exam, user pool and identity pool usually come up together in scenarios or options where they're combined to form a solution. Amazon Cognito Identity Pool supports two types of identities, authenticated and unauthenticated. Authenticated identities are users who are authenticated by a trusted identity provider, such as Cognito User Pool, Amazon, Facebook, Twitter and many more. Unauthenticated identities on the other hand, simply refer to the guest users or users who don't have to be logged in to access your application. If you have an application that serves media files like images or videos from Amazon S3, you may want to have some control over what your users can and cannot do. For example, you can make it in such a way that only registered users can upload and share images, while guest users are only allowed to view photos posted by those who actually have an accounts, kind of like how Facebook and Instagram work. Obviously, the permissions that you assign to guest users should be more restrictive than those four authenticated identities. You can enable an authenticated identities upon the creation of an identity pool or by modifying the setting of an existing identity pool. Cognito Identity Pool with unauthenticated access works by providing a unique identifier and AWS credentials for your guest users. You can control their permissions by defining the policy associated with your unauthenticated identity's role. You can, for example, set read only access in the policy so that any guest user can only see media files from your S3 bucket. As a result, they won't be able to delete, update, or post any files to the S3 buckets.

AWS Secrets Manager overview
- [Instructor] AWS Secrets Manager is a secret management service that enables you to easily rotate, manage, and retrieve database credentials, API keys, and other secrets throughout their lifecycle. As a best practice, you should avoid keeping sensitive information such as database credentials or API keys in plain text, and they shouldn't be accessible or hardcoded in your code as well. It is also recommended to rotate passwords or keys on a regular basis, which can be easily overlooked when keys are maintained manually. If you are in charge of storing and managing secrets, as well as ensuring that your application complies with regulatory and compliance standards, you may use Secrets Manager to accomplish all of these duties from one central location. As a developer, Secrets Manager can assist you in protecting and rotating application credentials without too much overhead on your side. Instead of storing your database connection details in the application hosts, you can save it in Secrets Manager and just pull it and reference it in your code. A secret refers to the resources that you save and retrieve in Secrets Manager. It can consist of a database username and password, a key value pair, or even a JSON documents. Secrets Manager also lets you keep a lengthy string of secrets, allowing for managing certificates with a long chain of trusts. Although you can save API Keys, JSON objects, and other stuff, storing database credentials is where Secrets Manager is particularly useful. Secrets Manager has built in integration for Amazon RDS, Amazon Redshift, and Amazon DocumentDB. What this means for you is that you no longer have to write a Lambda function to rotate secrets, because Secrets Manager natively supports the rotation of credentials for these services. Secrets Manager also supports some relational databases outside of RDS, such as a MySQL database hosted on an EC2 instance or on-premises. However, to rotate credentials for other databases, key value peers, and JSON Objects, you have to author a custom Lambda function that will handle the rotation of those credentials. Secrets Manager uses AWS KMS by default to enforce encryption at rest. Thus, storing data in plaintext is not an option. When creating a secret, you must choose a KMS key. If you don't have a CMK, it's fine. Secrets Manager will encrypt your secret with a default encryption key that it manages. So how do you retrieve a secret? When you create a secret, you associate a secret name with it. These secret name simply acts as a pointer to a secret that you can refer to in your application code. So for example, instead of hardcoding your database credentials, you can replace those with codes that utilize the get secret value API to pull the secrets at runtime. Of course, we don't want to call this API every time your application needs to access a secret, because that is inefficient and will lead you to hit the API limit faster. As a workaround, Secrets Manager provides a client side library that you can work with to cache your secrets locally instead of retrieving them directly from Secrets Manager. Another useful application of Secrets Manager is when you're deploying RDS DB instances via a cloud formation template. For example, a cloud formation template that launches an RDS DB instance might look like this. As you can see, the username and password for this database are exposed just like that in plaintext. This could be a potential security hole, for example, if you accidentally upload this template in public, like in your GitHub repository. So a more secure approach is to replace the plaintext with a Secrets Manager dynamic reference to retrieve the secrets values that you need from Secret Manager.

AWS Systems Manager Parameter Store overview
- [Lecturer] Parameter Store is a part of the application management tools offered by the AWS Systems Manager Service or SSM. Parameter Store allows you to create key volume parameters where you can store your application configurations, custom environment variables, product keys and credentials on a single location. Unlike Secrets Manager, the Parameter Store is designed to cater to a wider range of use cases, not only for passwords or database credentials. If you want to store parameters that don't require encryption, such as AMI IDs, security group IDs, or URLs, then Parameter Store is the better tool for the job. In the Parameter Store, there is a concept of a parameter hierarchy. A parameter hierarchy is nothing but the way of organizing parameters in the same way that you organize your files on your computer. So for example, under your user folder, you might have different folders that store different files. You might have a pictures folder for images, a movies folder for your movie collection, and a document folder for documents, spreadsheets, or PDFs. The idea is similar to that of a parameter hierarchy, except that instead of organizing files, you organize parameters. A parameter hierarchy is helpful in determining the right parameter for a job, preventing you from accidentally using the wrong parameter. To understand this better, let's take a look at an example. Suppose that you have two endpoints for MySQL databases, one for the prod, and one for the dev environments. You can create a parameter hierarchy for each endpoint like this. The hierarchy follows a slash separated naming convention. With this, we can easily keep all prod-related parameters separated from the dev environments. In this example, the top key is the environment name, followed by DB, which denotes that this is a database parameter. And finally, the actual parameter, which is the database endpoints. Also, by organizing your parameters in this manner, you can retrieve multiple parameters at once under a certain path level. For example, you can grab all database parameters that you have at the dev level by using the /Dev/DB/path, when you call the get parameters by path API command. you can specify your parameter as a String, a StringList, or a SecureString. The string data type, as the name implies, can be any block of text that you wish to store unencrypted. In the StringList, you specify strings separated by commas, and if you have sensitive information such as passwords or license keys, you can store them as a SecureString data type. The SecureString data type uses AWS KMS to encrypt your parameters. AWS KMS uses either a customer-managed CMK or an AWS-managed CMK when encrypting the parameter volume. Parameter Store doesn't have a built-in password rotation feature, so this is something that you have to build on your own. There are also two tiers that you can choose from when creating a parameter. The first one is the standard tier, and the second is the advanced tier. The standard tier has a limit of 10,000 parameters and a parameter value size limit of up to four kilobytes. If your use case is within these limits, then good for you, because storing data on the standard tier comes at no additional fee. The advanced tier, on the other hand, can have more than 10,000 parameters and a parameter value size limit of up to eight kilobytes. This one has an additional charge that you have to pay monthly. For both tiers, you pay for the API interactions.

AWS Systems Manager Parameter Store vs. AWS Secrets Manager
- [Instructor] In this video, we'll do a side-by-side comparison of AWS Secrets Manager and Systems Manager Parameter Store to help you understand and choose what best fits your given security requirements. When you're dealing with security, you're not just looking to encrypt user data. Oftentimes, it also extends to protecting the application itself. And one aspect of application security is how the parameters, such as environment variables, database credentials, API keys, product keys, and so on, are stored and retrieved. Both AWS Systems Manager Parameter Store and AWS Secrets Manager can help you manage and secure these types of data. Parameter Store and Secrets Manager are two distinct services but offer similar functionalities that allow you to centrally manage and secure your secret information. In the Parameter Store, a standard parameter can hold up to 4,096 characters, which are equivalent to four kilobytes for each entry, while an advanced parameter can store up to eight kilobytes per entry. Secrets Manager, on the other hand, allows you to store secrets of up to 64 kilobytes in size, making it ideal for use cases such as maintaining certificates with a lengthy chain of trusts. In terms of encryption, both services use AWS KMS to encrypt data. Parameter Store was designed to handle a broader range of use cases, including not just secrets and passwords, but also application configuration variables such as URLs, database endpoints, configuration settings, product keys, and so on. And for that reason, the default choice for generating a parameter is a plain text string value. But you can also encrypt parameters if you want to by storing them as secure stream. Conversely, Secrets Manager is purposely built for protecting and encrypting sensitive information, which is why it enforces encryption by default. So you cannot store data in plain text. Both services have a versioning feature. This allows you to view previous versions of your parameters in case you needed them. You can choose to restore the older version of the parameter. Parameter Store only allows one version of the parameter to be active at any given time, while Secrets Manager allows multiple versions to exist at the same time when you are performing a secret rotation. Secrets Manager distinguishes between different versions by the staging labels. Another unique feature of Secrets Manager that is not available in the Parameter Store is its ability to rotate secrets automatically. AWS Secrets Manager offers the ability to switch secrets at any given time and can be configured to regularly rotate depending on your requirements. Secrets Manager also offers cross account access and has built-in integration for Amazon RDS, Document DB, and Redshift for rotating database credentials. The Secrets Manager can also generate passwords for you, which is another helpful feature when you want to create a resource via cloud formation without hard coding the password in the templates. With all of these additional features, storing secrets in Secrets Manager comes at a higher cost than Parameter Store. There's no additional charge for storing data as a standard parameters. However, there is a cost associated with advanced parameters. You also pay for the API interactions. AWS Secrets Manager, on the other hand, charges a fixed monthly fee for each secret and for a fixed number of API calls.

Amazon S3 encryption
- [Instructor] There are two methods by which you can encrypt data on Amazon S3. The first method is called server-side encryption, whereas the second is known as client-side encryption. The idea behind server-side encryption is when you upload an object to an S3 bucket, Amazon S3 will encrypt it first before saving it to disk storage. Conversely, when you download an encrypted object, Amazon S3 will first decrypt it before returning the object to you. So in short, Amazon S3 handles data encryption and decryption on your behalf. This process of encrypting data prior to saving it to a disk is called encryption at rest. This protects your data from an authorized access or data theft in general. Aside from encryption at rest, there is what we call encryption in transit. Encryption in transit refers to the act of encrypting data as it travels from a source to a target. The goal of encryption in transit is to prevent your data from being intercepted in plain text as it moves from one end to the other. Keep in mind that Amazon S3 accepts both HTTP and HTTPS requests. So if you upload data to S3 via HTTP and use server-side encryption, your data will only be encrypted once it reaches Amazon S3, meaning it's still vulnerable to man in the middle attacks. You can encrypt data in transit by making an HTTPS request to Amazon S3 or by using client-side encryption. Server-side encryption in S3 can be done in three ways. We have server-side encryption using KMS or simply SSS-KMS server-side encryption with Amazon S3 managed keys or SSE-S3 and lastly, SSE-C or server-side encryption. Using customer managed keys. In SSE-KMS, you can either provide an AWS managed CMK for S3, or a customer managed CMK that you can manage, rotate, disable, and audit its usage. Amazon S3 will then perform envelope encryption using the CMK that you provide. So under the hood, S3 will generate a data key using your CMK and use that to encrypt incoming data. Finally, S3 stores the cipher text version of that data key alongside your data. Keep in mind that in order to encrypt an object with a customer managed CMK, you need to add the KMS generate data key and KMS decrypt permissions to the key policy associated with your CMK. If you're using the AWS managed CMK for S3, then there's no need to do this. When you upload an object to S3 via rest APA endpoints, you can include the XAMZ server site encryption header with a value of AWKS-KMS to let Amazon S3 know that you want to encrypt the object using SSE-KMS. You can then specify your CMK ID in the XAMZ server site encryption, AWKS-KMS key ID header. If you're using the AWS-ELI or AWS-SDK, these headers are wrapped in built-in function parameters, which means you don't have to explicitly add them to your requests because the SDK client does it for you. You could also enforce the encryption of objects by adding a condition to your bucket policy that denies any requests that don't contain the XAMZ server-side encryption header with AWKS-KMS as its value. Okay, so that's it for SSE-KMS. Let's go to SSE-S3. In SSE-S3, Amazon S3 uses an AES-256 KMS key that it owns and manages. Keep in mind that this is different from the AWS manage CMK. The KMS key that we're pertaining to here is an AWS owned CMK. If you remember, I mentioned this when we discussed KMS. You don't have access to this type of CMK, you cannot view it in the KMS console, and you cannot read or edit its key policy. Just like SSE-KMS, Amazon S3 manages the encryption and decryption of your data. If you wish to enforce the encryption of objects uploaded to an S3 buckets, you can do so by using the same XAMZ server-side encryption header in your bucket policy. But this time, you give it a value of a AES-256. This way, S3 will deny all requests that don't include an XAMZ server-side encryption header. Next is SSEC. In SSEC, Amazon S3 will still manage the encryption and decryption of data for you. However, you have to provide the actual encryption key along with the data as part of your requests. Amazon S3 will encrypt your data using the key that you sent and remove it from memory afterwards. To retrieve the encrypted data, you must include the same encryption key in your request, so Amazon S3 can decrypt it. Amazon S3 does not explore your encryption keys. You're responsible for managing it, meaning if you lose your encryption keys, you lose your data. When you use SSEC, you must include three encryption key information with your request headers. These are the XAMZ server-side encryption customer algorithm, XAMZ server-side encryption customer key, and XAMZ server-side encryption, customer key MD5. Again, as I said before, the AWS-SDK clients and AWS-CLI provides wrapper APIs for these headers. However, this might come up in the exam, so you have to remember them. Take note that SSEC only accepts a AES-256 keys and rejects requests made over HTTP, so it only accepts HTTPS requests. As a result, in a way, it enforces encryption in transit and encryption at rest. Finally, we have client-side encryption. In client-side encryption, data is encrypted first before sending it to Amazon. S3 does not handle encryption and decryption for you. The management of keys, data encryption and decryption are all your responsibility. You can use a third party library in your application to help you with the encryption and decryption of data as you send and retrieve them. As a matter of fact, AWS has one. It's a client-side library called AWS Encryption SDK, and you can use it to more easily apply encryption best practices in Amazon S3.

Amazon S3 bucket policy
- [Instructor] In this lecture, we're going to talk about S3 bucket policies. Before anything else, it's worth noting that there are two resource-based policies that you can use to manage access to an Amazon S3 bucket. The first one is called access control list, or ACL, which is less commonly used. ACL is a legacy feature that provides a simple way of granting access to buckets and objects. The second one is the bucket policy, which we will discuss in a short while. As a general rule, AWS recommends using S3 bucket policies over ACLs for controlling access. An S3 bucket policy is a JSON document that you can attach to an S3 bucket to specify which principals you are allowing or denying access to an S3 bucket and the objects inside it. You also get to select the S3 actions that a principal is allowed to do. There are a lot of S3 actions available, thus, with the S3 bucket policy, you can do far more than simply granting PutObject or GetObject permission. S3 bucket policies cover a broader range of restrictions than ACLs. You can create granular rules with it at the object level, or you could also write more advanced rules using conditions. An S3 bucket policy is written and works almost similar to an IAM policy that you attach to an IAM user or IAM role. The difference is, in an S3 bucket policy, it is mandatory to define the principal or principals to which you'd like to allow or deny access. Also, take note that when you create an S3 bucket, by default, it won't contain any bucket policies. Here's an example of an S3 bucket policy that allows all objects of the tdojo buckets to be accessed publicly. The first element is the SID, or a statement ID, and this helps us easily read and identify what a statement is for rather than going through the action, effect, principal, or resource to understand the purpose of a statement. This is optional. You may skip it for basic use cases, but it will be quite useful if you have multiple statements in the policy. This SID is called PublicRead, and it does exactly what it says. The second one is Effect. This element explicitly dictates whether the policy allows or denies access to an S3 bucket or the objects is stored in it. The Effect only accepts an Allow or a Deny value. This is a required field that you must fill out. In this bucket policy, it has a value of Allow. The third element is Action, and this includes a list of actions that the policy allows or denies. In this case, the sample bucket policy is allowing the GetObject method, which means that you're allowing S3 objects in these buckets to be read. Next, we have the Resource. The ARN of the S3 buckets to which the bucket policy is attached is specified here. The asterisk denotes that all objects in the tdojo buckets can be read publicly. If you only want to expose a particular object or a set of objects, you can do that by specifying the path for those objects. For example, you may only allow all images to be read from these S3 buckets using this path style. The fifth element is the Principal. This refers to a specific IAM identity where it could also be an anonymous user if the website hosting feature for the S3 buckets is enabled. In this bucket policy, the asterisk value means that you're allowing all users to access all objects from these buckets. We can also have an optional condition elements. This specifies the preliminary rules under which the policy grants permission. What if, for example, you just want to accept reads from your public IP and block all requests from other IPs? It is impossible to list all IPs out there, so what you can do is take a blacklist approach, which could look something like this. This bucket policy has a condition of NotIpAddress, with a key of aws:SourceIp, and an IP address value of 180.123.156.13. You could also specify a range of IPs here by writing them inside their formats. Okay, so this statement implies that we're denying read requests to the tdojo buckets from all IPs except for 180.123.156.13. This means that a Get operation from any user is denied unless the request originates from the IP address or addresses as specified in the condition.

AWS Key Management Service (AWS KMS) overview
- [Instructor] Now let's talk about AWS Key Management Service or KMS. AWS Key Management Service or KMS is a managed service that makes it easy for you to create, rotate, enable, and disable encryption keys. When you think of data encryption in AWS, think of KMS. But first, what is encryption? Encryption can be defined as the process of converting plain text data into an unreadable format or also known as cipher text. Obviously, this is done to hide the original meaning of the message so that only the person or party to whom it is addressed can read and understand it. An encryption key is basically what you would use to encrypt and decrypt data. With AWS, KMS creating an encryption key is as straightforward as clicking a button or calling an API. Aside from that, you can manage access to an encryption key via the key policy. A key policy is a resource-based policy that you attach to a KMS key to control who can use that key and what KMS actions a user can perform to it. You can also use CloudTrail to track, which IAM user made an API request using a KMS key. This is useful for auditing key usage or troubleshooting issues where a KMS key was misused. We refer to the KMS key as the customer master key. A customer master key is a primary resource in KMS, which serves as your master or root key. KMS supports symmetric and asymmetric encryption. Symmetric encryption uses a single key for both encryption and decryption. It means that the encryption key that you use to encrypt data is the same key to be used for decryption. A symmetric CMK represents a 256 bit AES key that never leaves AWS KMS unencrypted. AWS services that are integrated with AWS KMS like Amazon DynamoDB and Amazon S3 use symmetric CMK to encrypt and decrypt data. And asymmetric CMK on the other hand, uses a related key pair, a public key, and a private key. The public key is only used for encrypting data and in order to decrypt it, you have to use the private key. You can generate an RSA or an elliptic curve key pair in KMS. Asymmetric CMKS are usually used for signing and verification. So for example, suppose that a client sends you a digitally signed contract form, you must ensure that the information within the contract has not been altered by third parties. If you have the right key, you can cryptographically verify whether or not the contract has been tampered with. The exam doesn't usually ask questions on asymmetric CMKs, so from here on out, we'll talk more about symmetric CMKs. A CMK is capable of encrypting data up to four kilobytes in size, making it not suitable for large data sizes. To encrypt data more than four kilobytes as you most likely will, you'll need to use a process called envelope encryption. Envelope encryption is not specific to KMS, but when we talk about envelope encryption in the context of KMS, it refers to the process of creating a data key using a CMK. Then you can use the data key outside of KMS to encrypt plain text data or another data key. You can achieve multiple layers of security by encrypting a data key under another key, and then encrypting that key under another key, and so on. The catch is there should always be a top level key that remains in plain text, so you can eventually decrypt your data keys and your plain text data. In KMS, the top level key is your CMK. There are three types of CMK. The first is the customer managed CMKs. These are the CMKs that you create, own, and manage in your AWS accounts. By default, KMS uses a managed key material, which is a secret string of bits using a cryptographic algorithm to generate a CMK. You can, however, import your own key material to create a CMK if you want to. The benefit of relying on KMS key material is that you can enable its automatic rotation, which is done annually. You are charged monthly for each CMK that you create, plus the KMS API operations that you've consumed. Also, take note that you cannot export a CMK created by AWS KMS. Next is the AWS managed CMK. These are CMKs that are created, managed, and use on your behalf by an AWS service. You do not pay a monthly fee for AWS managed CMKs and the key material for these CMKs are set to rotate automatically every three years. Last is the AWS owned CMK. They are part of a collection of CMKs that AWS owns and manages for use in multiple AWS accounts. AWS services can use AWS' own CMKs to protect your data. You can view, manage, or use AWS' own CMKs or audit their use. Under the hood, KMS uses a hardware security module that has been validated under FIPs 140-2 level two to generate in-store KMS keys. The Federal Information Processing Standard publication, or FIPs, is a standard that governs the approval of cryptographic modules like HSM. There are four FIPS levels that represent how strong the security level of an HSM is, making four the highest level. A hardware security module is a dedicated tamper-resistant computing device that uses physical processes to generate strong encryption keys. It can store encryption keys in perform cryptographic operations like encryption and decryption. HSMs can be really expensive, but with KMS we don't have to buy it as it is offered as a service. When you use AWS KMS, you share tenancy access to HSM with other customers. This means that other customers may be using the same HSM device as you, and it is AWS's job to make sure that the access of these customers is separated logically so there won't be any problem. If you wish to procure exclusive single tenant access to HSM, that is FIPs 140-2 level three validated. Use AWS cloud HSM instead.

AWS KMS API commands
- [Instructor] Now let's look at the most commonly used KMS API commands that you would most likely encounter in the exam. The first command is encrypt, and what this does is uses your CMK to encrypt plain text data into cipher text. Please keep in mind that the encrypt operation can only be used to encrypt small amounts of data, such as user IDs, passwords, or database credentials; any data under four kilobytes. If you need to encrypt large documents, then you have to use envelope encryption. As we discussed in the previous video, envelope encryption in the context of KMS is the process of creating a data key under a CMK to be used for encrypting data. Next is the decrypt command. The decrypt command does the opposite of the encrypt operation. If encrypt is used to turn plain text data into gibberish data, then decrypt is what you use to convert cipher text data back into its plaint text formats. Please keep in mind that the decrypt operation can only be used on data or data keys that have been encrypted with a CMK. As a result, you cannot use it to decrypt files encrypted with an external key, for example. The generate data key API returns two versions of data key; a plain text, and an encrypted data key. This plain text data key is what you use to encrypt files, not the encrypted data key. Take note that you cannot use the encrypt API to encrypt data using this data key. Usually you're going to take this plain text data key and use it with the help of a cryptographic library like OpenSSL, to encrypt data. Next is the generate data key without plaint text. This KMS API is almost similar to the previous API command. Their aim is to create a data key that we can use for envelope encryption. The only difference is that generate data key without plain text, as its name implies, will generate a data key that returns only the encrypted version of it. One case where it is preferable to use generate data key without plain text rather than generate data key, is if you have a system that encrypts or decrypts data at some points, but not immediately. So instead of keeping your plain text data key somewhere in your file system, you can replace it with its encrypted version. It is more secure that way because not everyone will be able to use it to decrypt your files. To make the encrypted data key usable, you have to decrypt it first using the decrypt API to turn it in its plains text format.

Amazon API Gateway authorizer
- [Instructor] API Gateway offers two methods of authorizing requests to an API endpoint. You can either use an Amazon Cognito user pool authorizer, or a Lambda function authorizer. Let's first discuss how a Cognito user pool authorizer works. Below is a diagram representing the workflow of a serverless application that authorizes request using the Amazon Cognito user pool. We haven't seen Amazon Cognito yet, but just to give you an insight, it is a service that provides authorization and authentication for web and mobile applications. So first, when a user logs into the user pool, Cognito will check if the credentials that the user has submitted are valid. And if the login is successful, Cognito will return a JSON web token as a response. And this JSON web token is what you need to pass a custom header when making a call to the API. Here is an example of how that code might look like in JavaScript. So I'm using the fetch await here to make a GET request to this API endpoint. And aside from the GET method, I also set a custom header with a JSON web token as the header value. Once your application has received a token, it can now make the necessary calls to the API gateway. Once the request has reached API Gateway, it will look for the XAMZ security token custom header and verify the validity of its value from Amazon Cognito. Once verified, API Gateway accepts the request and performs the actual get API call. In this case, the Lambda function, which is the backend service for this application, is invoked. If there are no issues, API Gateway returns 200 status code, along with the response from the Lambda function back to the client. Next is the Lambda authorizer. This method of authorizing API requests requires the usage of a Lambda function. And a use case for this is if you want to enforce a custom authorization scheme that uses a bare token authentication strategy, such as OAuth or something that uses request parameters like query string to determine the caller's identity. So let's see how this works using this workflow. First, the application calls a GET method on the API Gateway, passing a bare token or request parameters. Then, API Gateway will check whether a Lambda authorizer is enabled for the method. If it is, API Gateway calls the Lambda function that authorizes the request. The Lambda function authenticates the caller by calling out to an OAuth provider to get an OAuth access token by calling out to a SAML provider to get a SAML assertion by generating an IAM policy based on the request parameter values or by retrieving credentials from a database. If the call succeeds, the Lambda function grants access by returning an output object containing at least an IAM policy and a principle identifier. API Gateway evaluates the policy. If access is denied, API Gateway returns a 403 forbidden status code. And if access is allowed, API Gateway performs the GET request. Since this is a custom method, you have to write the logic that carries out the authorization process. So this takes more development effort on your end compared to using the Cognito user pool.

Amazon CloudFront security features
- [Instructor] We have discussed the basics of Amazon CloudFront in our previous lecture and the importance of content delivery network to your web applications. In this lesson, we'll tackle the different security features that you can use in your CloudFront distribution to deliver content efficiently and securely. CloudFront revolves around three fundamental things, namely the content, the delivery, and the network. Apparently, this is the meaning of CDN, but these three words give you a clue of how CloudFront works under the hood. Knowing these core concepts can also help you understand how to secure content more effectively. Firstly, the word content refers to the data that you want to distribute to your users. The content that we're talking about here can either be static or dynamic. Static content refers to photos, videos, documents, and other files that don't change frequently. In contrast, dynamic content refers to the response data of the API requests, such as get HTTP requests. This could be the response that was returned by your origin server in JSON or XML format that will be cached for several minutes or hours only before it's replaced with the latest data from your server. The content that CloudFront distributes is fetched from your origin, which could be an Amazon S3 bucket, an elastic load ban, and EC2 instance or even your on-premises server. The second concept is about delivery. CloudFront allows you to configure higher content to be delivered to your website viewers. You can add a policy on both your origin and to your viewers to achieve fine-grain control over your content. If you have private content, you can securely deliver them via signed URLs and signed cookies. You can also configure your CloudFront distribution to only access your content via HTTP, via HTTPS or both. Amazon CloudFront can also have a custom SSL and TLS certificate if you want to set up an alternate domain name for your CloudFront distribution instead of just using the default CloudFront URL. The third one is network. As you know, a CDN is a global network of servers and edge locations that interconnects different internet service providers and private networks around the world. Geographic restrictions can be implemented to restrict certain countries from accessing your content. You can also secure the data in transit between your origin and your CloudFront distribution, as well as between CloudFront and your viewers that are located in totally different networks. These are the important security concepts that you can implement on your content delivery network service like Amazon CloudFront or any other CDN provider. Let's discuss the different CloudFront security features in detail and discover the related AWS services that you can integrate with. We will begin with the protocol policy setting of both your origin and viewer. The protocol policy refers to the protocol that will be used by your CloudFront distribution, which can be HTTP or HTTPS. For the origin, you can select HTTP only, HTTPS only, or the match viewer option. The first two options are quite self-explanatory, but for the last one, this means that CloudFront will communicate with your origin either via HTTP or HTTPS depending on the protocol that is used by the viewer. So if a user types http://tutorialsdojo.com., then CloudFront will use HTTP. And conversely, if the user enters https://tutorialsdojo.com. CloudFront will communicate to its origin using HTTPS. The match viewer simply matches their protocol of the viewer request, just as its name implies. For the viewer policy you can choose HTTPS only, redirect HTTP to HTTPS, and HTTP and HTTPS options. The second security feature that we'll talk about is called the Origin Access Identity, or OAI. OAI is commonly used for CloudFront distributions with an Amon S3 bucket as the origin. This feature allows you to restrict access to the content that you serve from your S3 bucket. Essentially, there are two ways of accessing your files in Amazon S3. The first is by directly accessing the files using its direct URL, and the second is through the CloudFront URL. You can block viewers from accessing your content in CloudFront, but it is possible that they can circumvent that restriction by directly using the S3 URL. Hence, your data is at risk since people can still directly access your content via Amazon S3, this is where the OAI comes into play. The Origin Access Identity is basically a special CloudFront user that you can create and associate with your CloudFront distribution. It works like an IAM user for CloudFront, which you can use to authorize access to your bucket. Once you've created an OAI, you can associate it with your origin resource or the origin group of your CloudFront distribution, and then configure your S3 bucket policy to complete the process. In this way, your users won't be able to use the direct URLs of your S3 objects, but only the CloudFront URLs alone. The next feature is called Field-Level Encryption. This allows you to encrypt the specific data fields of your content that contains sensitive information. For example, your origin is handling credit card numbers, personal health information or PHI, personally identifiable information or PII, and other confidential information. The field level encryption feature encrypts those fields using a public key of your choice and provides you with a private key that your client application can use to decrypt the protected fields. If you're distributing private content over the internet, then you can use the signed URLs and signed cookies in CloudFront. These features will restrict access to your private documents, business reports, media streams, or any other content that is intended for selected users only. Say, for example, we have premium users who have paid a fee to access exclusive data or for sharing confidential files that should only be accessed by authorized personnel. To securely serve your private content, you can structure users to access the data using a signed URL. A signed URL is simply a link or a URL that includes query parameters containing the expiration date and time for that URL. This link can be programmatically generated by using the AWS SDK. To put this into perspective, say the custom domain name of your CloudFront distribution is to tutorialsdojo.com, and you want to securely share a file named report.pdf. You can develop a Java or a .NET application that generates a signed URL for the report.pdf file. The output will look like this, https://tutorialsdojo.com/report.pdf and followed by the ?Expires, &Signature, and &Key-Pair-Id query parameters. On the other hand, the signed cookies option is utilizing the session cookies of the user's browser. You'll have to develop your application to send Set-Cookie headers that set signed cookies for authenticated users. This will allow a subset of your users to access their private content in CloudFront. The session cookies are automatically deleted when the user closes the browser or deletes the browser cache. Both the signed URL and signed cookies can be attached with a policy that specifies certain restrictions. This policy can either be a canned policy or a custom policy. The latter is more customizable as it allows you to reuse the policy to other files, indicate the allowed IP address range of the users who can access your content, specify the date and time for the access, and many more. Amazon CloudFront also has a geo-restriction feature that allows you to restrict access to your content based on the geographic location of your viewers. You can select the specific countries where you want CloudFront to deliver your content and block other countries from accessing your data. There are two restriction types that you can use, namely the white list of the approved countries and a black list of the banned countries. There's also a feature to set an alternate custom domain for your distribution. By default, the domain name of your CloudFront distribution is cloudfront.net with a unique subdomain. This subdomain usually starts with a small letter d, then followed by a bunch of alpha numeric characters. For example, you have a web application hosted in an autoscaling group of is EC2 instances behind an application load balancer. This ALB is set as the origin of your CloudFront distribution with a default URL of https://d123456abcdef.cloudfront.net. If your viewers want to access your website, they'll have to use this default cloudfront.net domain name. This URL is not fully secure since it doesn't have your own custom domain name. For this use case, you can set up an alternate domain name that you prefer for your CloudFront distribution that can serve the traffic using HTTPS. This is also called a CNAME or a canonical name record. You can serve HTTPS requests to your viewers using a dedicated IP address or through the server name indication, which is the recommended option. The server name indication is also called SNI, and it's an extension of the Transport Leader Security or TLS protocol. These two options perform the SSL/TLS negotiation in CloudFront using your own SSL/TLS certificate, the certificates can be easily generated using the AWS Certificate Manager service, or through a third party certificate authority. Using this feature protects your data that originates from your origin server to CloudFront and from CloudFront to your viewers. The same security benefit is also manifested for the user request sent by your viewers to your CloudFront distribution, and then from CloudFront to your origin server. Amazon CloudFront can also be integrated with different AWS services too. For example, you can integrate AWS WAF with your CloudFront distribution to protect your web applications from SQL ejection, or cross-site scripting attacks. You can also protect your distributions from distributed denial of service attacks or DDoS by upgrading to AWS Shield Advanced. Take note that AWS Shield is different from the Origin Shield feature of Amazon CloudFront. Origin Shield is an additional layer in the CloudFront caching infrastructure that is primarily used to minimize the incoming load to your origin and not for securing your content.

AWS CloudTrail overview
- [Instructor] Now let's talk about AWS CloudTrail. AWS CloudTrail is a managed service that you can use to monitor and log the activities that were done by an IAM user, IAM role, or an AWS service on your AWS accounts. All actions made in the AWS Management Console, API, or CLI calls are recorded as events by CloudTrail. CloudTrail can help you facilitate governance, compliance, operational auditing, and risk auditing of your AWS accounts. So what does this mean? I'll give you an example. Suppose you have a dev account where your team can play around and experiment on different AWS services. And one of your members has been launching too many EC2 instances without letting you know. At the end of the month, you've seen that the cost of your EC2 instances has gone way higher than what you're expecting. So when it came to your knowledge that there are many unused instances running 24/7, you ask your team whose fault it is, and sadly, no one is honest enough to admit it. This is where CloudTrail would be helpful to you. With CloudTrail, you can pinpoint exactly the IAM user responsible for launching those instances. Furthermore, to add conviction, you can also tell the exact time the action was made and the source IP address where the action originated. When you create a CloudTrail trail, you choose the type of events that you wish to record. There are three types of CloudTrail events. The first one is management events, and these are events that include management operations that have occurred within your AWS account, such as user logins. Management events are enabled by default. The next one is data events. Data events refer to events about resource operations performed on or within the resource itself, such as S3 object-level API Activity, Lambda function execution activity, and DynamoDB data events like when you put a new item in a table. AWS charges you for logging data events, so it is disabled by default. The third one is insight events. These events log unusual activity and user behavior in your accounts. When you create the trail, you choose the S3 buckets where the event logs will be stored. However, there's a way where you can still view the history activities in your accounts even without creating a trail, and that is through the event history. The event history is enabled by default when you create an account, and you can access it as long as you have the necessary permissions. You can view events in event history to gain immediate visibility to activities that occurred in your AWS account for the past 90 days. This means that you cannot retrieve logs that are older than 90 days. If you wish to have long-term storage of logs, then you must create a trail.

Cross-origin resource sharing (CORS)
- [Instructor] In this lecture, we will talk about cross-origin resource sharing, or CORS. If you have built a web application before involving more than one domain or origin, I can bet that you most likely run into a CORS problem. Later on, we will see why this happens and also understand why it's important to be aware of CORS when managing APIs in API Gateway. So first, let's see the textbook definition of CORS. CORS is defined as an HTTP-header-based mechanism that allows a server to indicate any other region aside its own from which a browser should authorize loading of resources. If that went over your head, it's totally fine and understandable. I myself have had troubles with it before. Don't worry, as I will try my best to explain it to you in simpler terms. And hopefully, by the end of this video, you'll have no problem explaining it to your friends, or at least it would clear most of your doubts regarding CORS. Okay? So first, of all CORS is not a feature of API Gateway. It is actually a thing that a web browser enforces to relax the restrictions of the Same-Origin Policy. So this is something that most browsers like Google Chrome, Safari, Microsoft Edge, or Opera have in common. So as I have said, a browser like Google Chrome uses CORS to loosen up the protective restrictions of the Same-Origin Policy. So to properly understand CORS and why most browsers use it, it is a must to understand the Same-Origin Policy. The Same-Origin Policy is a security feature that most browsers use to mitigate attacks like a cross-site request forgery attack. Let's understand it in a simple example. So this is what happens without the Same-Origin Policy. Suppose that for some reason, you were tricked into visiting an attacker's website, maybe through a URL redirection link and somewhere on its webpage is an iframe element that loads the login page to twitter.com to which to proceed to login. Once you're logged in, the bad website can now read the HTML elements of your Twitter account page loaded on the iframe. And with JavaScript, the attacker could also perform malicious actions on your behalf without you knowing, like resetting a password or making a post that disguises as you. There are a lot of factors that we are not considering here, but to make it simple, this is how a CSRF attack would look like. Now, with the Same-Origin Policy in place, requests coming from the bad website's script to load resources from the legitimate website are rejected. Say you have a website called mywebsite.com. If you have a JavaScript file that dynamically loads assets like CSS files, fonts, or images from your domain, then that will be allowed by your browser obeying the Same-Origin Policy. What your browser won't allow is if you use the JavaScript file to load resources from different domains, like in this case, myblog.com. In the modern internet, we usually rely on external APIs to make our website more engaging or to provide features to customers, and that would not be possible with the Same-Origin Policy since it is too restrictive. With this, engineers came up with the idea of cross-origin resource sharing, which intended to relax the Same-Origin Policy restrictions. So how does CORS work? Say that a user visits a website using Google Chrome. So Google Chrome loads all the assets from the pet.com server, such as the JavaScript files, CSS, images, and so on. One of the JavaScript files makes a GET request to an external Petnamegenerator API, which has a domain name of petnamegenerator.com. Before issuing the actual GET request, Google Chrome will send a preflight options request to the API server. Take note that this is done automatically by the browser. You don't have to call the options method from within your JavaScript code. What the options method does is ask the server whether it permits GET requests from the pet.com domain. On the API server's end, the allowed domains and methods can be specified through the Access-Control-Allow-Origin and the Access-Control-Request-Method header. For example, if I'm the developer of the Petnamegenerator API, I can select which values to put in these headers. As for the Access-Control-Allow-Origin header, I can put in here pet.com to explicitly allow pet.com to access my API. All requests from domains other than pet.com are denied. I can also put an asterisk here to basically allow all domains or regions to call my API. As for the Access-Control-Request-Method header, we can assign a GET value. So methods other than GET, like POST or DELETE, are rejected. So next, the API server will return the API methods and the origin it is allowing back to Google Chrome. And if the pet.com is listed in the Access-Control-Allow-Origin, then Google Chrome will send the actual GET requests. Otherwise, you would see this error on Chrome's developer console. So this is basically what CORS is. The same principle applies to API Gateway. You have to remember that CORS is disabled by default in API Gateway, so you have to enable this yourself. And you can do it by specifying the correct headers in the header mappings of the integration response of your API. For a simpler approach, you can simply click the Enable CORS on the API Gateway console and select the methods and origins that you want.

THREE. Deployment

AWS deployment services
- [Instructor] There are different ways to build, test, and deploy your applications to your development or production environments. Back in the day, we used to just copy a ZIP file, a Word file, or the binary files in the web apps directory of our web servers. Then, we have to manually configure and start our Apache, Nginx, and IBM Web Services servers to reflect the changes. This process is called a manual deployment, and it's prone to a lot of human errors. There are a lot of moving parts that we have to do by hand, which may take several hours to complete. Worse, provisioning the required virtual web server or dedicated database cluster will take you weeks or even months to accomplish, due to lack of automation. Good thing that nowadays, you can do your deployment in the blink of an eye through the various deployment tools available at your disposal. AWS offers a number of services that provide deployment and management capabilities for one or more aspects of your application lifecycle. These services enable organizations to build and deliver applications faster than their traditional CI/CD workflow. You and your team don't have to spend a lot of time manually provisioning, configuring, updating, monitoring, or securing your AWS resources anymore. These laborious tasks can be programmed into code and easily automated with the different deployment services available in AWS. Some of these services use the concept of Infrastructure as Code, or IaC. And IaC is the process of managing and provisioning your servers, databases, CDNs, and other resources through machine-readable definition files. Simply put, you only need to provide a text-based definition file that will automatically provision the required resources for your application in just one click of a button. The advent of cloud computing enables companies to deploy all their workloads entirely in the cloud. They also have the option to run a hybrid cloud architecture in which they utilize both their physical on-premises resources and cloud services in AWS at the same time. There's even an option now to do a multi-cloud deployment where you deploy your infrastructure to AWS, Azure, Google Cloud, and other public cloud providers simultaneously. You can run both your multi-tier applications and Kubernetes or dark container cluster almost anywhere, whether it be on the cloud, on-premises, on multiple public clouds, or a combination of all three. Let's discover the different deployment services in this lesson. These services have the capability to provision, configure, deploy, scale, and monitor your cloud architecture without any manual intervention on your part. They are AWS CloudFormation, AWS Elastic Beanstalk, AWS CodeDeploy, Amazon ECS Anywhere, Amazon EKS Anywhere, AWS OpsWorks, AWS Proton, and many more. AWS CloudFormation is a service that enables you to provision and manage your AWS resources using a custom code template. You can create a custom template in YAML or JSON format that defines the AWS resources that you require, like Amazon EC2 instances, Amazon FSx file systems, Amazon Aurora databases, CloudFront distributions, or any other resource. And the AWS CloudFormation service can deploy all of these automatically for you. AWS CloudFormation also comes with a graphic tool called the CloudFormation Designer. This is a drag-and-drop online tool for creating, viewing, and modifying your AWS CloudFormation templates. CloudFormation is the primary Infrastructure-as-Code service in AWS. It works like any other IaC tools in the market, like Terraform, Ansible, Chef, and Puppet. The key difference, however, is the additional features that it provides, which are fully compatible with the AWS Cloud. A CloudFormation template deploys your cloud infrastructure resources in a group called a stack. This stack can represent your entire cloud architecture, or just a subset. If you have a large multi-tier architecture, you can create multiple templates to represent the different tiers of your enterprise application suite. You can create a CloudFormation template for your presentation layer stack, another template for your application layer stack, and one more for your data layer stack. You can bundle your multiple stacks together into something called a nested stack. In CloudFormation, you can have a root stack with a hierarchy of nested stacks under it. This will effectively make the modules of your infrastructure code to be loosely coupled with each other, which makes the management of each individual stack much easier. Your application layer stack will only contain EC2, EKS, ECS, and other compute resources, while a data layer stack will have RDS, Aurora, DocumentDB, Amazon Neptune, Amazon Timestream, or any database services. Any change in one of the nested stack won't adversely affect the other stack. Aside from provisioning your resources, Amazon CloudFormation allows you to change, modify, or scale your services that are already deployed in your AWS account. It even has a dry run mode to check your upcoming changes before they're deployed in your cloud environment. This feature is called a change set. Essentially, a change set allows you to see how your changes might impact your running resources before finally implementing them. Say you want to change the name of your Amazon Aurora serverless database. You can create a change set in CloudFormation that'll create a new Aurora database and delete the old one.
