Of course, you'll lose the data in the old database unless you've taken a DB snapshot for backup. The change set will show you the upcoming change so you can plan accordingly before you update your stack. A CloudFormation stack is usually mapped in a single AWS account only, so if you're running your applications in two or more AWS accounts, you can use stacksets. A stackset extends the capability of CloudFormation stacks by enabling you to create, update, or delete stacks across multiple accounts in AWS regions in a single operation. You can select an AWS Secure account in your AWS organization and then choose a particular CloudFormation template for your stackset. This template will be the basis for provisioning the stacks into your selected target accounts. The stack change set, stackset, and the graphical designer tool are the base feature of the AWS CloudFormation service. There are different services in AWS that extend the capabilities in CloudFormation, namely, the AWS Cloud Development Kit and AWS Serverless Application Model. The AWS Cloud Development Kit, or AWS CDK for short, is an open-source software development kit for Amazon Web Services. You can use this to programmatically model your AWS infrastructure using TypeScript, Python, Java, .NET, or any other programming languages that you prefer. The AWS Serverless Application Model Service, or AWS SAM, is an open-source framework that simplifies the development of your serverless applications on AWS. This is commonly used if your cloud stack is using AWS Lambda, Amazon DynamoDB, Amazon API Gateway, and other serverless services. AWS SAM can also have a SAM template that's essentially just an extension of the AWS CloudFormation template. A SAM template has some additional components that make it easier for you to work with serverless services in AWS. Your apps can be stored in the AWS Serverless Application Repository. This repository service makes it easy for developers and companies to deploy, manage, and share your serverless applications in AWS and to the greater public. You can easily publish your serverless apps and share them with the community at large or privately within your organization. AWS Elastic Beanstalk is a match platform that allows you to upload your application code in AWS and provision the required cloud environment easily. You only need to upload your application package orchestrated in Java, .NET, PHP, Node.js, Python, Ruby, Go, or Docker, and then Elastic Beanstalk will deploy the necessary sources to run your application. You can either run a web server environment or a worker environment. A web server environment runs a static website, a web app, or a web API that serves HTTP requests, while a worker environment, on the other hand, runs a worker application that processes long-running workloads on demand. The latter also performs tasks on a schedule that you define and can be integrated with the Amazon SQS queue. The AWS Elastic Beanstalk service also uses a configuration file like CloudFormation to automatically deploy and configure your applications. These configuration files in Elastic Beanstalk are stored in the .ebextensions folder. AWS CodeDeploy is a fully-managed deployment service that automates your application deployments in AWS. You can deploy your applications to Amazon EC2 instances, Amazon ECS clusters, AWS Lambda functions, and other computing services in AWS. You can even use this to deploy your application to the servers located on your on-premises network. This service is different from AWS CloudFormation, AWS SAM, and Elastic Beanstalk, since you can only deploy your applications to existing compute resources. AWS CodeDeploy does not create AWS resources on your behalf, and is intended for application deployments only. Amazon ECS is a fully-managed container orchestration service that supports Docker containers. This orchestration service allows you to easily run containerized applications on a managed cluster that you can control. Basically, container orchestration is an automation tool that reduces the operational effort needed to run your containerized workloads and services. Amazon ECS can automatically orchestrate or control the manual tasks of provisioning, deploying, scaling, networking, and many other tasks, so you don't have to. When you create an Amazon ECS cluster, you can choose whether the compute resources will be deployed or launched in your own VPC, in AWS Fargate, or externally via Amazon ECS Anywhere. A cluster launch in a VPC uses Amazon EC2 instances that are orchestrated and controlled by Amazon ECS. You can use the Amazon CloudWatch Container Insights to monitor your container workloads. If a launch type is AWS Fargate, then your cluster will be serverless and the computing resources will be fully managed by AWS. Using AWS Fargate can significantly reduce

AWS CloudFormation overview
- [Instructor] In this lecture, we will talk about AWS CloudFormation. AWS CloudFormation allows you to manage your whole infrastructure using code. This gives developers and businesses an easy way to create a collection of related AWS resources and provision them in an automated, orderly, and predictable fashion. Manual processes are slow and always prone to errors. For example, when it comes to provisioning resources or updating a configuration, an IT admin might miss a step or forget something when completing the task. The infrastructure-as-code approach eliminates all of this. AWS CloudFormation is an infrastructure-as-code service that helps you simplify infrastructure management by building your infrastructure using templates, quickly replicate your infrastructure by reusing CloudFormation templates that you created, and easily control and track changes to your infrastructure by using rollback actions and version control on your templates. A CloudFormation template is a JSON or YAML formatted text file that serves as blueprints for building your AWS resources. Take this example, to create an Amazon EC2 instance, you declare the instance type, the AMI ID, block device mappings, and its Amazon EC2 key pair name on a template. CloudFormation is not just limited to provisioning EC2 instances. You can also declare multiple resources that work together to build your inter infrastructure like security groups, EIPs, and more. Whenever you create a stack, you also specify a template that CloudFormation should use to create whatever you describe in the template. Now, what is a stack? When you use CloudFormation, you manage related resources called a stack. You create, update, and delete a group or collection of resources by creating, updating, and deleting stacks. All the resources provisioned in a stack are defined by the stack's CloudFormation template. Suppose you created a template that includes an EC2 instance security group, autoscaling group, ELB load balancer, and an Amazon RDS database instance. To create those resources, you should create a stack by submitting the template you created, and CloudFormation provisions all those resources for you. If you need to make changes to the running resources in a stack, this means you need to update the stack. Before making changes to your resources, a recommended practice is to generate a change set. A change set is like a summary of your proposed changes. This allows you to see how your changes might impact the resources in your current stack, especially if you're working with critical resources before implementing them.

AWS CloudFormation template anatomy
- [Instructor] In this lecture, we will look at the different sections of a cloud formation template. So right now, I'm on my VS Code Editor, and you're looking at the blank yml file in which we will write a boiler plate for a cloud formation template. Let's go ahead and do that. As you can see, that was quick, and it's because I'm using an extension called, CloudFormation Snippets by Danny Steenman. What this extension does is that it adds snippets for all the available resources that you need in AWS Cloud formation speeding up the process of writing templates. What we have here are the components that make up a cloud formation template, so we have the AWS template format version, description, parameters, metadata, mappings, conditions, resources, transform, and outputs. First, let's focus on the AWS template format version, description, and metadata sections. All these sections just simply help you describe your template. The AWS template format version section lets you set the version of the template that you want to use which determines the capabilities of the template. If you don't assign a version, cloud formation automatically assumes that you want to use the latest version. The description section allows you to include comments about your template to help you better describe and add more details about your template. And last is the metadata section. In this section, you can provide objects that give details about the template or even implementation details of specific resources. We will skip the transform section for now, and we will get back to it later when we go to AWSM. So that leaves us five sections which are the parameters, mappings, conditions, resources, and outputs. These are the sections that you'll be working on most of the time, so we'll put our focus here. Before I forget, take note that all of these sections are optional except for the resources section. The resources section is the only required section. Let's start with the resources section. We'll discuss the other four as we progress. The resources section is where you define all the resources that you want to add to your stack. So if you want to create an EC2 instance load balancer and S3 bucket or any resources, you define them in the resources section, let's define an EC2 instance. First, let's provide a logical id. The logical ID is used to reference the resources in your template. The logical ID is arbitrary, so you may assign it a value however you want, but it has to be a unique value. We'll call this instance, My EC2 Instance. Under the logical ID is the type field which identifies the type of resource that you want to declare. It could be an EC2 instance, an S3 buckets, or maybe a Lambda function. In our case, it is an EC2 instance. Next is the properties field. The properties field helps you declare additional options for a resource. As you can see, the cloud formation snippets extension has listed all the properties available to an EC2 instance. We don't need all properties in this demo, so we'll delete all of them, except for the image ID and instance type. Just a reminder, you don't have to memorize all of the properties. Always check for the AWS documentation for available resource types that you can use. As for image ID, I will provide an ID that I've created earlier, and as for the instance type, I want this to be a T2 micro instance. Let's save this template and head over to the cloud formation console to submit it. Click Next. Let's give it a stack name. I'll name this stack, My Stack. Click Next. Scroll down to the bottom of the page and click Next. Again, scroll down and click create stack. Here we can see the events that are happening as our stack gets created, and you can verify them under status. The status does not update automatically, so you have to click the refresh button to update. This may take a while, so I'll get back to you once completed. Now we can see from the status that our stack has been created successfully. Let's go to the EC2 console to confirm it. I can see one T2 micro instance running in this region. So this means that cloud formation has successfully launched our stack. While this works, you won't usually find cloud formation templates that use the resources section alone. When you use the resources section alone, to define your stacks, you would have to hard code all the property values that you need. For example, here, we declare that we want to create a T2 micro instance. T2 micro is hardcoded. What if you need to share this template with your friend, but your friend wants to use a different instance type instead? Of course, he can just edit this value and write a different one. It works for this overly simplified template, but for a stack that has multiple resources with a lot of properties, it won't be a pretty solution. Wouldn't it be nice if we can dynamically set a value for the instance type? Here comes the parameters section. The parameters section allows you to customize your templates with custom values that you input into your template each time your stack is created or updated. Let's try to add a parameter for the instance type. Let's give it a name. I'll call this EC2 instance type. As for description, let's say, choose an instance type. We can restrict the allowable values for the instance type parameter by specifying the allowed values field, and I'll just copy the one that is already written in my other template. Finally, let's specify T2 micro as the default value. In the instance type property, we will replace T2 micro with whatever value a user might select, and the way we can do that is by using the ref intrinsic function. Intrinsic functions are built-in functions that only cloud formation can understand. The ref intrinsic functions returns the value of the specified parameter or resource. We will specify here the EC2 instance type parameter that we created. If a user has selected T2 nano, then T2 nano will be passed here as instance type value. Okay, let's save this template and submit it to cloud formation. So now, the parameter section is displayed to us, and in here we can choose the instance type dynamically, compared earlier where we have to declare it explicitly. So that's it for parameters. Let's now discuss mappings. The mapping section matches a key to a corresponding set of name values. Say you, have custom AMIs stored in US East 1 and US East 2, and you want to ensure that you use the correct AMIs in each region. With the mapping section, you can create keys for each region and create name value pairs for each key. I will copy the mapping section from my existing template and paste it here. Okay, in this case, there are different AMI IDs within the US East 1 and US East 2 regions. Regions is your mappings. The name on each key is My AMI, and the values are the AMI IDs that you want to use. Right now, the AMI ID is hardcoded in the image ID field. Take note, AMIs are region-specific and this hardcoded image ID belongs to the US East 1 region. If I launch this template in US East 2, it will fail because this AMI does not exist in US East 2. So just like what we did with the instance type, this needs to be a dynamic value, and the way we do that is by using the find in map intrinsic function. This intrinsic function is used to retrieve values from a mapping section. The first value is the mapping name which is regions followed by the key name which is a region followed by the value. We need the region to be dynamic, so we'll use the AWS region pseudo parameter. Like intrinsic functions, this is a built-in parameter in cloud formation. We'll use the ref function to reference it. This way, the stack would be created successfully even if a user decided to launch this template in US East 2. So that is mappings. Let's now go to the condition section. The condition section defines conditions by using the intrinsic condition functions. These conditions determine when AWS cloud formation creates the associated resources. Here's an example. Say, you want to make an option on whether a user wants to create an EIP or not. So to do this, first, you can create a parameter that asks if a user wants to create an EIP. If it selects yes, then cloud formation will create the EIP as part of your stack. Otherwise, cloud formation will just ignore it. Last is the output section. The output section declares output values that you can import to other stacks to create what you call cross-stack references. For example, you can output the instance ID that will be given to your EC2 instance once the stack creation is completed. And you can also use the exported field to name the instance to be exported and to reference the exported values in this template. You can use the intrinsic function import value in another stack to import the values exported.

AWS Serverless Application Model (AWS SAM) overview
- [Instructor] In this lecture, we'll be looking at AWS Serverless Application Model or SAM. AWS SAM is an open-source framework for building serverless applications. SAM is divided into two parts, the SAM templates and the SAM CLI. The first one that we're going to be talking about is SAM templates. A SAM template is actually an extension of a cloud formation template that lets you use shorthand syntax to define serverless resources, such as lambda functions, APIs, dynamo DB tables, and other event sources that trigger your lambda function. So how is it different from a cloud formation template? When writing a standard cloud formation template, we define resources for resource type. So if you want to build a backend API out of the lambda function in API gateway, you would need to declare the AWS lambda function resource. You must construct an execution role for that function, which is another resource type. And with API gateway, you have to do the same. You need to define the API resource, the API stage and so on. Now, the advantage of the SAM template is it provides you custom resource types, which allows you to write these resources in shorter lines of code. For example, AWS SAM has a resource type called AWS serverless function, which does a bit more than just creating a lambda function. This single resource type can build a lambda function, an IAM execution role, and an event source, and it's going to wire them altogether for you. All of these components can be created under a single resource type. Just imagine how much time and development effort you would save by using the SAM templates. To start writing a SAM template, you must declare the AWS serverless transform macro in the transform section of the cloud formation templates. The transform section lets you define one or more macros that will be used by AWS cloud formation to process your templates. In computing, a macro refers to a command that automatically expands to a set of simpler commands. In the case of SAM, the AWS serverless function resource is eventually expanded into several cloud formation resource types. When you deploy resources in a SAM template to cloud formation, cloud formation will first look for the transform section, and if there is one, and assuming that it is valid, all resources defined under that transform macro are transformed and expanded into native cloud formation resources. So at the end of the day, it's still interpreted as a normal cloud formation template. Take note that SAM has limited options in terms of its resource types. It's very specific to serverless resources. If you need to create resources that are not available in SAM like an EC2 instance, you can declare them in their native cloud formation syntax with no problem. You can also access the intrinsic functions and see the parameters in cloud formation within your SAM resources. The next part of AWS SAM is the SAM CLI. The SAM CLI is a set of commands that you can use to build, tests and debug serverless applications. You must know a specific SAM commands going into the exam, mostly those that are commonly used, and we will go over them in detail in the next lecture.

Deploying codes with external dependencies to AWS Lambda
- [Lecturer] Now let's talk about how to deploy codes with external dependencies to AWS Lambda. Let me show you something. Say I have this Python script on my laptop, and what this script does is that it converts text files into CSV files. As you can see here in the first two lines of the code, I imported two dependencies to write this script. The first dependency is a module called re. And re is a built-in module in Python that allows you to check if a particular string matches a given regular expression. The second dependency is an external package called pandas, which is commonly used for data analysis jobs. Now let's run this code and see if it works. As you can see on the leftmost side of the VS code editor, the code generated a CSV version of this text file. And this is how I know that the code works. Okay so the question is, what if I want to migrate this code to AWS and deploy it as Lambda function? Right now, it is only runnable on my local machine. Suppose I want to convert text files to CSV files as they get uploaded to Amazon S3 using a Lambda function. How can we do that? Let's figure that out. Right now, I'm logged into my AWS account, and I'll head over to the Lambda console, by typing in Lambda right on the navigation search bar, click Lambda. Once the page is loaded, click create function. Let's give our function a name. I'll name a function text to CSV converter. As for runtime, I'll use Python 3.8 and leave the default execution role as it is. After filling out the needed information, click on create function. Let's wait till the function is created. Now that the function is created, scroll down to the Lambda editor and click the functions Lambda handler. The next thing that I'll do is to copy the code from my computer and paste it directly to the Lambda editor. Let me fix the indentation of the code and move the dependencies outside the handler. Again, I'd like to mention that one of the best practices in writing Lambda function is to declare all resources that your function needs outside of the main handler. As we saw in the previous section, by doing this, Lambda will be able to reuse those resources for the next function invocations, allowing for better performance. Examples of these resources are your libraries, packages and frameworks. And we just call them dependencies in general because a function won't run without them. Click deploy to save the changes made and to deploy the function. Click test and provide an event name. Again, click test to finally run the function. So basically I get an error message saying unable to import module, and according to the error, the runtime wasn't able to import pandas. Why did this happen? My code work exactly as intended in my local environment. However, after deploying it to AWS Lambda, the response suddenly throws an error. What's the reason behind this? The reason is AWS Lambda only provides standard libraries for every supported runtime, including their AWS SDK. Some of these libraries are usually enough for doing typical jobs, such as passing JSON data or making HTTP requests. But what if you need to use libraries other than the built-in ones. Lambda does not have a terminal where you can run pip commands to install external Python libraries for example. The answer is a three step process. The first step, and also the most important one, is to install all external dependencies that your function needs on the same folder where your code is stored. Second, generate a deployment package by creating a zip file of that folder. You can use the zip command line utility in Linux to zip up the folder containing your application code and its dependencies. Finally, proceed by uploading the deployment package to AWS Lambda. You can directly upload the file to AWS Lambda, or store it first to an S3 bucket and upload it from there. In the next section, we'll have a simple hands on exercise to familiarize yourself with the process of deploying codes with external dependencies to AWS Lambda.

AWS CodeDeploy overview
- [Narrator] Now let's look into AWS CodeDeploy. In the previous section, we saw how CodeBuild can be used as a continuous integration service. This time we'll see CodeDeploy as a continuous deployment service. So first, what is continuous deployment? Continuous deployment is a software development strategy wherein code changes that pass automated tests from a build service like CodeBuild are deployed automatically into an application environment. And this can be your production test or development environment. In a nutshell, continuous integration prepares code that is ready to deploy and continuous deployment is a stage that comes after it that will take care of the code deployment for you. AWS CodeDeploy is a fully managed deployment service that automates software deployments to different sorts of compute services, such as Amazon EC2, AWS Lambda, Amazon ECS, and AWS Fargate. It also supports deployments to on-premises servers. So if you want to automate application deployments to your servers outside the AWS Cloud, then AWS CodeDeploy is the right service for you. It is important to know that to make EC2 instance and on-premises deployments possible, you need to have the AWS CodeDeploy agent installed on your instance or server first. AWS CodeDeploy makes it easier for you to release new features and versions of your application without downtime during the deployment phase. CodeDeploy offers two deployment options. The first one is called in-place deployments, and the other one is blue-green deployments. We will further discuss these two later on. Deployments are not only limited to application codes, as CodeDeploy also supports a variety of application assets such as serverless Lambda functions, configuration files, executables, packages, scripts, or even multimedia files. CodeDeploy is platform agnostic so it shouldn't be a problem even if your applications are running on Windows, Mac or any Linux distro. AWS CodeDeploy can also perform automatic rollbacks in case a deployment fails. In this case, the last working revision of your application is redeployed with a new developments ID. You can also take over and perform rollbacks manually, and you might want to do this if you wish to have more control over choosing the application version that will be redeployed.

AWS CodeDeploy primary components
- [Instructor] AWS CodeDeploy has different components that are important for you to know before you start working with this service. Let's discuss them one by one. The first component is application. This is what you have to create first when you set up a deployment on CodeDeploy. You can think of application as a container that holds up the rest of the CodeDeploy components. An application identifies the application version that you want to deploy. When you create an application, you simply have to give it a name and select the compute platform of your choice. This name must be unique across your applications because CodeDeploy uses it to ensure that the correct combination of revision, deployment configuration and deployment group are referenced during a deployment. The next component is called the deployment group, and this is where you will set up most of your configuration settings in CodeDeploy. A deployment group refers to the set of instances or Lambda functions that you want to deploy an application revision to. Your deployment group contains various information such as service role, which gives permission to CodeDeploy to do its job. This is also where you get the chance to choose the deployment site, which can be in place, or blue-green. You also get to choose an environment configuration and a deployment configuration. You can create multiple deployment groups within an application in CodeDeploy. The advantage of this is that you can create different stages of deployments before finally deploying your code to production. For example, you can deploy a code update to a set of instances first in a test environment to ensure code quality, and after that, you can deploy it to a staging environment to do some confirmation. And if you're happy with the result of your deployments, you can finally deploy the code to your production environments. The next component is deployment configuration. This component is also very important to know in the ZDA exam, so we'll discuss it in detail in a separate section. For now, let's see what it is and what it does. So a deployment configuration is a set of conditions and deployment rules that CodeDeploy applies during a deployment. In simple terms, what a deployment configuration allows you to do is it lets you modify the behavior of your deployment type, which again, could be an in place or blue-green. Take note that these rules and conditions vary for the compute types that you're deploying to. So a deployment configuration available for an EC2 instance might not be available to a Lambda function or an ECS deployment type. And then the last is the AppSpec file. If code build has a build spec file, then CodeDeploy has an AppSpec file. An AppSpec file can be written in YAML or JSON. CodeDeploy uses an AppSpec file to manage each deployment stage as a series of lifecycle event hooks. Just like the build spec file, the AppSpec file allows you to instruct CodeDeploy of the deployment actions that it'll execute during the deployment process of your application. So, for example, you can tell CodeDeploy to install dependencies that your application requires before proceeding to install the new application version or modify some file permissions first before starting the deployments.

AWS CodeDeploy in-place vs. blue-green deployment
- [Instructor] In CodeDeploy, you can either use an in-place or a blue/green deployment option for releasing code versions to your production environments. In this section, we will learn about their differences and their pros and cons so you can make a better decision when choosing a strategy for your deployment process. First, let's talk about how in-place deployment works. Say you have a web application running across EC2 instances inside a deployment group, and you want to update its user interface to make it more responsive. After developing the code for the new UI, the next thing you want to do is to archive the new application code along with an AppSpec file into a deployable content, which can be a ZIP or a TAR file. Take note that just like the buildspec, the YAML file, an AppSpec file must be located at the root level of your application folder. After building the archive, upload it to a repository that CodeDeploy supports. You can store the archive file to Amazon S3 and GitHub or Bitbucket. However, only EC2 and on-premises deployments can use GitHub and Bitbucket as repositories. When you deploy these new updates using in-place deployments, CodeDeploy will stop and uninstall the previous application version from your instances. What happens next is that CodeDeploy will pull the latest code version from the repository that you selected and install it to your EC2 instances. CodeDeploy will restart the application back into service once the deployment is completed. The disadvantage of an in-place deployment is that your application will experience an outage at the period of time during the deployment process. It also has a slower rollback compared to a blue-green deployment because it would take time to revert back the changes made to all instances. However, there is a way on how you can get around these disadvantages. And that is by modifying your deployment configuration. And by the way, what I just described here is an example of an all-at-once deployment configuration, which is also the default configuration for in-place deployments. We will discuss more about deployment configurations later on. For now, let's just focus on in-place and blue/green deployments. The biggest advantage of an in-place deployment is that it's cheaper to implement since you're only maintaining a single environment. Also, take note that in-place deployment is only available for deployments on EC2 and on-premises compute platform. Now let's go to blue/green deployments. In a blue/green deployment, you maintain two separate environments that have their own compute and network resources. Usually, each environment contains a load balancer that targets a group of computing resources. The blue environment refers to the active environment to which current user traffic is directed. The green environment is where the latest application revision is deployed. Once the new version has been successfully deployed to the green environment, the web traffic will be shifted away from the blue environment to the green environment, which now contains the updated version of your application. This cutover is done by using Route 53 to redirect the DNS records of the blue environment's load balancer to the green's load balancer. Once all user traffic is served by the green environment, the previous blue environment now becomes the green environment, and the current environment where the new version is installed becomes the new blue environment. The biggest advantage of a blue/green deployment is that switching to a new application version won't involve any downtime since you're just redirecting traffic, unlike in in-place deployments. The second advantage is that rolling back to the previous environment in case something goes wrong is much smoother because the switching's done at the network level. Also, since the blue and green environments are isolated from each other, you can perform tests on the green environments without affecting the operation of the blue environments. The obvious downside of a blue-green deployment is its expensive implementation cost that comes from running two environments at the same time.

AWS CodeDeploy deployment configuration
- [Instructor] Now let's look at the different deployment configurations available in CodeDeploy. What deployment configuration is used for is it allows you to define how aggressive or conservative your deployment approach is going to be. A deployment configuration is a set of rules and conditions that CodeDeploy applies during deployments to determine if a deployment is successful or not. There are five predefined deployment configurations in CodeDeploy. The first one that we'll talk about is the all at once deployments. All at once deployment is very easy to understand and it is the simplest among the deployment configurations. As the name implies, CodeDeploy will try to replace the old application code on all existing instances with the latest code version in one go. So if you have five instances, for example, the new application version is deployed to these instances all at the same time. So what are the requirements for a successful deployment? For deployment to succeed, the new application must be deployed to at least one instance. So even if the succeeding deployments fail, CodeDeploy will still mark that as a successful deployment. Only if all deployments to all instances fail does the overall deployment fail. This configuration isn't recommended for production workloads, however, its real value shines when you're deploying to test environments where you want to push changes and see them as quickly as possible. The second deployment configuration is one at a time. In this deployment configuration, the latest application version is rolled out to each instance one at a time. So if you have a fleet of instances, this could make your deployment process very slow. The overall deployment succeeds once the new application version is deployed to all of the instances. The exception to this rule is that even if deployment to the final instance fails, the overall deployment still succeeds. Say you have five instances in a deployment group. Even if CodeDeploy only succeeds in deploying to four instances, the overall deployment is considered successful. The deployment only fails if deployment of one of those first four instances fails. Next is half at a time. In this type of deployment configuration, your application is deployed to at least 50% of your instances at once. So if you have four instances, it will deploy to two of them simultaneously. If you have an odd number of instances, for example five, you take the half of five, which is 2.5, and round it down giving us a result of two. This means that your code will be deployed to two instances at once. However, for CodeDeploy to mark it successful, at least half of your instances must have a successful deployment. In the case of five instances, three is the next number larger than 2.5. But because we deployed two instances at a time, deployment will only succeed if the new application is deployed to at least four instances. We only take whole numbers into account because it's impossible to deploy to 0.5 of a computer. Okay, this deployment configuration is more conservative than the all at once deployments, and you won't have to wait as long as you would with one at a time. This is preferable if you're confident that at least half of your servers will be able to serve traffic without issue while the new application is being rolled out. The next one is Canary. These deployment configuration is only applicable to a blue-green deployment type. In a canary deployment, the shifting of traffic from the blue environment to the green environment is done in two increments. You can specify how much traffic will be shifted to your green environment at the first increment, as well as how many minutes CodeDeploy will wait before shifting the rest of the traffic. Let's take a look at this predefined configuration for a Lambda deployment. This says Lambda Canary 10%, five minutes. 10% means that at the first increments, 10% of user traffic will be shifted to the green environments. Five minutes means that the remaining 90% will be shifted immediately after five minutes. So by looking at the interval alone, which is five minutes, we can determine the total deployment time for this configuration. The last deployment configuration is Linear. like Canary, this deployment configuration is only applicable to a blue-green deployment type. In a linear configuration, traffic is shifted in equal increments. Let's take this predefined configuration for example. CodeDeploy default that Lambda linear 10% every one minutes. Just looking at how it is named, figuring what it does is pretty intuitive. So this configuration means that for every minute, 10% of the traffic will be shifted away from the old environments to the latest environments. The latest environment will serve 10% of total traffic in the first minutes. After two minutes, it'll serve 20% and it'll continue to shift 10% away from the old application to the latest application every minute until all user traffic is directed to the new application version. So in total, it would take about 10 minutes before all user traffic is shifted to the green environments. Take note that out of all these five, only all at once, canary and linear deployment configurations can be applied to AWS Lambda and ECS deployments. EC2 and on-premises deployments have no problem using any of these deployment configurations.

AWS CodeDeploy lifecycle event hooks
- [Instructor] The AppSpec file, as we talk about, is a piece of a configuration file that you need to include along with your application, particularly at the root level of your application folder. There are certain sections that must be defined when writing an AppSpec file, and some of those sections are dedicated to a specific compute platform, so we will not cover all of them. What we'll talk about in this lecture is the section that is common and the most important amongst all compute types, and that is the hooks section. The hooks section contains a set of logical groupings called lifecycle event hooks, which has their own set of instructions for deploying an application. Lifecycle event hooks are basically a list of scripts that CodeDeploy runs in sequence. Here's an overview of the lifecycle event hooks of an in-place deployment. Let's briefly go over each of them. The first lifecycle event is the Start event, and this initiates the instance for deployments. CodeDeploy automatically executes this for you, so you don't have to include this under your hooks section. What comes after the start event is ApplicationStop, and this event is used for running any scripts that will prepare your instance in receiving the new application version. For example, if you're deploying a new application version, say v.2, this event hook will allow you to include a script that will disable the previous version, v.1. Next is DownloadBundle. Like the Start event, this event gets executed automatically after the ApplicationStop event. As the name implies, during this stage, CodeDeploy will pull the new version, v.2, from a source repository into the instance. Next is the BeforeInstall event. The BeforeInstall event gives you the chance to do whatever you need to do before installing the new application version. A use case for this event hook is when you want to keep the previous configuration files of your old application, or maybe you want to back up some old logs and store them somewhere in your file system so they would not get overwritten when the new application comes. So after the BeforeInstall, the Install event will take place. During this event, CodeDeploy copies your new application files to the file destination that you specified. Again, this event happens automatically so you don't have to configure it on the hooks section. After the Install event comes the AfterInstall event. So usually this event is used for decrypting database string information, application secrets, or any encrypted data that you've sent down as a part of your application bundle and move them to the respected destination. Next is ApplicationStart. As the name implies, you use this event to restart your application back into service, which in this case is now v.2. Next is the ValidateService event, and you can use this event to run any validation script to check if the application is behaving as expected. Next is the End event, and this is the last lifecycle event hook. If all goes as expected, this event is where CodeDeploy will tell you that the deployment was indeed successful. If something wrong happens during the deployment, you'll have the chance to decide what steps to do next, like rolling back to a known working version for example. What's important to know here in the CD exam is the order of the lifecycle event hooks. This might seem a lot to take in at first, but if you really try to understand the function of each event, then you'll realize that you don't have to memorize all these lifecycle events because they're pretty intuitive anyway. For example, you can't immediately jump into the BeforeInstall event because, in an in-place deployment, the old application version must be stopped first to give way for the new version. You can't deploy a new version while the current version is still running. So even without having to memorize all these lifecycle events, if you understand the concept behind a deployment type, I'm pretty sure that you'll be able to answer questions regarding lifecycle event hooks.

AWS Elastic Beanstalk overview
- [Instructor] Suppose you wish to deploy an application in EC2, say a web application implemented in the MERN stack. In this case, you have to go to the EC2 console, launch an instance manually, log into the instance, set up the environment, configure a web server, download and install Node.js, and install Express or other dependencies that you need. Now we have a working web app deployed in a single instance. At this point, this is just basic architecture. If you want to protect the application from an outage, you need to create yet another resource. You have to launch an application load balancer, create more instances, spread them across different AZs, and point them as a target group through ALB. Great, so now our application is running in highly available environments. This is actually not a bad architecture, and we can already stop right here, but let's try to add something else. What about capacity? If the instance in this availability zone suddenly stops running, then it'll reduce the application's capacity to serve incoming requests. So we need to solve that. And what would be useful here is an auto-scaling group, and this is another service that we need to create and set up. As you can see, there are a lot of things that you need to do just to see your application up and running, and the list of things to do doesn't end there. If you need to deploy a new application version, you will need to go to each of these instances and replace the old version. The same thing applies to updates. If you are a developer, you normally wouldn't want to touch on things that concern architectures or reduces admin tasks. You basically just want to write application code, deploy it, and hopefully see your application running. This is where AWS Elastic Beanstalk can help you. AWS Elastic Beanstalk is a service that belongs to the PaaS cloud computing model. The acronym PaaS stands for platform as a service. By definition, it removes the need for organizations to manage underlying infrastructure and allows you to focus on the deployment and management of your applications. So as a developer, you can simply upload your application code and everything is taken care of by Elastic Beanstalk, from resource provisioning, load balancing, monitoring, database management, patching, or any heavy lifting activities, such as deploying a new version and rolling back to the previous version in case a deployment fails. You wouldn't have to bother yourself doing all these things. Here is the list of configurations that Elastic Beanstalk can do for you. It can create an auto-scaling group for you where you can choose the instance type where your application will be deployed to. You can deploy an application to a single instance or to instances behind an ALB. If you create a load balance environment, Elastic Beanstalk takes care of the creation of the load balancer. You can choose a root volume for your instance, and this refers to EBS volumes. Elastic Beanstalk is also integrated with Amazon RDS, which means it can create an RDS DB instance for your application. But this is optional. In case you have an external database, like in our Beanstalk example, which is MongoDB, you can connect to that database by applying the correct configuration to your security group and setting the correct database connections string in your code. Elastic Beanstalk is also integrated with services like AWS X-ray, CloudWatch Logs for monitoring, and Amazon SNS for notifications. Elastic Beanstalk also offers different deployment methodologies that we'll talk about more later. Elastic Beanstalk supports applications written in Java, .Net, Node.js, PHP, Ruby, Python, and Go. It also supports Dockerized applications. Take note that Elastic Beanstalk as a service is free of charge, but you would still have to pay for the resources that it creates on your behalf. You can also still take full control of the underlying resources that Elastic Beanstalk has created if you want to. So for example, you can freely log into your application's EC2 instances to make configuration changes or change the inbound rule of your EC2 security group.

AWS Elastic Beanstalk deployment policies
- [Instructor] Now let's talk about the different deployment policies that Elastic Beanstalk offers. Being a platform-as-a-service, Elastic Beanstalk does not only take care of the resource provisioning for your application, but can also help you simplify the process of deploying a new application version to your environment. A deployment policy simply defines how Elastic Beanstock carries out the deployment of your new code revision. The first deployment policy for Elastic Beanstock is the all-at-once deployments. As the name implies, when you deploy using the all-at-once deployments, Elastic Beanstock will deploy your new application code to all of your instances at the same time. So let's see how this works. Say you have an environment with autoscaled instances running behind a load balancer, and you deploy the new code version using all-at-once. What will happen next is that Elastic Beanstalk will temporarily disable your application across your instances and install the new code version simultaneously. When the deployment is successful, Elastic Beanstock will start the new application version back in the service. The clear advantage of an all-at-once deployment is its quick deployment time, since you are updating all instances at the same time. And this is something that comes in handy if you're doing tests in a development environment where you just want to release updates as fast as possible to see the results of your changes. Under all-at-once, your application will suffer a period of downtime that depends on how long your new code would take to be successfully deployed. That being said, this deployment isn't something that you would normally see in production and is only really helpful for doing iterative tests on developing environments. The second deployment policy is called rolling. So the idea behind a rolling deployment is that your new code version is rolled out in batches where you get to choose the batch size number. Let's see an example with a batch size of two instances. When you release a new code version to these environments using the rolling deployments, Elastic Beanstock will take two of your instances out of service at the time and install the latest code. So if your maximum capacity is six instances, anytime during the deployments, your maximum capacity will be reduced to four. It also means that some of your users will still see the old application version, and there will be some who will be interacting with the latest version. And this will happen until the deployment is completed. The advantage of this deployment is that the impact of failed deployment is lower compared to all-at-once. In our example, in case a deployment has failed, rolling back the changes will only apply to two instances. The rest of the instances are unaffected. Unlike all-at-once, this deployment policy does not have any downtime as long as the reduced capacity is able to serve the volume of traffic during deployments. The next deployment policy is rolling with an additional batch. So this is a variation of rolling, and it solves the problem of applications running below the maximum capacity during deployments. Let's see how this works. When you deploy the latest code version of your application to these environments, instead of taking an instance or instances out of service, Elastic Beanstock will provision a new batch of instances, install the latest code in them, and register the new instances to the load balancer. So in our example, Elastic Beanstock will create and deploy the code to two new instances. Take note that, during the deployment, your application is still using the old version and there is no reduction in capacity. After a successful deployment, Elastic Beanstock will register the two instances to the load balancer and will take out two old instances in return to go back to the defined capacity. This process will repeat until the latest application version is deployed to all instances. So what happens if the deployment fails? Well, if the first batch of instances fail, those instances are terminated, and your existing instances remain unchanged. But what if the first batch of instances succeeded, and the next batch after it has encountered a failure? In that case, both the latest and old versions of your code will be exposed to some of your users, so you need to redeploy the old version to revert back the changes that have been made. The advantage of this deployment policy is that your application will always run at full capacity. Since Elastic Beanstalk is spawning new instances on top of the existing instances, make sure that you don't exceed the number of EC2 limits for your account. For example, if you have a limit of 20 instances per region and you're already running 19 instances, if your batch size is two instances, Elastic Beanstalk won't be able to create those additional instances because it would violate your account limit, resulting in error. So be careful. The next deployment is called immutable. Immutable is synonymous with unchangeable, which implies that existing instances are not updated or modified in any way. In this deployment type, Elastic Beanstock will duplicate the existing instances into a fresh set of instances running in a separate Auto Scaling group. The new application version will be deployed to this new set of instances, and if the deployment is successful, Elastic Beanstock will register the new Auto Scaling group as a target for the load balancer and terminate the old Auto Scaling group. If something happens that has caused the deployment to fail, the new Auto Scaling Group will be terminated. The advantage of this is that rollbacks do not involve downtime and do not affect the existing instances where the working application version is running. Rollbacks are also faster because you're just terminating an Auto Scaling group. Your application will also run at full capacity during the deployments. This deployment strategy, however, is costly because you're duplicating the whole fleet of instances. Like rolling with an additional batch, this deployment is also subject to your EC2 account limit, so you need to be cautious if you plan to use this deployment method. The next deployment is traffic splitting, and this is great for A/B or Canary testing. A Canary test allows you to gradually release updates to a subset of your users while still serving your current application version to some of your users. In Elastic Beanstalk, you can set the percentage of traffic that is a split between the old and latest versions and the amount of time to monitor the new version before completing the deployment. When you deploy a new version using traffic splitting, Elastic Beanstock installs the new version to a new set of instances. It will then shift a percentage of traffic that you specified towards the new instances for a certain evaluation period. If the instances pass the health checks until then, the deployment is successful. Elastic Beanstock will route the rest of the traffic to the new instances and terminate the old instances. If the new instances did not pass the health checks or if you decide to cancel the deployments, Elastic Beanstock will simply reroute the specified percentage of traffic back to the old instances and terminate the new instances.

AWS Elastic Beanstalk lifecycle policy
- [Instructor] In this lecture, we will talk about how you can manage different versions of your Elastic Beanstalk application using the application version lifecycle settings. Whenever you deploy a new version of your application, whether via the console or the EB CLI, Elastic Beanstalk creates a new version of it with its own unique label. If you're doing an iterative test where you're frequently deploying small code changes, it's easy to lose track of the number of versions growing under your application. And as this continues, the amount of old versions that you most likely won't ever use again also increases. If you don't delete the versions that you no longer use, sooner or later, you would hit the application version quota and won't be able to create new versions of that application. The application version quota applies across all applications within a region. So the implication of this is if there are other applications in a region and you end up consuming all the versions available in that region, then those other applications will be affected as well. Developers who are managing those applications won't be able to deploy a new version. So the solution for this is to delete versions that are no longer useful. This will free up the number of versions that can be created by other applications. This is a manual solution and it works, but we can do better than that. This is where the Elastic Beanstalk lifecycle policy comes into play. The lifecycle policy determines how old versions should stay before Elastic Beanstalk removes them from your account. It also tells Elastic Beanstalk to delete versions if the total number of application versions exceeds a specified number. For example, if there are five applications in a region and the total application version quota for that region is 1,000, a sys admin might consider creating a lifecycle policy with a limit of 199 versions per application. This way, the total version is always less than 1,000. And the reason why we want to restrict the total version to a smaller number is that the lifecycle policy only applies if Elastic Beanstalk has successfully created an application version. Once you've already reached the quota, the lifecycle policy won't take effect and you must delete some versions manually. Here is what the lifecycle policy looks like in the Elastic Beanstalk console. So it's disabled by default, and once I enable it, we have to choose a lifecycle rule. The first option is called Set the application versions limit by total count. So if I set a value of 100 here, it means that the maximum number of versions that this application can have is only 100. So what will happen then if I upload a new version? In that case, if you still haven't reached the quota in that region, Elastic Beanstalk will create a new version and delete the oldest version. Take note that the new version is not counted towards the max number of versions defined in your lifecycle policy. The next option is called Set the application versions limit by age. And what this does is if I set a maximum age of 180 days here, any versions older than 180 days will be deleted when I upload a new version. Take note that the lifecycle policy only kicks in upon uploading a new version. That's why I said earlier that you should always maintain the total number of versions less than the application versions quota. So you can only choose either of these two. Next is the retention policy. Here you can select whether you'd like to retain the source bundle of your application version in Amazon S3 or not. So if you choose to retain the source bundle in S3, Elastic Beanstalk will still delete the application version from its record, but the source code of the version will still persist in Amazon S3. And if you choose to delete the source bundle in S3, then both the application version in Elastic Beanstalk and the source code in S3 will be deleted.

AWS Elastic Beanstalk CLI
- [Instructor] The EB CLI is Elastic Beanstalk's own set of commands that you can use to manage your application environments from a terminal. Take note that EB CLI is different from AWS CLI. In the Elastic Beanstalk Console, all kinds of information relating to your environment are displayed under your environment. They are all in one place, as you can see here. So if I want to get an overview of my environment's configuration, I can simply click here and all of the configuration settings will be shown to me. If I want to change a particular configuration, say I want to increase my auto-scaling group's capacity to five, I can also do that here. We can also monitor our application on the console. So if you click on monitoring, we'll have access to a graphical view of what is going on in our application. You can view different information like environment health, the target response time, CPU utilization and so on. And most importantly, if you're doing development work, you can also upload and deploy new application versions in the console. So if I click on my environment's name, we can see here the option to upload and deploy an application. All the functionalities that we need to manage our Elastic Beanstalk environments are already available in the console. So why do we even need the EB CLI? Well, the main point of using the EB CLI is to simplify creating, updating, and monitoring environments from your local environment. Imagine you're developing a feature for your application and every time you make code changes, you want to be able to deploy that change to your instances. If you're deploying using the console, you'd have to click a few buttons to deploy the changes, and you'd also have to redeploy the entire project. Using the EB CLI simplifies everything for you. So for example, if you want to deploy a change that you've recently committed to your local repository, you can use the eb-deploy command and under the hood, EB CLI will create an archive of that change that it deploys to your instances. If you need to do iterative deployments, then you can just simply repeat the command, you can press the Arrow Key and hit Enter, or just copy the command and use it every time you need it. This is a much simpler way of deploying updates than using the Console. Aside from using your local repository, you can also use the eb-deploy command to deploy changes from an AWS CodeCommit repository. AWS CodeCommit is a managed source control service that hosts private Git repositories. You can set the repository to CodeCommit using the eb-init command. When you use CodeCommit as your code repository, every time you run the eb-deploy EB CLI will push new commits to CodeCommit repo and use the head revision of your branch to create an archive that it deploys to the EC2 instances in your environment. So this way you can deploy incremental code updates without uploading the entire project every time. Aside from eb-deploy and eb-init, there are other available EB CLI commands that you can use to manage your Elastic Beanstalk environments and we won't be able to discuss all of them in this video. Just to give you an idea, here are the basic commands in the EB CLI, which you can look up in the AWS documentations. So we have the eb-create, eb-status, and here's the eb-deploy that we talked about earlier and a lot more. What's good about the commands is they are not difficult to remember as they do exactly what they are named after. So there you go for EB CLI, you can visit the documentation, see and try the other commands for yourself, and I will see you in the next lecture.

AWS Elastic Beanstalk vs. AWS CodeDeploy
- [Instructor] In this video, we'll look at a side-by-side comparison between AWS Elastic Beanstock and AWS CodeDeploy. Both services offer a variety of deployment actions that you can choose, which often leads to confusion. You can, for example, use Elastic Beanstock to perform an all at once deployment to EC2 instances, which are also possible with CodeDeploy. So which service should you use? To begin with, AWS Elastic Beanstalk is not a native deployment service. It is a platform as a service solution that allows you to deploy applications without having to deal with the operations aspect of it, such as capacity provisioning, load balancing, auto-scaling, and building application help monitoring. For this reason, Elastic Beanstock is more suited to developers that wish to deploy and maintain applications in the AWS Cloud in minutes without having to worry about the underlying infrastructure. Aside from these features, you can also choose from a variety of methods for deploying new versions of your application seamlessly. So, for example, if you want to modify a CSS file on your website, you can change it first in your local environment, create a package of your application, and use EB CLI to deploy it. You don't have to log into your instance or instances to manually do the change every time, but here's the thing with Elastic Beanstalk. The deployment options that it offers only apply to instances inside an Elastic Beanstalk environments. This means that you cannot use it to deploy codes to existing or any instances outside your Elastic beanstalk environments. Elastic Beanstalk has direct integration with AWS CodeCommit, allowing you to deploy incremental code changes using EB CLI. If your source code is in a different remote repository, such as GitHub, it'll need more effort to set it up, probably some help from third party services, in order for the integration with Elastic Beanstalk to work. Now let's look at AWS CodeDeploy. AWS CodeDeploy is a service that is purposely built to automate code deployments. You can use CodeDeploy to deploy code updates to not only EC2 instances, but also to on-premises servers and Lambda functions, as well. It's also possible to perform deployments to instances managed by Elastic Beanstalk. It makes it easier for you to rapidly release new features, helps you avoid downtime during deployments, and handles the complexity of updating your applications. With CodeDeploy, you create a deployment configuration file, which is the AppSpec that channel file to specify how you want your deployments to proceed. Unlike Elastic Beanstalk, CodeDeploy does not automatically handle capacity provisioning, scaling and monitoring. In short, CodeDeploy does not deal with infrastructure configuration and orchestration. It's only focus on deployment automation. CodeDeploy would make more sense to use if you want to automate the deployment of codes to existing on-premises servers or instances.

Amazon API Gateway overview
- [Instructor] Now let's look at Amazon API Gateway. Amazon API Gateway is an API management service that lets you create, publish, maintain, monitor, and secure APIs at any scale. So why would you use API Gateway in the first place? So imagine you have an application that talks to different microservices in your backend. In this case, each of the microservices owns a public endpoint that is used directly by your client application. This poses a flaw from a security standpoint as your microservices are exposed to anyone creating a large attack surface. Also, implementing security features like authorization and rate limiting requests to protect your backend from abuse would be quite challenging as you would need to apply them to each microservices. With API gateway, you can create a single point of entry that maps to your microservices. This provides an obstruction layer that hides all of your internal microservices from your clients making your application more secure. Routing requests to the proper microservices, enforcing authorization and rate limiting are all done on the API Gateway. API Gateway does not require any server management. So creating serverless architectures is a popular use case for it. Most of the time you'll see API Gateway coupled with AWS Lambda in serverless applications. Say you created an API, hosted it in Lambda function, and now you want other people to use it over the internet. How would you do that? You cannot directly attach a domain name to Lambda function. You can invoke Lambda function by its ARN, but that requires letting people access your AWS account. So here, API Gateway comes into play. With API Gateway you can expose your Lambda function through an API endpoint that can be distributed publicly. This way people can invoke your Lambda function without them having access to your accounts. You just have to give API Gateway enough permissions to invoke the Lambda function. You can also use API Gateway to modernize legacy applications. If you have a legacy application that you want to expose, say a SOAP web service that processes XML data, you can have clients send formatted in JSON and then have Amazon API Gateway transform that JSO data to XML using mapping templates. You can then use API Gateway to trigger rest service that posts the XML as a payload to the SOAP web service. When you create an API, you get to choose between a REST API, HTTP API, and a WebSocket API endpoints. For most cases, the REST API is what you'll want to use. This gives you complete control over the request and response, along with other API management capabilities like caching, creating API keys and usage plans REST API also offers integration with other AWS services. HTTP API is cheap and is designed for simple low latency applications, but it lacks other API Gateway features and ask for the WebSocket API you may want to use this for applications requiring persistent connections such as real-time chat applications. It is also important to remember in the exam that API Gateway does not support unencrypted endpoints. When you deploy an API, by default, you get unencrypted endpoints. For HTTP and REST APIs you get an HTTPS endpoint. And for WebSocket APIs, you get a WSS endpoint, which is similar to how an HTTP endpoint is encrypted with DLS. When you create a REST API, you get to choose an endpoint type. When you use the Edge optimized API endpoint, your API is deployed to the specified region while using a CloudFront distribution to facilitate client access typically from across AWS regions, API requests are routed to the nearest CloudFront point of presence. Regional API endpoint, on the other hand, routes an API request to the region specific API Gateway without going through any CloudFront distribution. A private API endpoint allows a client to securely access private API resources inside a VPC. Private APIs are isolated from the public internet and they can only be accessed using VPC endpoints for API Gateway that have been granted access.

Amazon API Gateway usage plans
- [Instructor] Now let's talk about usage plans. Whether you're developing at work or building applications for fun, it is highly likely that you've integrated an API service or two to your projects. I mean, why not? They're easy to use, there is literally an API for every use case, and you can use them out of the box to enrich your application with features that you would have otherwise developed yourself. We're surrounded by APIs. In fact, if you think about it, AWS is just a large collection of APIs that are managed meticulously to create a service. Now, there is something common for every published API, that is their throttling limit and quota. Request limits are staple for APIs. So even if you're using a free plan, basic plan, premium plan, or whatever subscription plan, you are still bound by a certain degree of limits. So usually how it works is lower tier plans, like a free subscription, are given lower limits compared to a paid subscription. If you have an idea for an API that you'd like to expose and sell, perhaps you've built an EA service or a tool that can help out other businesses, then you might want to look at the usage plans. Usage plans is an API Gateway feature that can help you control different levels of access to an API. So for example, maybe you want to create three subscription plans for your API, a basic plan, a premium plan, and an ultra plan. And you want to enforce different throttling limits and quotas for each of them. You can do so by creating three usage plans. Creating a usage plan in API Gateway can be done with just a few clicks away. Let me walk you through it. So first you're going to give it a name. Let's name this usage plan as basic. Next, we'll give it a throttling limit. So we have two parameters that we can configure. The first option is the requests per second, which basically restricts the number of requests that can hit your API endpoint in a second. Let's give it a value of 10. The second option is burst, and this refers to the maximum concurrent requests that API Gateway can fulfill at any moment without throwing a too many requests error. For this one, let's also put a value of 10. I'm just making up these values as I go along. The value for rate and burst is up to you and will depend on your application's requirement and the capacity of your backend server. You can also disable throttling, but we don't want this in a production setting, so maybe you can do this in your test environment. Finally, we can set a quota. This limits the number of requests that can be made per day, week, or month. Let's give it a volume of 100 requests per day. Then click the next button. After that, we associate this usage plan to an API stage. First, we'll choose an API and select its stage. Click on next. And also we must associate an API key to this usage plan. API keys serve as a unique identifier for your application. And with API keys, we can track API calls associated with a specific usage plan for quota, limit and billing purposes. Once done, click on next. And there you have it, we have created a usage plan for the basic subscription plan. Now if you notice there's a marketplace section here, and what this lets you do is it allows you to offer your API as a product to the AWS SaaS marketplace so other users or companies can actually see your product and subscribe to it. Your API subscribers are billed based on the number of requests made to the usage plan. So this is really a great way to monetize your API.
